THE RED PAGE.
Brewer quotes Walpole's story how the con-
fesssor of a French king reproved him for conjugal
infidelity, and the king abruptly changed the
subject by asking what di h he liked best. The
confessor thought, an.l an aw red " Partridge ”;
whareupoa the king ordered him to be served
with partridge every day. tdl he loathed the sight
of it. Then the kprg \i.ited the confessor, and
enquired if he hid IK a welrserved. “ Mais oui,”
replied the coufeasi r, “perdrix, toujours perdrix ! ”
“ Precisely,'’return.*! the fickle monarch, “and
ray wife is all very well ; but not ‘ perdrix, tou-
jours perdrix,’
In Dyson’s case there is variety enough to make
his book excellent reading even at a sitting;
though half-a-dozen sittings help the flavor.
Dyson’s literary style is peculiarly terse and lucid ;
his effects are all clearly seen and cleverly
worded ; and he finishes his stories cleanly and
well, as a good prose workman should. He is
particularly a miners’ story-teller, as in “Rhymes
from the Mines ” he was the miners’ verse-writer.
To the men' and women and scenes of the early
Victorian reeling days he recurs continually : and
here his knowledge is ample and his touch sure. The
lights on Chinese character in “ A Golden Shanty ”
and “ Mr. and Mrs. Sin Fat ” are hardly less vivid ;
and these stories are bedecked by a somewhat
sardonic humor which makes them among the
most interesting the author has penned. Of
Dyson’s skill in climaxing grim tragedy “The
Trucker’s Dream ” and “After the Accident ’’are
leading examples : the tales could hardly be con-
structed better. Indeed, only a certain hardness
of touch, a certain lack of vivifying emotion, pre-
vents Dyson from excelling almost any competi-
tor in the art of dramatic- story-telling.
“ Below and On Top ” is a substantial addition
to Australian fiction : and The Bulletin heartily
recommends it to Australian readers. Those who
have read some of the tales before will he glad to
renew acquaintance; and those to whom they
come freshly cannot fail to conceive admiration for
Dyson’s industrious talent.

“JackSliay” writes on Australian slang -—Some
of the idioms which 1 quote are probably foreign;
of some the local use is restricted; hut all are
currently used.
The horse is a power in Australia, and a few
choice expressions spring from horses. Horse-
shoeing is a word occasionally heard to describe
abuse, obscenity, profanity. A bit off, an off-sider,
off color, out of collar, collar-proud, off his oats—
these are familiar.
In draught work the laziest or worst horse is on
the off side, under the whip; thus it is an inferior
man or thing that is a bit off, off color, or an off'-
sider. Out of collar aptly describes out of work ;
off his oats, sickness or a state of off-islmess.
Collar-proud is applied to horses put to work after
a spell. They jerk at the collar, pull indifferently,
and want the whip to keep them “up in the
collar. ” Working dead-horse is working to pay off
a back debt; or, as a teamster would say, “This
trip has got to pay for a horse that I lost last trip;
£2O to make up to pay for the new horse.”
From shooting comes that murderous phrase,
“ I’ll blow your light out ” ; and a sure win is a
dead bird. The first is no doubt from the gallery
pistol-trick of blowing a candle out; the second
from pigeon-shooting. A horse or man just beaten
is said to have “ just blew out ” ; and to have lost
money on a man or horse oi any gamble is laconic-
ally expressed by " I bit wed ” my money.
Waster is a timbei -getters’ phrase for worthless
logs or flitches. This is applied to men and things
promiscuously.
Snow-dropping is thieves’ argot for clothes-line
robbing. Duck-shoving is poultry-thieving. The
practice which Morris (Austral-English Diet.)
calls duck-shoving (i.e., cabmen forestalling each
other for a fare) is known among Sydney cabmen as
touching off. A sweet idiom is nicking the -peter---
the stealing of tills in small shops. There may be
a connection with Peter’s Pence.
Among cabmen, touting around the streets for
chance fares—a forbidden practice—is known as
gagging. Men with licenses hut no vehicles, who
“do a mind ” for a regular driver, live by plurri-
ming, and are known as plummers.
To get narked is to lose your temper ; also ex-
pressed by getting dead wet or going to market. An
informer or n,ar-plot is a nark or a Jonah or a
Noah's Ark.
The Yiddish element is very strong in Austra-
lian slang. Kosher is good; shiker drunk;
stoomeg broke; and a stoomer or stumer is a man
without money. Mozzle is luck, derived from
wurzschimozzle. Good mozzle= good luck ; kronk
mozzle, bad luck ; and a shiksa is a girl.
Speaking of things in general: if things are
going fairly with a man he is throwing deuces ; but
if bad, throwing sevens. If any one dies it is
known as throwing the seven or throwing his hand
out.. Both these phrases originate in the hazard
school.
An incessant talker is a skiter or a fluter, and a
request to him to pass the flute or the kip is to
allow someone else to “do a pitch.” Flute ex-
plains itself; and the kip is the piece of wood
used in “two-up,” otherwise pitch and toss.
Xjoint may be applied to anything, but usually
refers to a gambling place or machine. Its origin
is the “break joint,” a mechanical contrivance
attached to racecourse roulette tables to enable
the o] erator to cheat the bettors. Umpcka, re-
versed, spells “chump,” a inug or flat'. This is
the old gy] sy method : as delo namow —old woman ;
oekerla - -tobacco. Chyack is more properly chy-
ike, the cockney pronunciation of “cheek”—im-
pudent badinage.
Tmlinj, winch Morris gives as literally “to
follow close behind the tail ” of sheep or cattle,
is a corruption of ‘ tallying to count, which
was a nightly custom with many shepherds in the
days of no fences and open pasturage. A tally-
clerk is a common term ; and tallying, on a good
many stations, is spoken tailing.
The cockney rhyming slang is popular in Aus-
tralia, and the lion comiques ami lydies of the
variety stage are helping to make the hold
stronger. This slang is built thus—
Collar Arty Rolla. Old man— Pot and pan.
Rvea—Mince pies. Drink Tiddly-wink.
Mrs.— Cheese and kisses. Tie -Lamb’s-fnj.
Waistcoat— Charlie Prescott. Head-Lump o’ lead.
Street organ— Joe Morgan. Feet—Plates o' meat.
Lie—Fairy-tale.
The Australian mostly abbreviates by using
only the first of the compound words. Thus, a
drink is a tidley. A fairy-tale has become a post-
and-rail or “snake-yarn.”
Drum— derived from the kettle-drums (evening
parties) of the days of the Georges—was a high-
class word, but it fell. The cockney turned it
into rub-a-dum-dum ; the Australian now calls the
same thing a rubadcy.
The use of Christian names in this form of slang
seems to have originated the Australian Billy-Can;
Jack-Shay, a quart pot for making tea, with which
word shay almost rhymes ; and Jimmy- Woodser, a
solitary drinker. Rye-buck (all right) is no doubt
an abbreviation of “all right, my buck.” Good-
iron, synonymous with “Bravo! ”is unexplainable.
Xrook or kronk is bad ; while sweet, roujig, and
not too stintin’, are good. To these are prefixed
the adjectives motherless and dead, thus dead
motherless broke (or sweet, or kronk).
Familiar similes are as yet unnoticed. “As
mean as Hungry Tyson” (used by people who
know nothing of Tyson). “ Like a bandicoot on a
burnt ridge ’’ was, I think, applied by Parkes to
a lonely and forlorn opponent. “ Like a possum
up a gum tree ” is not bad to express quickness or
cleverness in doing anything.
The only classical idioms I have found (there
must he more, I think) are rotter, he., an adept in
learning anything ; and panerti agere, Sydney Uni.
slang for “ doing a loaf.”
A watch and chain'is a block and tackle; if of
gold a red lot.
The most curious inversion I come across is the
word Fat. Anyone would naturally think a Pat
was an Irishman, but a Pat is a Chinaman
(? abbreviation of paddy, Chinese-used for rice).
A spieler or yuy-a-whack is a professional sharper
or kronk bookmaker ; while a gee is their outside
confederate, who “gees up ” the mugs for them.
A bludger is about the lowest grade of human
thing, and is a brothel bully. A man-about-town
amounts to the same thing. A battler is the
feminine.

C.B. remarks oirthc other side The secret is
out. To speak a priori, and settle questions in
advance —envious prestige !—all you want is phy-
siology. “No man ever creates anything in
letters when over 50 ; no man creates anything
which is worth creating (gives forth a fresh, strong,
original impulse) after 40. To expect a poetic
masterpiece after 25 years hard scholastic labor is
to fly in the face of physiology.” Ipse Physiologus
dixi. Punctum.
Perhaps the “ physiologist ” is, after all, “flying
in the face ”of fact. Tradition, at least, imaged
Homer as “the old man in rocky Chios,” and
Aeschylus called song “a wayside gift from
heaven to men whose prime is past.” History
would have us believe that Aeschylus wrote the
Prometheus and Oresteia when over 60, Sophocles
the (Edipus Coloneus when over 80, Euripides
his most passionate and romantic tragedies—a
striking contrast to the realistic problem-dramas
of his youth—On the environs of 70. Milton wrote
no verse beyond scattered sonnets for 20 years, sat
him down at 51 and dictated “Paradise Lost."
Tennyson did finer lyrical work at GO and upwards
than m his youth ; Swinburne’s “ Tale of Haleb,”
written at 58, is as fresh and poignant as the
“Poems and Ballads” of his twenties. Only a
few examples these.
“ A poet is not there to compile.” Perhaps
not; but if you mean to say that there is no
memory for inspiration, no power of developing
and amplifying the confused riches thereof—
generally just the raw matter of art—that the
poet cannot patiently and systematically exercise
his voluntary consciousness on the sudden
“surges” and flashes of his subconscious mind,
just as other people can in other departments,
pray how came Goethe to write “Faust,” Milton
to write “ The Paradises,” Dante to write “ The
Commedia,” Hugo “ The Legende des Steeles,”
&c., &c., &c. ? Nor did all the lyrical poets, from
whom the “ surge ” theory is a hasty generalisa-
tion, get them delivered of their songs in a frenzy.
Technique ?—technique can neither be learned nor
practised apart from the matter it is suited to :
there is no abstract technique.
The fact is, genius generally “flies in the face ”
°f everything > of physiology, by living harder and
wilder, and yet longer than cautious people ; also
m the face of critics. For another instance,
haven’t they been telling us that to write one’s
tongue well and preserve originality one should
know only that one tongue. Proofs : Milton,
skill’d and eloquent in six languages ; Swinburne,
rhythmical in five.
Now what is behind all- this “physiology”?
Perhaps this : poetry is the product of green and
salad days, prose the result of thought. There-
fore, as thought is to emotion —pardon, as grey
matter is to white . . . or is it as white is to grey?
—of course, I don’t know, but no odds—so is prose
to verse. “Verse is the weak feminine of prose,”
and the man is the woman’s head. As for the
prose which is the sturdy—not to say “stodgy
male of the verse of Aeschylus, I )ante, Marlowe,
Milton ; if I were to enquire as to its whereabouts
who knows whether the “physiologist ” mightn’t
turn a somersault, while still remaining true to
the good old “ highpriori, ” and tell me that it was
laid up, along with physiological certainty, scien-
tific truth, final criticism, and Mallarine’s CEuvre ,
in Plato’s transcendental heaven ?

This is reprinted from Lond. Athenceum for the sake of
the Australian application—
“ The Last Poems of Susan K. Phillips” (Grant
Richards) are worth notice as being typical of a phase of
modern book production. They do not contain a line
which could be called purely poetic, and yet they will
probably have a considerable circulation. We have to
recognise and accept the fact that the bulk of the popu-
lation of these islands can get on very well without
poetry--that it is satisfied with very much less. A large
proportion of it regards as poetry everything which is
not prose. The average man probably never reads any-
thing in the shape of verse. In general the feminine
taste does not rise above Miss Procter ; usually it falls, in
a literary s.ense, much lower. Widespread as is the spirit
of culture in England, it has not, in the matter of poetry,
sunk very deep. The suburbs and the provinces feel no
need for the higher poetic achievement, and they are
quite well pleased when, instead of pure poetry, they get
fluency and prettiness. It is not true of the world of
verse that “ we needs must love the highest when we see
it.” The highest is offered to the big reading public, and
in most cases it, wins no regard. Something much in-
ferior will suffice; Can we wonder that, such people are
written for? Nothing could be more natural. There is
a demand for a certain article, and that article is duly
forthcoming.
“Take the following, from S. K. Phillips's ‘ Last Poems,’
and note how pathetically it falls short of the outcome of
Miss Rossetti in the same vein
Do you forget it, love, the old sweet phrase?
Do you forget the clasp of hand in hand,
The gesture we two only understand?
The magic lying in the dear old ways?
The glow of passion in its morning days
Has softened to the evening, where we stand
Watching the wavelets break upon the sand,
In the soft reflex of the sunny rays.
Dear, the sweet roses that we gathered then
Are faded quite, and the pale twilight sees
Nig4it-blowing lilies glimmer, each a gem,
Beneath the low boughs of the yellowing trees ;
But, oh ! their scent is rich and subtle yet;
I prize the fragrance, dear. Do you forget?
“ We have no doubt there are thousands of women, old
and young, in England whom the above lines have the power
to thrill. They are not real poetry, but they are the
poetry of the parlor—the only poetry the parlor really
cares for. They embody, clearly and harmoniously, a
familiar sentiment. Why should not such things be
penned? Every class has its intellectual needs, such as
they are. It is not disgraceful, or even discreditable, to
satisfy those cravings so long as they are wholesome.
After all, it is something to have given articulate voice to
the feelings and the thoughts of millions. The result
may not be literature, and yet may lead to better things.
The time may come when the British public—even the
magazine-reading public —will insist upon having poetry,
and nothing less. Meanwhile, it is no one’s business to
sneer at the earnest and amiable souls who furnish the
pabulum required of them. They do their best, and we
ought to be thankful they exist among us, willing and
ready to produce what is asked for. S. K. Phillips said
the old things over again, but she said them better than
many a poetaster has done. To the average reader,
indeed, her volumes can positively be recommended ;
and assuredly they will do no harm to him or to any one.”
Lance T. : Rn definitions, etc., of poetry. Reading G.
H. L-wes’s “ Principles of Success in Literature,” I came
across the following : —“ Poetry is the beautiful phasis of
a religious idea.” “ Poetry is the metrical utm ranee of
emotion.’’ Solger’s “vEsihetik” is quoted : “ Everything
must be action or emotion. Hence a purely descriptive
poetry is impossible, if it confine itself to its subject,
without action or emotion ; on which point Lessing
has some admirable remarks in the Laoeoon. In
Horner, you never see a particular subject merely
described, hut the description is always contained in
some action. So tire clothing of Agamemnon, or the
shield of Achilles, where the subjects represented appear
themselves as living and in action " ; and (G. H. Lewes)
the reason of this is given by Hegel when he says, “ Not
things and their practical existence, but pictures and
imaginative symbols are the materials of poetry.’ Quite
akin, this defining of Hegel's, with Class VI. and Quinn’s
printed “Camp within the West.”
Lewes goes on It is this emotive principle which
creates all the ornaments, as they are styled, such as per-
sonification, metaphor, and trope; for nothing being
announced as a fact, but everything as seen through the
passionate medium of the speaker's soul, it necessitates a
figurative impassioned language. [ln this connection
Ogilvie’s “ Land of Dumb Despair” and Victor Daley’s
lines—
But where are all the ancient singing-spheres
That, burst through chaos like the summer’s breath— etc.]
Lewes again : Professor Wilson’s definition of poetry—
“ man’s thoughts tinged by his_ feelings ” —becomes ad-
missible, except that it does not cleniarcate it from novels
or oratory (or even the bellowingsof the mud-brained Leg.
Councillor). “Ornaments” may be used by imitators
and verse-makers ; but they are always foreign, repulsive,
and cumbersome, simply because they are ornaments,
ostentatiously worn for their glitter, and not . real
associations clinging round the central feeling. But in the
true poet, imagination acting on the feeling, or the feeling
acting on the imagination, condenses and fuses a whole
series of ideas into one nexus of expression ; such is per-
sonification, one of the most poetical of figures, hut which,
when not springing from the ground of real passion, be-
comes an impertinence in the imitator or scholar-poet,
and warms the mind no more than prose.
To think much of Poetry and all it signifies—artisti-
cally, philosophically, and historically—calls up imagi-
nary swaddlings of the fevered brow in ice-cold cloths,
and much tired endeavor to assimilate Watts-Dun ton’s
labyrinthine article in Encyclopaedia Britannica.
After all, tot homines qaot sentential ; closing with the
Shelleyan paean—somewhat paradoxical—of the true
bards : “ Poets are the hierophants of an unapprehended
inspiration ; the mirrors of the id-antic shadows w v u
futurity casts upon the present; the words whTh exnr' 1 *
what they understand not; the trumpets u t. , n e . x P re M
battle and feel not what they inspire * - to
unacknowledged legislators of the world.” W are tbe

Blood PoiSoo-
This is a most loathsome affliction. Blood poison is a disease very insidious in its first stage.
Its first appearance is not attended by pain, as some suppose, but when once contracted it is a very
troublesome disease to eradicate from the system. Paint a picture as dark as the darkest night, en-
compassed with all the horrors imaginable—with shrieks, groans, cries and utter despair, and then
you’ll have a picture of blood poison in its last stages. It is then that the bones begin to decay, and
chunks of flesh drop from your body. Your eyes become weak and watery, your throat and tongue
are affected, and life becomes indeed a burden. If you are so unfortunate as to be afflicted with blood
poison, we would implore you to take treatment without one moment’s delay. Don’t wait until you
a™ a complete physical wreck. We have Remedies which effect a POSITIVE CURE of this terrible
affliction. We treat and cure blood poison at any stage, but the sooner treatment is commenced the
better. If your affliction has only reached the first or second stages your system can be cleansed
speedily and permanently. A lon tier course of treatment is necessary to effect a cure of blood poison-
'ng when in the last stages. We GUARANTEE TO CURE, ABSOLUTELY and PERMA-
NEN L'LY, every case of blood poisoning undertaken. When we are not absolutely certain of accom-
plishing a cure, we say so frankly and decline to take the ease. If you live out of the city and cannot
call personally, write to us. Remedies can be prepared and sent you, and you can be cured at home.

NerVoCis Debility, Loss of Vitality.
WE CAN CURE NERVOUS DEBILITY, in any stage, permanently at the Office, or by a
course of home treatment especially prepared to suit each case. Nervous Debility is not a chance or
natural affliction. In its early stages it is not attended by a great amount of discomfort. It is an
affliction that begins as a mole-hill and ends as a mountain. At first, the only symptom noticeable
is a feeling of unrest, attended by gloomy forebodings of the future. Your sleep at night is not
peaceful. By and bye you have an aversion for society—you are troubled with stiipicfness, bashfulness
and loss of energy. You soon become ambitionless, and your purpose in life is aimless. You find
little enjoyment in life, and just at this stage vour thoughts run more or less to suicide. The cemetery
looks beautiful, and you think of the peaceful slumber of those beneath the sod. You grow in elan-
choly. Lif e becomes visionless and tiresome. You become troubled with an aching back, and if you
indulge in excesses there is likely to develop a breaking-out of pimples. It is the broad and certain
road to PREMATURE DECAY. Stop for a moment. Call together your better nature. Don’t
despair. Think the matter over coolly and calmly. You need treatment for Nervous Debility.
Consult us, either at our office or by mail, and learn the extent of your affliction. We can cure you
speedily and permanently. We can regain your lost hopes—bring back the lustre to your eyes, and
make of you a perfect man—with an object in life—ambitious—successful. Don’t delay. Immediate
action is far the best.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Fixed Up.”
"I went to ehapple, Sunday,
Not as I often go.
(Em’s friend said she was going.)
The everlastings grow
All round the little sandhill
•lust back of Thompson’s lot.
The air was sweet with wattle
And blue ‘forget-me-not.’
I don t rec’lect the sermon;
I sat back near the door
And kept a-waiting for her
Eor half-an-hour or more.
She came in late and flurried,
Her dress was something blue
That seemed to love her figure,
And Emily came, too.
I don’t reo’lect the sermon ;
I didn’t seem to hear ;
Something her friend had told me
Kept buzzing in my ear.
I waited, waited, waited—
I thought he’d never stop !
She had a black straw hat on
With little bows on top.
Em. giggled when I met them
Outside the chappie door,
Them and a city feller
As works in Carey’s store.
Of course I walked with Emma j
Of course she walked with him.
And Em. stopped at the corner,
Sat on a fallen limb,
And fiddled with her hymn-book,
Then, looking at me straight,
Says, ‘Jim, I’ve been a-thinking
That— p’r’aps— we needn’t wait
Until you reaps twelve bushels.
There mayn’t be any more
Of them old-fashioned seasons.
Suppose we—make it four ?' *•
NIL.
Bandaroo.
There was dearth and drought at dusty Bandaroo,
There was seldom drinking bout at Bandaroo,
There were crows whose joy was awful,
Jubilating fat and cawful,
As their wont is, in a black and dirty rout, at
Bandaroo.
And the creek was crammed with dying and with
dead,
And the sky was ever cloudless overhead,
And the flocks were daily thinning,
And the ground a-gape was grinning,
And the boss’s face was marble—while the boss’s
heart was lead.
And we daily scanned the sky at Bandaroo,
And we watched the pot-holes dry at Bandaroo,
And the only prayer we’d utter
Was a kind of curseful mutter
When a casual cloud would mock us and go by at
Bandaroo.
Of a sudden with a changing wind there came
A blackened sky that split in flash o’ flame,
Roar of rain in deaf’ning chorus,
Giant cannon pealing o’er us,
As though Heav’n had gone a-gunning in a joyous
thunder game.
And we stood within the shelter of the door,
Gazing out upon the revel and the roar,
And the boss had brought a bottle
So that every thirsty throttle
Should be duly irrigated as a sign the drought was
o’er.
Now a deed of shame was done by Jimbour Joe
For he hurried where the bucket caught the flow,
And he tried to mix his liquor—
But I guess the boss was quicker—
He had scruffed him in a jiffy, for the boss was
never slow;
With a growl, the boss had gripped him, full of
gall;
He had pinned him, with his pint-pot, to the
wall:
“ By the holy Peter Piper !
Drink it neat , you sniveling swiper ;
Would you spoil your grog with, ivater — when the
ground should have it all /”
And ever since that day at Bandaroo,
When the drought has cleared away from Ban-
daroo,
It’s the custom on the station
That, in sign of jubilation,
Whisky neat’s the only tipple that’s on tap at
Bandaroo.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Mrs. M'Caulay’s husband fell off the gang-
way of one of M’ll wraith, M'Eaeharn’s ships and
was drowned in Sydney harbor. The widow sued
the co. for damages with varying results to date :
First trial, nonsuit; second trial, verdict for de-
fendants ; third, leave granted for a new trial ;
fourth, new trial and verdict for plaintiff with
,£,‘500 damages, with prospect of the series being
continued.

With the close of the year 1898, the Australian Mutual Provident Society will complete FIFTY YEARS of its
existence. Members are invited to make this a RECORD YEAR by taking out additional policies themselves, and by
inducing their friends who are not already assured to become members. ALL POLICIES ISSUED before
the end of the year will share in the division of profits to be made as at 31st December next. Amount of Cash Surplus
divided amongst the members during the 49 years ending 1897 was £7,726,170. The Cash Bonus distributed amongst
the Policyholders for the single year 1897 was £445,235. EVERY YEAR A BONUS YEAR.

HISTORY of
YANKEE DOODLE.
SEE
DIXSOFS TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
ID AiD^rica.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through Hew Jersey on Hew York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father L with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head fifing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
grandsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight. The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
arose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Doodle was heard as never was beard
before or since.
If) Australia.
YANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to produce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD IN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Wales alone now reach
Tons per Day.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TONGUE. Every smoker should
TRY FOR HIMSELF.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 17, 1898.
The Richest Land on Earth.
1. That inasmuch as New South Wales is, in proportion
to population, according to the estimates of its official
Statistician, the richest country in the world, and as it also
has no burden of war debt, and no heavy (? naval) or mili-
tary expenditure, it should pay all the ordinary expenses of
government out of taxation, as poorer countries do. 2.
That the land revenue, being a species of special endow-
ment arising from the peculiar circumstances of a new
country, should be entirely set apart for the construction
of pubiic works. 3. That borrowing, except for the pur-
pose of renewal of old loans, should be entirely abolished.
4. That if the Legislature is not prepared to abolish
borrowing, loan moneys should be used only for the con-
struction of railways, telegraphs, telephones, and such
water and sewerage works as will yield direct revenue to*
the Treasury, but all works not productive of direct re-
venue to the Treasury, such as gaols, lockups, bridges,
roads, schools, crockery for Jenolan accommodation-
house, fortifications, breakwaters, dredging works, river
improvements, repairs to public buildings, &c., &c., shall
be chargeable to revenue? 5. That there be established
a separate public works fund, into which all land revenue
shall be paid, and to which all works shall be charged, it
having been found that the principle of mixing up works
and ordinary expenditure leads to confusion. The Trea-
surer who pays most of his expenses out of revenue is
ejected for being an extravagant person who spends too
much, while the one who puts most of his works expendi-
ture into loans poses as an economist who keeps down the
public outgoings. 6. That the foregoing resolution be
communicated by address to his Excellency the Governor.
—Motion by Chanter, M.L.A., in N.S.W. Assembly.
The last time the State Statistician made an
official estimate of the private wealth of New
South Wales he set it down at the enormous
average of £336 per head. This was in
1893, but in lately reiterating the estimate
(“Wealth and Progress ofNew South Wales”
for 1896-’97, page 905) he states that a new
valuation is in progress, and that “ the
popular impression of the extent of the
decline in property - values is not fully
borne out by the returns so far received.”
Therefore it would seem that there has
been a decline, but not a large one,
and that, though the average private wealth
of New South Wales is now somewhere below
the glorious £336 per inhabitant of 1893,
still it isn't far below it. According to the
State Statistician, New South Wales stands
in this respect at the very top of the list of
the Seven Provinces. The last time the
Victorian Statistician made an official esti-
mate of the wealth of his province he put it
at the magnificent figure of £4lO per in-
habitant, but the New South Wales official
saws £lOO off this, and estimates the wealth
of the rival province at £304 per head (Seven
Colonies of Australasia for 1894, page 290).
Presumably it was the duty of each statisti-
cal authority to show that his own province
was the richer of the two, but for the pur-
poses of this article the figures of the New
South Wales official may pass muster.
Barring his disagreement with the Melbourne
gentleman in the same line of business they
look plausible enough. According to New
South Wales’ official authority, therefore,
the cornstalk people are the most gloriously
wealthy community on earth, for the miser-
able places that lie outside Australasia are
simply nowhere by comparison. Cogiilan
quotes a few of them on page 288 of his
“ Seven Colonies ” for 1894 : Britain, £246
per head, or a good 25 per cent, below New
South Wales ; France, £235 ; Belgium,
£226 ; United States, £205 ; and the rest
nowhere.
* * * *
According to the same authority (“ Wealth
and Progress of New South Wales for 1896-
’97, page 908) the annual earnings of the
people of New South Wales are £59,000,000,
which, in proportion to population, is the
biggest private income in the world. He
also claims that New South Wales has
the biggest mass of bank deposits in
Australasia, and according to his figures
it has the biggest mass of deposits, in pro-
portion to population, among all the countries
of this globe, Victoria and Queensland
excepted. Also it appears that it has the
largest stock of gold on this earth in pro-
portion to population—the accumulations of
Victoria and Hawaii (!) only excepted. It
is also alleged that it has the biggest mass
of savings-bank deposits in Australasia, and
that, in proportion to population, it has the
biggest trade in the world—Westralia, South
Australia, and Belgium only excepted. (This
is taking no account of mere ports of call,
whose trade isn’t really their own.) In
fact, by its own official statistics,
New South Wales is the richest and most
glorious place of its population on this globe.
And on top of all its other good fortune it
has had no war up to date ; no war debt ; no
war damage to make good ; no sudden
military levies to pay and feed ; no bom-
barded towns to rebuild ; very little military
or naval expenditure of any kind ; and it has
the biggest land revenue in proportion to
population, not only in Australasia, but in
the world. This magnificent endowment,
which comes in annually without any neces-
sity on the part of its recipients to toil or
spin, is nearly four times as much in propor-
tion to population as the entire revenue of
India ; more than twice that of Java ; more
than that of Russia, Sweden, Norway, Rou-
mania, the United States or Brazil, and very
close up to the entire revenue of Denmark
and Egypt.
* * * *
And The Bulletin respectfully asks why
this land of magnificent wealth and magnifi-
cent brag can’t pay its own way instead of
always holding out the abject hat for loans
fromlands less favored than itself. Ithaslong
since given up the pretence of borrowing only
for permanent and reproductive works. It
now owes, according to Coghlan, no less than
£10,199,000 for which it has no reproductive
assets to show. In proportion to population
this unproductive liability more than equals
the total debt of either Chili, the German
empire, Japan, Mexico, Norway, Russia,
Sweden, Switzerland, or the U.S. All these
countries have struggled through bitter wars,
and all save Switzerland have maintained big
defence forces to protect them against the
dangerous neighbors at their doors, and none
of them had the magnificent, pension which
New South Wales enjoys in its land re-
ceipts. Yet they contrive, despite their
poverty, to build and maintain out of revenue
the public works which the listless, shirking
Australian borrows the money for—his
reason being that he owns the richest country
in the world, and belongs to the richest com-
munity in the world, and is too poor even to
pay for repairs to his own public buildings.
It is another of the particular boasts of the
Cornstalk that he belongs to “the most
lightly-taxed country in the world,” and
even big daily newspapers of repute and
alleged intelligence draw attention to this as
if it was a subject for special pride that New
South Wales would rather borrow the money
for its simplest public requirements, and
leave the bill to posterity, than tax itself
sufficiently to pay its way. If New South
Wales was one of the most heavily-taxed
countries in the world, and could show at
the same time that the tax revenue was
wisely spent in building railways, roads,
bridges, telegraphs, irrigation works, and
other undertakings calculated to enrich the
land, it might be something worth bragging
about.
* * * *
It is also a special boast of the present
New South Wales Ministry that it has re-
duced the expenditure oat f i eventte lower
than it has been for many years past. In
1897 the Reid Government spent out of
revenue £7 os. lid. per inhabitant against
£8 16s. per inhabitant expended by Parkes
and Dibbs in 1891. The largest part of the
saving consists in the fact that the amount
spent out of revenue upon public works was
cut down from £1,487,000 in 1891 to
£855,000 in 1897, and a lot of the roads
and bridges and dredges and fortifica-
tions and military ammunition and salai'ies
which used to be charged to revenue are now
charged to loans instead. That kind of saving
is a disgrace to any honest and solvent com-
munity. But in New South Wales, as well
as in all the other provinces (Maoriland only
excepted), owing to the absence of a Public
Works Fund, and the lumping together of
the money spent on works and that laid out
on salaries and miscellaneous expenditure,
every Treasurer is judged by the total
amount of his outgoings, and by that only.
If he is honest, and pays for the guns and
ammunition and the repairs to old roads,
bridges, wharves, buildings, dredges, &c.,
and the crockery for Jenolan accommoda-
tion-house, and the other unproductive
items, out of revenue, he is denounced as a
grossly extravagant and ruinous person who
ran the public expenditure up to 10| millions,
and the country fires him out. And if he is
brazen and unscrupulous enough to allege
that these things are “permanent and re-
productive works,” and put them into loan
charges, he is reverenced as the brilliant
economist who cut down expenditure to
9-Jf millions. Therefore, most Treasurers
take the easy and dishonest road to
popularity and office. Up to the
end of 1893 New South Wales had
misapplied 15 per cent, of its loans to save
the tired and shiftless population of the
richest country on earth the trouble of paying
its own expenses. Since then, under the
Reid regime, 32 per cent, of the new loans
have been similarly misapplied. Within the
last year or two the most affluent and most
lightly-taxed country in the world (we have
Premier Retd’s word for the fact that it is
the most lightly-taxed country in the world)
has got things down so low that even the
cartridges for its little army are largely paid
for with borrowed funds. Also, it has got
things down so much lower even than that
depth that it practically raises money now at
3 per cent, to put into revenue. Large sums
are borrowed to improve State lands and
make them saleable or lettable. And when,
say, £50,000 has been thus spent, and the
land consequently realises £50,000 more than
it would otherwise have done, the extra
£50,000 goes into the year’s receipts,
and the sum borrowed remains in its old
borrowed condition. Apart from the
appearance of the thing, it would have been
just the same if the land had been sold for
what it would fetch in its original unim-
proved state, and the borrowed £50,000 had
been put direct into the Treasury.
* * * *
The motion of Chanter, M.L.A., asks for
very little after all, though the tired, listless,
habitual borrowers of this great loan land
greeted its appearance on the notice-paper
with a shriek of derision. He asked, in the
first place, that New South Wales—accord-
ing to its own official statistics the richest
land on earth—should pay its ordinary
expenses of government out of revenue.
Such poor common people as the Chilians,
Mexicans, Yankees, Germans, Swedes, Nor-
wegians, Swiss, and British contrive to do
that, though, according to the State statistics
of New South Wales and many other authori-
ties, they are incalculably poorer than we
are. Most of them even contrive to main-
tain good-sized armies and navies, and to
carry the burden of heavy war-debts, out of
their receipts from taxation; and Britain,
the U.S., and Mexico (since the beginning
of the Diaz regime) also manage somehow
to pay off part of their old debts from the same
source; and Russia, Sweden, Norway,
Prussia, and others have even built some of
their State railways out of the proceeds of
taxation. Chanter’s motion didn't go so far
as to ask the people of rich New South Wales
to show the same grit and self-reliance as
these poor miserable foreigners. He merely
asked them to tax themselves sufficiently to
pay the ordinary cost of government, and to
let the State land revenue—that huge endow-
ment which falls on the country like the gentle
coin from heaven, wherein New South Wales
is, in proportion to its population, more
blessed than any other country on this impe-
cunious globe—remain apart as a fund for the
construction of public works. Thereby New
South Wales would get its railways, tram-
ways, roads, bridges, telegraphs, telephones,
wharves, harbors, gaols, and other public
buildings supplied to it without paying a
penny of taxation for them, or incurring
another penny of debt, or adding anything
at all to its interest bill. And it is a very
small thing to ask, after all.
* * * *
The Bulletin doesn’t quote New South
Wales specially because it is in the aggregate
the worst offender in this matter—for it
isn’t. Proportionately to population, Queens-
land, South Australia, Westralia, Tasmania,
and Maoriland have all worse records than it,
and only Victoria shows a reasonably clean
sheet in this matter. But New South Wales
is allegedly the richest province of the group,
so it can best afford to go straight. It has a
larger land revenue than all the other six
provinces put together, so again it can best
afford to go straight. It is deteriorating
badly even from its previous bad state,
and if it keeps on as it has been doing in the
last four years, a time will come when it will
be about the most shocking example in
the Seven Provinces. Apart from every-
thing else, the motion of Chanter, M.L.A,
is directed at New South Wales alone, and
furnishes a handy peg on which to hang a
much-needed homily. Also, the greatest
obstacle to reform lies in New South Wales,
in the shape of the continuous struggle be-
tween Foreign-traders and Protectionists.
The effort of the Foreign-trade party in office
is not to make its finance clean, but to show
that that of the Protectionists was even
dirtier than its own. No Foreign-trader will
venture to complain of his own leader’s dis-
honest and ruinous loan policy, lest by ex-
posing it he lets the hated Protectionist
party get into office. If a Protectionist
draws attention to it, his effort is always set
down as an attempt to get office. Also,
if a Protectionist tries to reform the nefar-
ious loan system he is always met by the
brilliantly irrelevant answer that his party’s
system needed just the same kind of reform
when it was in office ; and if a Foreign-
trader makes a similar attempt he is informed
that the other crowd was just about as bad
in its day, and that it is no good changing
Ministries over such a matter under the cir-
cumstances. In fact, the whole reply to all
suggestions of financial reform in New
South Wales is “ You’re another,” or words
to that effect. At least two political parties
say that each of them is another, and they
both agree that the third party would be
another also if it got the chance.
A Cry From Private Enterprise.
The Bulletin has been favored with a copy of a
pamphlet, by one F. Allen, of Maoriland, on the
evils of State Fire Insurance, as proposed in that
province ; its foolishness, and injustice, and the
manner in which it interferes with the liberty
of the subject, and many other things. The
writer is local manager for a well-known English
insurance co., and, as he gets his crust and cheese
by the present system, he is naturally qualified to
take a perfectly unbiassed view of that system.
Also, as the adoption of universal, compulsory
State fire-insurance would deprive him of his pre-
sent job and salary, he is naturally qualified to
take a perfectly unbiassed view of that likewise.
* * * *
The case of the unbiassed person against State
interference begins with an assertion that has been
on deck since the controversy first started: —
State fire-insurance violates the fundamental principle
upon which fire insurance is conducted. Fire insurance
is a question of averages, of distribution of risks, not
alone in variety, but in area. The large fire-insurance
companies are world-wide in their operations. A State
scheme confining insurance to one colony flies in the face
of recognised experience and scientific principles. It
oncentrates instead of distributing the risk. In the case
of a large fire the loss is distributed over a number of
companies, and the effect is not severely felt.
It is about time this argument, anyhow, was taken
out and decently buried. If the M.L. Govern-
ment compulsorily insures all the insurable pro-
perty in Maoriland, then it will have as much
variety of risk as any civilised community can well
afford. And it will have, for all practical pur-
poses, as wide a distribution of risks as if its busi-
ness spread over Europe, Asia, Africa,. North and
South America, and Australasia. The one great
object of the distribution of risks is that one in-
sured building won’t take fire from another already
in a state of combustion, till the total loss becomes
larger than the insurance co. can conveniently
face. And if Invercargill takes fire it can no more
spread the conflagration to the Bluff township,
17 miles away, than it can to New York. A ten-
mile distribution is as good, for all practical pur-
poses, as one of 1000 miles —and it saves a vast
amount in working expenses. Anyhow, the most
prosperous fire insurance co. in Australia—the one
which shows the biggest and steadiest percentage
of profits—does confine itself to one province, and
has always done so. And every co. which has
burst disastrously in Australia spread itself over
a wide space—many of them as far as Great
Britain. These two circumstances give the lie
very considerably to the distribution argument,
anyhow.
* * * *
Then the disastrous F. Allen goes on to enlarge
his argument:
As exemplifying the distribution of fire risks : —By the
Chicago fire of 1871 there were destroyed 17,450 buildings
of all kinds with their contents, and other interests; the
insurance on which, distributed amongst two hundred
and sixty companies, was £16,000,000. Of the two hun-
dred and sixty companies, more than sixty were forced
into absolute failure, whilst nearly as many more were
compelled to adopt measures for the immediate restora-
tion of their capital or assets.
So the commendable system of private fire insur-
ance, with its admirable distribution of risks,
went down crash whenever it faced a real
emergency. And the loss was severely felt;
also the insurers were badly left. Out
of 260 companies, nearly a fourth couldn’t
pay up when they were wanted. A.nd if the
United States had insured the property it could
have paid up to the last farthing and hardly felt
the strain. The total loss was 40,000,000 dollars,
and, though the private insurance covered only
two-fifths, it wasn’t near all there when the time
came for paying the bill. A Federal fire insur-
ance department could have found the whole
40,000,000 dollars inside 24 hours. If the private
co. hasn’t the cash it closes up and leaves the in-
sured to go to ruin. If a State Department
hasn’t the cash its credit is practically unlimited.
It can pay in bonds that are as good as gold, and
redeem them by instalments out of future profits.
It can distribute its risks, if need be, over an un-
limited amount of time, as well as over a reason-
able area, whereas the private co. can distribute
them over area only. The advantages of the dis-
tribution of risks are really with the State system.
» - * * *
Some more of F. Allen :
As to rates, the “ Manager ” will fix them according to
his own will.
The proposed Government Bill provides for the com-
pulsory insurance of buildings, and, by some few words,
indicates that provision may be made for the insurance
of goods and chattels.
By the proposed Bill the “ nominal insurable value” of
buildings is defined to be not more than seventy-five per
cent, of the “capital value” : the “actual insurable
value ” is such amount as the “ manager ” may choose to
name, but in no case to be greater than the nominal in-
surable value ; the owner has not any voice in determin-
ing the amount of insurance on his own property.
Any officer or other person authorised by the
“ Manager ” in that behalf may at all reasonable (?) times
enter in any building for the purpose of inspecting the
same or the contents thereof, and every person who in
any way hinders such entry or inspection, or refuses to
give any information in his power relating to such build-
ing or its contents, or knowingly gives false information,
is liable to a penalty not exceeding fifty pounds.
Owners would lose all freedom of action. Whether
they would insure or not, for what amount they would
insure, are points which would be arbitrarily decided with-
out consultation with them. _ ,
Every owner would be compelled to insure with the
Government and for such amount as the “Manager
might determine ; but the Government would not be
compelled to insure every owner. .
The “Manager” has power under the Act to refuse to
insure any building he may consider undesirable.
are the owners of those undesirable buildings to protec
themselves ?
At present, rates are fixed by the managers of
private insurance companies according to tlieir
own will, which seems much the same thing. Am
the private manager seldom insures, if he knows
his business, much over 75 per cent, of the real value.


The private manager also refuses buildings which
he considers undesirable, and it is to the public
interest that undesirable buildings should be unin-
sured—buildings so badly constructed as to be un-
insurable should be discouraged out of existence.
It is also to the public interest that every person’s
property—if it is insurable —should be compulsorily
insured, if the owner hasn’t sense enough to insure
it without compulsion. When the uninsured man’s
property burns down he loses money himself—
which serves him right under the circumstances ;
but often his creditors and employes also lose
heavily by his loss, which is quite another thing.
Insurance isn’t a matter which concerns the owner
of the property alone ; and he ought to lose his
“ freedom of action ” seeing that it often means
freedom to bring disaster on others. He isn’t
allowed any freedom of action in the matter of
insuring his property against foreign invasion,
which is quite as great an outrage. His premium
—otherwise his contribution to the defence ex-
penditure—has no freedom about it.
A final sample of the case for private manage-
ment :
The inevitable losses which would be largely increased
under State management, would be met bj' additional
taxation, augmented premium income, or by drawing
upon other sources of public revenue.
One of the cardinal qualifications for the efficient fire
Underwriter is the knowledge how to “ select ” risks of a
profitable character for his company—a talent not inborn
and only to be acquired by practice—combined with care-
ful reflection and oftimes painful experience. It has been
stated as an insurance axiom that “ it were better to in-
sure gunpowder than goods of a man of bad character.”
One bad or ill-selected risk in any class may absorb the
whole profit on that class upon any year’s operations.
The skilled underwriter endeavors to select from the
risks submitted those he deems likely to pay, and, when
occasion arises, he does not hesitate to decline business.
This expert knowledge, if it were possessed by, would not
be of any use to, the “manager,” the necessity for selec-
tion being done away with by the Act. The Bill trans-
gresses every canon of fire insurance.
Obviously the losses can only be larger if there are
more fires, and no reason for excepting more fires
is set forth. Or if the State insures buildings
that are now uninsured and therefore collects
more premiums to pay the losses with. Or if it
stands a larger proportion of the loss than the
private companies do—and it has just been
anathematised for not standing a large enough
proportion by refusing to insure over 75 per cent,
of the value. Or if it insures buildings which the
private companies reject—and it has just been
cursed for drawing the line at undesirable build-
ings. _ And it appears that the private companies
only live by a wise selection of risks and the rejec-
tion of bad business—yet the fact that the State
Department proposes to reject very bad business
is assigned as a reason why it shouldn’t exist at
all. “F. Allen” asks how the owners of un-
desirable buildings are to protect themselves when
the State won’t insure them? Well, how much
better are they now when the great business of
the private co. ’s is not to insure undesirable build-
ings ? Also, it further appears that—
Statistics prove that even amongst the most carefully
managed companies the actual nett premium income,
after allowing necessary deductions for management, &c.,
leaves little more than sufficient, as a general rule, to
cover the actual losses incurred.
If this is the best that private management can do
then it had better give up bragging about the
methods which produce such poor results, and
sing in a humbler key about the advantages of its
unprofitable system of distributing risks, and
about the “canons” of private fire insurance.
Seeing that close attention to these canons leaves
practically no profits, and the companies are taking
their stupendous risks gratis, all this patronising
attitude ps uncalled for. And if the private co.’s
are making nothing out of the business they cer-
tainly needn’t rage so dreadfully about the State
annexing it.
* * a *
The representative of the private system which
has formulated such a perfect scheme of manage-
ment that it is earning nothing for the share-
holders, also casts a loose, miscellaneous brick by
the assertion that Maoriland “is a specially un-
promising field ” in which to try the proposed experi-
ment. That fiction, anyhow, refutes itself.
Maoriland has no big, congested city like Sydney
or Melbourne, where one-third of the population
and half _ the insurable property are crowded
together in one combustible area. Its biggest
centre contains only about one-twentieth of the
total population. Therefore it is, above all the
other provinces, a place where insurance risks are
widely distributed, that being allegedly one of the
great essentials of the business. At least, the
interested gentleman whose pamphlet throws so
much gloom on the question says it is, and he
ought to know.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Great Federal Joke.
More and more The Bulletin wonders what
kind of chance Federation has with the dread-
ful gang now in office in New South Wales.
At time of writing the latest edition of the
Federal scheme was being considered in Pre-
mier Reid’s unreformed Upper House, with
Attorney-General Jack Want in charge, and
the proposed Referendum clause had just been
disposed of. Seven months ago, when the
original Convention Bill was before the country,
this same J. Want raged at large on the
Referendum subject, and among other pearls of
wisdom that he got off his chest was the follow-
ing :
When they thought the Bill was safe it was taken to
Melbourne and the wolves began their game. The whole
body and the meaning of the Bill when it had been before
us was taken out. In the first place out went the Refer-
endum. They knew they had got enormous voting power
in the Senate, and they knew the Referendum was a
check on it. But when they got down there they kicked
the Referendum out. (Howls of “ Shame ” from the fools
who actually took J. Want seriously).
As a matter of fact, there was no truth what-
ever, nor even a semblance of truth, in this
statement. The Referendum was never in the
original Bill. The “ wolves,” which was
Attorney-General Want’s pleasant description
of the representatives from the other provinces,
didn’t kick it out, for no wolf or other animal
can kick out the thing that isn’t in. Premier
Kingston, of S.A., however, being one of the
wolves, made a great effort to put the Re-
ferendum in, as a substitute for the dis-
astrous three - fifths - majority scheme offered
by Carruthers, of New South Wales ; but
only Wise, of N.S. W., and ten wolves from
the other provinces, supported him (Convention
Hansard, page 967, 21st September, 1897).
Still, though Attorney-General Want’s state-
ment was mere beer-froth, and an absolute
inversion of the facts, and could only be
regarded as the loose shriek of a'very unscrupu-
lous provincialist, who was against union in
any shape or form, some people thought that
it showed a desire on his part to have the
Referendum inserted in the Federal Constitu-
tion, and set that down to his credit. The
N.S. W. Assembly did insert it, the other day,
among its proposed amendments, and, in the
Council, Attorney-General Want proceeded to
damn it thus (Hansard report) :
I do not want any one at any future time to say that I
voted for the Referendum because I do not approve of
it. ... I do not want bon. members to say that because
I have advocated the last alternative left in the resolu-
tion I have advocated the Referendum.
J lien Jack Want’s House of Parliament took
the hint and fired the Referendum out on its
ear, Want himself voting for the thing he
didn’t like, apparently as a mere matter of
form. The great majority, however, voted not
as the Attorney-General did but as he said he
wanted to do, and he took the rejection of the
obnoxious thing quite cheerfully. And now it
is about time for N.S. W. to ask itself how far
it can take its awful Government seriously in
Federal matters. The N.S.W. Ministerial
members of the Convention were almost solid
against the Referendum when Kingston and
others tried to put it in. Then a prominent
N.S.W. Minister incited the electors of N.S.W.
to reject the Bill because the Referendum
wasn’t in it, and alleged that the other pro-
vinces basely threw the Referendum out —which
they didn’t. Now the same N.S.W. Minister
says that he wants the Referendum to be out of
the Bill, and, in a sidelong sort of fashion, urges
its rejection. And the whole N.S.W. Govern-
ment is against the three-fifths-majority clause
which was inserted in the original Bill on the
motion of a member of the N.S. W. Government.
What a howling farce it all is !
The Crow-Eater’s I O U.
South Australia, which has already, in pro-
portion to population, the Biggest Debt on
Earth (Queensland’s only excepted), proposes to
borrow another .£1,798,750 this year. At 30th
June, 1898, S.A. owed £23,622,147, of which
£5,928,328 was buried in works which yielded
no direct return to the Treasury, and which
should, in all fairness, have been paid out of
revenue. Among the six Australian provinces,
Tasmania has the most awful record in the
misapplication of loans, over one-half its debt
being sunk in roads and bridges and other un-
productive undertakings. Queensland is second,
and S.A. and Westralia are about level for
third worst place. About £517,000 of the
present S.A. loau is for unproductive works.
One very lovely item is “ cost of floating loan,
£43,750 ” ; in this country even the stamp-duty
and the small incidental expenses for raising
the cash are charged to posterity and called
“ permanent and reproductive works.” S.A.
carries the swindle so far that, when it com-
pletes a new railway with borrowed money,
it even pays the working expenses up
to the end of the financial year out
of loans, which is something unique in
the way of the great borrowing swindle.
Also, the regular Northern Territory
deficit of some £70,000 a year is paid out of
the loan fund. It is supposed to be a temporary
advance to be repaid by the Territory later on,
but there is practically no one in the Territory
to repay it. This includes the shortage on
the one lonesome railway in the Territory ;
that remarkable line was built with borrowed
money, and as it has never even quite yielded
working expenses, the rest of the expenses and
all the interest on the loan are paid out of new
loans, and the interest on the new loans is
charged to further loans, and it all goes into
that remarkable account known as the debt
which the Territory owes to S.A., which is
practically the amount which the empty left-
hand pocket owes to the right-hand pocket—
the one with the left-hand pocket’s I O U in it.
The one good feature about South Australia’s
financing is that it pays ss. per cent, per annum
off the capital of its debt, which means that, in
a general way, it borrows £500,000 and pays
back £55,000. The great national P.N. of
South Australia is built on worse security than
that of any other province ; it has practically
no gold, very little known coal or other
minerals, little rain, much desert, and N.S.W.
wants to steal its only river by way of conclu-
sion. S.A. certainly produces a vast amount of
wealth for a province with so little visible
means of support, but, all the same, the second
biggest debt on earth is a tremendous load for
barren Crow-eater Land to stagger under.
The Same Old George and the Somewhat
Different George.
Just now both N.S.W. and Vic. Parliaments
are discussing Treasurer’s estimates. In
N.S.W., Treasurer George Reid— the Same
Old George— having sewn a Protectionist patch
on that Freetrade coat which has more colors
than Joseph’s, told the Assembly that he must
impose certain new duties, or provincial sol-
vency would be absolutely up the spout. And
he specified 3d. per lb. on tea; 2d. on dried
fruits ; something extra on probate ; and some-
thing on coffee, cocoa, and rice. In addition,
he proposed to signalise his Freetrade apostasy
by retaining or re-imposing the Protectionist
duties on sugar and its corollary products
which he had previously announced himself
specially ordained of Heaven to destroy.
The Same Old George was most emphatic
in asseverating that the duties couldn’t be done
without; but the Assembly ridiculed his em-
phasis. When the struggle was over, and Reid
rose, clutching the dishevelled remnant of his
demands, his tea duty had been lowered to Id. ;
his rice, coffee, and cocoa duties were gone
altogether; some minor items had been re-
duced, and his probate duties had suffered a
little ; and he pulled out of the fire some
£150,000 of new taxation, instead of the
£280,000 which he had told the House was
quite indispensable. Yet he took his half-loaf
with the best grace in the world, and is calmly
carrying on towards the deficit which, accord-
ing to his own assurances, is inevitable. As
for resigning because Parliament refused to
endorse his policy—he never dreamt of it.
He took Parliament’s policy, did Parliament’s
bidding, and threw the ancient notions of
constitoosh’nal guvment and ministerial respon-
sibility to the hot winds. A few days passed
and in Victoria the Somewhat.-Different George
Turner was submitting his estimates of expen-
diture, which provided for paying the mass of
policemen 6s. per day. Labor-member Tren-
witii moved that in the opinion of the Assem-
bly the minimum pay should be 6s. 6d. per day ;
and the amendment was carried. It had no
compulsory force to alter this year’s expendi-
ture ; the Govt, could have disregarded it and
re-fought the issue next year ; but it was enough
for George Turner that the House had failed
to endorse one small item of his estimates. The
curtain immediately rose upon aConstitoosh’nal
Crisis. When the Assembly met next day,
Premier Turner said the vote must be recon-
sidered, or the Govt, would not carry on. The
House reconsidered, and approved Turner’s
original estimate by 23 majority ; and the
ancient notions of Ministerial responsibility
threw up their hat in triumph. The situations
were not precisely identical. In the one case,
Vic. Assembly practically enjoined Premier
Turner to spend more money ; in the other,
N~.S. W. Assembly refused to give Premier
Reid more money to spend. N.S.W. Parlia-
ment was defensive ; Vic. Parliament aggres-
sive. But the broad lines are the same.
Barring the argument that Trenwith’s was a
snatch vote in a weak House, the trouble in
both places was whether the Assembly or the
Treasurer was to prevail in matters of finance.
Treasurer Turner insisted that the Assembly
should swallow his policy ; Treasurer Reid
swallowed the Assembly’s. In Turner’s place,
Reid would probably have caved. In Reid’s
place, Turner would have seen the Assembly
bio wed first. So that, in politics as in war,
it does not matter much whether you employ
the rusty old coustitoosh’nal weapons or the
new-fangled artillery which shoots in accord-
ance with the idea that Parliament exists to do
tiie people’s w'ill and the Ministry to do Parlia-
ment’s. It’s a question of the Men Behind the
Guns. Was Reid right? Was Turner
wrong ? The Bulletin considers that Turner
in Reid’s place would have been right; but it
cannot say that Turner in his own place was
wrong. Even under the sensible nominee
system—when Parliament elects its own
Ministry as an executive committee, without
binding itself to that Ministry’s “ policy ” or
absence-of-“ policy ” —a Treasurer w'ould have
a perfect right to stand by his own opinion, if
he thought that the result of enforcing Parlia-
ment’s opinion would be disastrous. He would
have a perfect right to threaten resigna-
tion if Parliament insisted—only it would
be merely his own resignation, not his
Ministry's! dissolution, or Parliament’s.
Under any system, nobody wants a
Minister to be an opinionless puppet. If
Turner saw all the evil consequences that he
alleged in Trenwith’s amendment, he was
quite justified in refusing to accept the amend-
ment. The fact that he persuaded the Assem-
bly to reverse the amendment may be taken as
evidence that the Assembly would rather lose
the amendment than lose Turner. Or it may
be evidence that Turner honestly convinced a
fuller Assembly that it had erred. It is
certainly evidence that Turner takes his
political principles seriously. That is why
Turner in Reid’s place would have done
rightly. For Turner’s acceptance of curtailed
duties would have meant that he still saw his
way to make ends meet, and found reasons for
believing that the future might justify the
Assembly’s view rather than his own. Reid’s
acceptance of curtailed duties merely means
that the future may take care of itself as long
as Reid takes care of office. For Reid is a
trickster who does not take his principles
seriously. Only his interest.
♦ ;
Tyson. ’
Old Tyson’s death opens the flood-gates of
anecdotage, and gives the embroidered liar a
rare chance. “ Hungry Jimmy ” and his
millions have focussed Australian eyes for a
quarter-century; and now the earth which he
grabbed has grabbed him, and given 7ft. x
4ft. in exchange for odd hundreds of square
miles. As the old man apparently died in-
testate, the addition and division of his pro-
perty among scores of heirs and heiresses —
chiefly nephews and nieces—will keep the
interest lively for quite a while. If Tyson
had owned a sense of humor, he might
have supplied a secret marriage and a
thunderbolt of a buxoin wife to help things ;
but he wasn’t built that romantic way. Pro-
bably Tyson was the only natural-born Aus-
tralian over 12 years old who never hugged a
woman in his life ; anyway, the woman who
can say Tyson hugged her hasn’t been found at
date of writing. The only goddess he ever
adored was Diva Moneta ; and to her he
clove faithfully. His money may have made
him happy; but he never looked like it.
Tyson never smoked “ couldn’t afford
it ” ; but, when travelling, he always
patronised the smoking-compartment, where
petticoats couldn’t hurt him. The Bulletin
often thought of offering Tyson Is. 9d. for an
article on women (when a man has millions,
you may almost as well offer him Is. 9d. as
100 guineas), but it never acted, and now ’tis
too late. Yet the old man’s speculative clutch-
ings at the skirts of the Eternal Mystery
would have been interesting. He “ couldn’t
afford ” marriage either. He “ couldn’t
afford ” liquor, or theatres, or society, or
a bit on the Melbourne Cup, or any of the
things that the ordinary £2OO-a-year person
takes his pleasure in. He was like the high-
class Maori chief who thoughtfully chopped off
his toes in order to get his fine new European
boots on. Tyson lopped this passion, and
smothered that appetite, and sacrificed yonder
human satisfaction ; but he made money. Six-
foot and a bit he was ; but a pretty stunted
specimen of humanity all the same, Bulletin
reckons. He died so much while he lived
that it’s no wonder he wouldn’t let a doctor
try and deprive him of the economy of
being dead altogether. But he made money
—or gathered dead leaves there was no
difference. In his daily life, and in a
small way, Tyson was a mean man—he
gave nothing for nothing ; but occasionally he
could be generous in a large way —that is, as he
grew older he gave away considerable sums,
and knew' himself as rich as he was before. The
widow who gave her mite was more generous.
Just, Tyson was ; but hard. “He assisted
Queensland by purchasing £250,000 Treasury
Bills ” —at a safe 4 per cent. They made him
aQ. Legislative Councillor (it meant free rail
travelling); and he was merely another nominee
nonentity. Grit, industry—these were his
virtues ; and in reclaiming land and breeding
stock he helped others, but his chief end was to
help himself. Many a millionaire has made
money to spend in noble actions : Tyson made
money to keep it—and he kept it as long as
ever he could. There is need of such men to
enforce the gospel of work in this country of
shirk ; and Tyson’s was not a bad life. But,
considering his powers and opportunities, how
far from the best !
-»
One Bullock.
Another case of wrongful conviction followed
by imprisonment has just turned up at Bathurst
(N.S. W.). In October last Catherine Ahern
was charged with stealing a bullock, and
Robert Hogan with being accessory. A few
weeks prior to this, Constable Reardon had
happened along when Mrs. Ahern had just
completed the slaughter of one of her own
animals. Apparently out of natural suspicion
the constable demanded to see the hide, which
was produced. A few days later he returned
for another look at said hide, which had in the
meantime been cut up for thatching purposes.
This increased the suspicion of the bobby, who
forthwith seized the remains of the bullock’s
cuticle and arrested the two supposed cattle
stealers, alleging that the beast killed was one
that Marmont, a local grazier, had missed some
time previously. At the trial it was admitted
that neither owner Marmont nor one Weeks,
on whose paddocks the lost bullock had
been grazing, had reported the loss of
any cattle, and both of these men swore
that, though the hide produced resembled
that of the absent beast, the feet of the
slaughtered animal were not the same as those
of their bullock. Finally, the nominal prosecu-
tor, Marmont, gave Mrs. Ahern— an old lady
of 68 and a resident in the district for 40 years
■— an exceedingly good character. Neverthe-
less, an astonishing jury convicted them, and
the court sentenced them to 9 months’ and 4
months’ hard labor respectively. A week or
two back Marmont’s missing bullock turned
up from the bush in a casual, cursory sort of
way, and, after due investigation and proper
representation and mature deliberation, the two
prisoners were last week released. There is no
moral attached hereto, nor any warning to be
given ; it is just one more instance of what may
happen to a man or woman—any man or any
woman—who goes about his or her usual work in
the usual fashion. The one redeeming feature of
the case is that there actually was a bullock,
anyhow. For in the ordinary cattle-duffing
case the law generally takes the bullock’s exist-
ence for granted to begin with, and assumes,
without much evidence, that it has really dis-
appeared ; and in most cases the finding of a
hide said to look at all like the one said to have
been on the alleged animal whose alleged dis-
appearance is the cause of the trouble is quite
sufficient to convict somebody. For the accused
is mostly tried before 12 jurymen whose God is
Beef, and their creed is that, the country being
wide and empty, and bullocks being easy to
shift in the dark, it is advisable to convict any-
body as a warning to somebody else.
During the recent trial of a man and a woman
for conspiracy to criminally assault a Sydney
girl named Ida Withey, it transpired that the
girl had been previously assaulted, or said she
had. Yet at the police-court enquiry the police
suppressed all mention of the previous assault,
and told or allowed the girl to suppress it
too; though her “whole truth” and her
complete chastity ” were material to the case.
“ Sergeant Blackburn, who had the case in hand, denied
that he had instructed the girl Withey- to make a false
declaration. lie did not mention the Manly case in giving
evidence, because he thought it would hamper the prosecu-
tion.”
Presumably, the police would go just as far in an
equally laudable effort to hamper the defence.
The accused were discharged. The Bulletin
notices with pleasure that the police-faked case
has been falling to pieces very frequently of late,
though not half as frequently as it ought to.

Dear Bulletin.— The fact that S.A. Parlia-
ment accepted by a large majority the idea of
providing for the cut-off-with-a-shilling widow,
against last year’s unsuccessful attempt, had
undoubtedly much to do with the Grotty will-
case. It is the old story that one good illus-
tration teaches the average man, who is
an ass and an M.P., more than 16J columns
of unanswerable logic. Which gives me a
chance to introduce my great national scheme
to the notice of the Australian public, free of
advertisement charges. It is intended to float
a Company for the Speedy and Satisfactory
Accomplishment of Australasian Federation,
the capital, £lOO,OOO, to be raised by subscrip-
tion, £5 a share, amongst earnest Federalists all
over the continent. With this sum I bind
myself to create and maintain in good working
order a first-class war-scare (quality guaran-
teed and no shoddy). Among the pro-
minent features of the plan of campaign
looms largely the charter of a dozen casual
steam tramps, to be turned by aid of
paint and canvas into cruisers. £lO,OOO will
have to go in bribes to Reuter and co. for an
efficacious backing-up in panic-raising cables
(it does not come unnatural to a cable-man to
tell lies), and I confidently prognosticate that
my high-class realistic night-attack on Sydney
harbor, under the Russian flag, will have the
needful unifying effect on Australian politics.
Among my first triumphs, I expect the sudden
demise, from paralytic stroke, of that able
statesman and shuffler, G. H. Reid. Further
particulars on application to the office of the
company, that also holds for sale a highly-desir-
able back-country block as a site for the future
capital. Buyers of 10 shares will receive a
medal describing bearer as a father of the
country, to be worn (not the father) on State
occasions, with a blue ribbon, and handed
dow'n to posterity as a decoration really worth
having, even unto the fortieth generation.
Write at once to the hon. sec., V. KoTZE.
