Flindbrs-st. Pitch Fitzgeralds’ Circus.
Hot crowds hustle each other harder than
ever at the Tivoli doors since Mr. Ajax arrived
without his bones. This filleted hero makes his
first stage entrance inside a big flower bud, which
bursts at a pistol shot and lets out a little bundle,
known to be a man only by its head. The neat-
ness of a draper’s parcel is nothing compared to
the spick and span manner in which the boneless
being rolls himself up. Legs, arms, body, are
creased, and doubled, and folded, and re-folded as
easily as if they were part of a calico shirt. When
the contortionist has entirely upset ail the
arrangements of nature, and has feet sticking out
behind his ears, and hands growing from the
middle of his waist, and one leg is round his neck,
and the other is tied into a true lover’s knot with
an arm, and the place where the spinal column
isn’t has twisted itself into the demesne vacated
by the ribs, and the whole erection quietly folds
itself up backwards, and a smile comes to you
from behind the left ankle in the middle of the
back, a sick desire for an interval seizes even
healthy-minded beings who can watch dogs chew
each other without a qualm. The rest of the
programme includes strong man Sampson, Cora
Caselli (whose high kicking is one of the best
things in the _ entertainment), and The Dazzler
(whose claim to distinction is still clouded in
mystery).
** * *
Various disagreeable things have happened
to Sampson, the strong man of Sydney Tivoli,
since this paper last had the pleasure of mention-
ing him. One of his remarkable feats consists in
breaking shillings between his fingers, and one
night an indignant committee-man, who had
handed over the coin and received it back in a
shattered state, alleged publicly that he hadn’t
got back the right shilling. The uncharitable
inference which some people drew there and then
was that Sampson carried shillings about with
him ready cracked, and broke them instead of the
sound ones handed to him. Sampson decided to
clear his character by a public exhibition of chain-
breaking at the Queen’s Statue, and was allowed
to make a disgraceful blockade of an important
thoroughfare in doing it. The understanding was
that two horses couldn’t break the chain and that
Sampson could ; but the horses did break it. Con-
sidering that the huge mob took a hand in the pro-
ceedings, and walloped the horses into greater
efforts, this isn’t so very wonderful. Then Sampson
broke the chain over again and departed insensible
through over-exertion. About three-fourths of
Sydney is now struggling with the question : Is
Sampson real or is he some kind of a fraud ? The
Bulletin, as its little contribution to the con-
troversy, suggests that it doesn’t matter much. If
Sampson is real then he is a foolish and
dreary individual uselessly breaking _ and lift-
ing property in public. If he isn’t —well,
he is quite as dreary, and the property
is broken or lifted just as uselessly. If the
dreariness is there and the breakage and the lift
are there, it is all this writer asks for anyhow—or
rather it is all he doesn’t ask for. The present
scribe wouldn’t pay to see Sampson if he is all he
professes to be, for then he merely ranks with the
ox and the steam crane and other uninteresting
objects. If he was sure, however, that Sampson
was a sinful fraud then he would pay to
see him—if he could get in cheap. For if Samp-
son is an ordinary weak fellow-creature who can
break things by some mysterious trick, then this
writer wants to learn the trick and break one or
two things himself. He would begin on Mr.
George Pile’s dog and break it in three places.
** & *
That tired and fragmentary feeling which
goes before the season when people eat too much
fowl on an empty stomach is already spreading
over the shows of Sydney. They are sicklied o’er
with the pale cast of duff. Always just before
Xmas there is a dull and unreal period, when few
people go to the theatres, and most people are
saving up their theatre money to buy the great
masses of hot, rich food that they crush into
themselves at the hottest season of the year.
Williamson, the wisest manager of the crowd,
closes his big theatre for the dead week just before
the yearly national drunk and indigestion. The
great “Harbor Lights” spectacle will blaze for
the last time on Saturday night, and then it will
depart with the proud consciousness of being the
very best “ Harbor Lights ” that Sydney has ever
seen, and leave Her Majesty’s in gloom for eight
nights, including Sundays. On Monday (December
26) the pantomime of the “ Forty Thieves ” falls
due, and as there are no signs of any other panto,
in Sydney it should have a large future before it.
* * *
“Robbery Under Arms,” with Dumpier
in his grand old character of the bushranger, and
Maginnis, and O’Hara, and the gum-trees, and all
the rest of the well-remembered and well-beloved
properties, has distinctly retrieved the fortunes of
the Criterion. At time of writing, it had done
good business for 21 nights, and nothing definite
was yet heard about its withdrawal. When it
goes, the gruesome tale of “His Natural Life”
will follow, and Gabbett, who has so often success-
fully eaten his mate in the bush, will again refresh
himself on that misguided person.
The Lyceum, having devoted Saturday to
the tremendous spectacle of Lucifer and Dunn
dragging each other about till far into the night
and well on to the confines of Sunday morning,
didn’t have its usual change of programme on
Monday. But at time of writing (Tuesday) it
was announced that “His Natural Life” would
be in full blast on Wednesday evening, and pre-
sumably by the time this issue appears Gabbett
will be busily chewing his victim in the Tasmanian
bush. Why there should be such a run on that
deplorable man of teeth that two theatres should
announce him simultaneously the present literary
authority knoweth not. In a general way it prog-
nosticates that the Lyceum show will have the
better Gabbett and that the Criterion will have
far the better Rufus Dawes. No one in Australia
can excel Dampier in the latter character, and, on
the other hand, Frank Harcourt, who will be the
Lyceum Gabbett, should put up a record in that
character—always provided he nas the right teeth

Edward Farley is organising a grand opera
co., with Miss Lilian Tree as prima-donna, Leu-
mane as principal tenor, and Rivers Allpress as
conductor; and the new combination will make
its first appearance under the MacMahons’ manage-
ment at Sydney Lyceum on 24th December
(Christmas Eve). “Carmen” will be the first
production, and to follow that are “ Rose of
Castille,” “Trovatore,” “Maritana,” “Bohemian
Girl,” “Lucrezia Borgia,” “Lily of Killarney,”
‘‘Martha,” “La Sonnambula,” &e.
Orpheus M‘Adoo’s Jubilee Singers are to
commence a season of old plantation songs and
negro melodies at Sydney Palace on Saturday.
The jubilators left a pleasant memory behind them
the last time they jubilated in Sydney, and if that
city has the craving after good music that it is
commonly credited with, the Singers will be a
great boom. The co. is 13 strong, including five
ladies, and every member of it comes highly
recommended.
** * *
“Fun on the Bristol” is doing a very
creditable revival at Sydney Alhambra, and some
respectable amusement is being knocked out of
Johnny Sheridan’s old comedy.
#* * *
Melbourne seems to have nearly made up
its mind to neglect Brough and Co. at the Bijou,
as it neglected Cartwright and Co., and the
management can only pray for luck with the Xmas
venture of “Madame Sans Gene.” Meanwhile
business is like unto the miserable sinners who
have no health in them, and want to be delivered
f, —t ev ii. “Lord and Lady Algy,” a charming
o >medy in some respects, drooped and pined
after the first night, and gave place on
Saturday to “The Happy Life,” which is
deservedly doomed to a joyless career in
this country. Manager Brough threw away
his sovereigns on the purchase of the afore-
said comedy. “ The Happy Life ”is an un-
real entertainment in four foolish acts. The
situations are jerry-built structures of the
thinnest stage material, bedaubed with grease
paint and streaks of gloom. Some tales of
sham sentiment and forced emotion are toler-
ably clever, but this tale isn’t clever, or amus-
ing (except for ten minutes in the second act).
A girl falls insensible outside the apartments
of a priggish young bachelor late at night,
and he dumps the damsel into his arm-chair,
where she remains limp and speechless for
eight hours at a stretch.
* * * »
Next morning he takes her back to
her people in a cab, and everybody, save
a level-headed Yankee, agrees that it was
a horrible calamity for an unmarried girl to
be insensible in the private room of any
bachelor other than a duly-qualified medical
man, so the bachelor gazes nobly at the ceiling
and undertakes to marry the weeping inno-
cent. After marriage they don’t know that
each is in love with the other until they
arrange to live apart for six months. After
about six hours of this idiotic separation
the girl comes along and groans outside her
husband's bachelor “diggings,” the same as
she groaned in the first act, whereupon they
make mutual discoveries of affection and the
end is peace. A few touches of nature are
put into a casual boarding-house scene. For
the rest “The Happy Life” is a pompous
fiction of the hollowest kind. Also it is ill
suited to the Brough company. The heroine
gets freely referred to as a beautiful girl, and
it is now a matter of common remark in
Melb. that none of the Bijou ladies (bar
Mrs. Brough) look beautiful on the stage.
They are, probably, lovely in private
life, but you don’t notice it when they
act. Mrs. Brough doesn’t appear in this
show, so the task of impersonating youth
and beauty devolves upon an imported lady
who fails to fill the bill. Another imported
lady cuts an unconvincing figure in the part
of a smart ’Murkan girl. Brough, the al-
ways valuable Brough, goes for as little value
as possible on this occasion, and the now
thrice-tested Lovell is for the third time a
disappointment. He talks and acts on stilts,
after the manner of Cecil Ward in his early
newchum days—but even more so. Ward was
never so unhuman as the romantic Lovell.
Messrs. Victor, Percy Brough, Carne, Mario
Majeroni, Miss Thompson and Miss Temple
are successful in sundry places, and the elegant
mounting of the piece suggests an eighteen-
penny oleograph in a ten guinea frame.
** * #
Bland Holt has revived “The Fatal
Card ” at the Royal, by way of filling in the
quiet fortnight previous to the awful festive
season. There are striking moments in this
distempered melodrama, and some of them
hit the audience very hard. One of these
is the moment when Albert Norman, Arthur
Styan and the corpse of Charlie Brown re-
main speechless and motionless together,
whilst Baker, brave and unsuspecting as
usual, talks to the dead body from outside,
on the mat. And again, when Styan vents
an unpremeditated gasp, and when the de-
signing Miss Watson conducts Norman off
the premises. The succession of silent effects
at this point is pretty fine and gruesome.
Likewise the drama achieves a large explo-
sive climax, and the “comic relief” in-
cludes the episode of a nude Bland Holt
hiding up a tree, and becoming engaged
to a cheerful maiden, who squeezes his fish-
ing rod. Altogether “The Fatal Card” is
a drama well worth playing, and the
dulk of the playing is good. 'Arthur Styan, in
particular, is a shining Gentle Jim; Norman
would be capital if he were less fond of the sound
of his own voice, and the minor members of his
gang deserve credit for dividing the spoil in black
belltoppers. (This world’s spoils do mostly fall to
men in black belltoppers and frock coats, the
present writer is thinking). But, on the other
hand, it seems hard to believe that Charles Brown
is “beautifully dead,” although Norman makes
the statement without a smile. One would rather
suppose that the raucous comedian is humorously
defunct.

Melb. Princess’s is just now closed for
rehearsals of “The Geisha,” which gaudy light
opera will face an expectant populace on Saturday
(17). The Firm promises some necessary additions
to the co. for this costly event. A pair of Misses
Perry have arrived from London and one of them
will take the principal soprano part: also Miss
Rose Musgrove is to appear for the first time as a
full blown professional, and baritone Pauli will
bring his renovated voice to bear upon the subject.
Miss Vane, Lily Titheradge, Laun, Howard Ver-
non, Bathurst and a large array of scenery, ballet
and Japanese effects are included in the bill.

At the Opera House they are hanging out
the distressful tidings that Peggy Pryde has
reached the “last nights” stage of her rorty
career. Peggy, however, is still going pretty
strong ; likewise Travis, the well-tailored ventrilo-
quist with the automatic, wooden-legged dummies.
On Saturday next, strong-man Sampson will
arrive from Sydney and vary the proceedings by
lifting the auditorium with his left hand, and
other remarkable feats of muscle.

. Fitzgerald Bros, are returning to Mel-
bourne after an absence of two long, weary years.
On Wednesday, Dec. 21st, those bronzed wan-
derers will open their tent in Flinders-street,
under the shadow of the Australian Church, and
every time that Pastor Strong likens the devil to
a roaring lion brother Dan will stir up his
menagerie and give the congregation fits. The
circus and wild beasts will be the same as appeared
in Sydney of yore, plus some astonishing novelties.
The tawny monarch of the woods, who lately had
a few growls with the elephant, has consented to
express peace and goodwill by devouring his
Xmas dinner on the back of his bulky colleague.
At Poverty Point.
“ J.S.” : The Australian who laughs at foreign ignor-
ance as exemplified in the “ Robbery Under Arms ”
poster has a corrective pill at hand for his hilarity. “The
Firm ” has circulated—as shop-window advts.—large
photos of the deck-scene in “Harbor Lights.” In this
a group of officers and blue-jackets pose around a gun
casement. Now the service regulation re face ornaments
in the way of hair says either full beard and moustache
or clean shave—all or none. In aforesaid picture all the
blue-jackets wear moustaches, a thing no jack-tar is
allowed to do. Also, blue-jackets are not as scarce here
as bushrangers are where that R.U.A. poster was done,
and W. and M. ought to know better.
Miss Alice Simmons, a very melodious singer, of
Rickards’ co., leaves for London, where all good Aus-
tralians go. She is a Ballarat girl, a pupil of Miss Fanny
Simonson and Signor Cecchi, and her voice seems to have
a future before it.
Miss Yirgie Vivienne has gone back to the stage—the
nice girl who leaves the stage almost always goes back to
it sooner or later—and has been doing Hamlet and
Ophelia, and other characters, in the goldfields-towns of
W.A. And the local paper has fallen down at the stately
Virgie’s feet and worshipped her.
“ Trilby’s love-match is now flickering, and is expected
to be extinguished by the divorce court shortly,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “It is said that
Mr. H. R. Irving has not been fully seized of the limita-
tions of a benedict, and Dorothea Baird, who dbbuted in
similar feet, will carry her lathy willowiness before a
tribunal, and ask that the mannerist’s son be deprived of
any claim on even the smallest pink nail of her world-
famous extremities.”
“ Maskelyne, the Egyptian Hall fakir, has been struck
a 500-pound blow in the trouser-pocket by a jury’s ver-
dict,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
“ The man of mystery has for many years been displaying
a ‘ disappearing-man ’ trick, with the aid of a box, the
secret of the construction of which he twice daily offered
to bet £5OO no one could fathom. Two young clerks
built a box which dissolved a man expeditiously, and
claimed the magician’s solid shekels. But the fakir said
his particular secret had not been discovered, and hence
the law case. The conjuring clerks won, and a big slump
in stage-money challenges may be expected.”
“ Sin and Its Shaddows” was the advertised title of a
blood-curdler recently produced at a Sydney theatre.
Mrs. Keightley, mother of Cyril Keightley, a promising
young Australian actor, and the heroine of an episode
in Boldrewood’s “ Robbery Under Arms,” died the other
day in Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney.
“Stroller” : The dead, black background used by
Dante in some of his illusion scenes absolutely rivets
attention on the performer. Every move and look
becomes intensely significant from sheer concentration of
interest. It shows how much is taken from an actor by
scenery, and suggests the experiment of trying a drama
—necessarily of the emotional class —with such a back-
ground as a measure of the actor.
“Dab.”: Re Walter Baker’s unscientific disposal of
the Bristol Chicken in “Sporting Life.” I was sitting
next to a way-back cookie during performance in Cup-
week, and when the hero administered the coup-de-grace
Hayseed jumped up disgusted. “ D it! ” he said ;
“ I’m off. It’s a fake ?”
“F.C.”: The dreadful Greek gipsies—formerly of
Syria, or Antioch, or Kalamazoo, and latterly of Ballarat
—after camping round various Melbourne suburbs, have
at last established themselves on a vacant block fronting
the esplanade at that holy of holies—St. Kilda. To the
inhabitants of that aristocratic township the visitors are
an expense and a grievance, but the local “ authorities ”
are somewhat chary of moving them on, lest they go and
camp at Toorak, or in the grounds of Govt. House. The
gypsies run a merry-go-round, near which the whole tribe
camps in tents, these being made of bags which the most
God-forsaken Murrumbidgee whaler would disdain, and
the whole outfit is a source of amusement—also of instruc-
tion—to the local youths, at night. It includes
two or three young women, passably good-looking,
of the Madden variety—plump, to wit. These tell for-
tunes—at a penny a tell, more if you feel inclined or are
susceptible. But they are a poverty-stricken, hopeless,
useless crowd, and the difficulty is to know what to do
with them.
Bill Nye and conjurer Hermann met at a restaurant.
Hermann, cutting open a lettuce, found a diamond ring,
and exclaimed, “Ha 1 What have we here?” “Oh,”
says Bill, offhand, “ Those are little things I scatter for
the waitresses.” With that he gave the ring to one of
the girls 1 The disconcerted conjuror had to beg hard to
get it back.

on the variety stage in ’Murka, and is drawing big crowds
and big cheques. Ah, if little, squat, bediamonded Urso
were a beautiful, tasteful woman, what lovely cheques
she would be able to make 1

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
At the end of November the revenue of five
Australian provinces stood as follows, when compared
with the situation at 30th November, 1897. In the cases
of Victoria, N.S.W., Westralia, and Queensland the figures
show how the revenue for the past five months compares
with the corresponding five months of last year. In the
case of Tasmania it shows how the past eleven months
compare with the first eleven months of ’97 :
Increase over cor-
responding period
of 1897.

New South Wales (5 months) ... 234,148

Tasmania (11 months) .. ... 60,739
Decrease.
Westralia (5 months) .. .. 121,527
The S.A. returns are only published quarterly, so the
latest available figures are up to 30th September.
The revenue of each province for the month of
November, 1898, as compared with that for November,
1897, pans out as follows :

New South Wales .. £847,643 £66,120

Westralia .. .. 200,033 39,352
Premier Reid, of N.S.W., estimated that for
the year ending 30th June, 1899, he would require about
£192,000 more revenue than he received last year to keep
things straight. He has already, with only fix e months
gone out of the twelve, received £234,000 more than he
collected up to the same date last year, and even if the
new tea and other duties hadn’t been imposed he would
have had, in five months, more than all the extra income
he professed to want in the whole year. Yet though
the monthly increase is growing larger instead of
smaller (it was £10,173 in September, £57,055
in October, and £66,120 in November) Mr. Reid still
alleges that he has reason to expect a great decrease
later on, and that the new probate-duties are necessary to
provide for it. Yet, on the surface, it is difficult to see
where the anticipated fall is coming from. At present,
expenditure is only £64,243 ahead of income ; this time
last year the deficit was £211,343.
One thing absolutely certain is that the loan ex-
penditure keeps steadily growing. For the five months
ended 30th November, ’9B, it was £789,590 as compared
with £571,259 for the corresponding five months of last
year. So the demand for increased revenue isn’t because
Premier Reid proposes to charge the mending of the old
bridge, and the new crockery for Jenolan accommodation-
house, and the various unproductive repairs and patches,
to income, as any honest Government would do.
Tasmania’s revenue for the month shows a de-
cline of £125 as compared with the receipts for Novem-
ber, 1897. It isn’t much of a decline, but it is the first
time in about three years that the upward movement in
the public income of Raspberry Land has been arrested.
Undoubtedly this will be the biggest year’s revenue
which Tasmania has ever recorded, but it won't reach
Treasurer Fysh’s estimate of £913,275, unless December
pans out for £90,000 odd, which isn’t likely. And when
Fysh looked far ahead into other years and estimated the
1899 revenue at £907,535 he was a very sanguine Fysh
indeed.
John Forrest, of Westralia, estimated his year’s
revenue at £2,905,350, or fully £150,000 more than that
for year ended 30th June, 1898. Instead of an increase
he is already, with only five months gone, £121,527 to the
bad, and the prospect is that the year’s revenue will be
£500,000 below the estimate. If the drop comes to an
end when the revenue gets down to £2,000,000, Forrest
will be the luckiest Treasurer in Australasia. At that
rate, he will be receiving about £ll per inhabitant, and
the province with the next biggest revenue, Queensland,
raises only £7 15s. 6d. annually per head. The fall of re-
venue in October is the biggest decline in any one month
up to date.
• • • *
The Colonial Mutual Fire Insurance Co. (Mel-
bourne) closed its financial year on 30th September, 1898,
with a loss of £3987, the great fire of nearly a .year ago
being, as usual, the cause of the trouble. ' Under these
circumstances, the dividend is wisely suspended. To
meet losses £2500 has been transferred from the reserve
fund, leaving only £12,500 at credit of the fund. This is
the first streak of bad luck in the co.’s recent record :
Profits. Dividends.
September 1892 £4lll Nil.
„ 1593 7139 Nil.
„ 1894 8192 Nil.
„ 1895 8584 Nil.
„ 1896 8790 5 per cent. = £1875
„ 1897 8848 6 „ 2250
Loss.
„ 1898 3987 Nil.
7 years profits £41,677 Dividends £4125
At the end of 1891 the Colonial Mutual Fire Co., then
13 years old, was at the lowest ebb of its fortunes. It
had no reserves of any kind, and more than half its
paid-up capital was lost. Since then it has restored
the £19,911 of lost capital out of earnings ; has created a
reserve which now stands at £12,500 ; written £3OOO off
the assets to provide for depreciation ; built up a con-
tingency fund of £2OOO in case of further depreciation ;
and has still a small profit balance of 1141. Altogether
it has been a most marvellous recovery, and despite the
present year’s misfortune there seems no reason why it
should not continue. If the dividend is kept rigidly
down to 5 or 6 per cent, for another dozen years, the
C.M. should, under the sort of management it has had in
recent years, become one of the strongest concerns of its
kind in Australia. One notable feature is that there are
absolutely no mortgages among the securities, which is
probably all the better for the shareholders; and the
deferred bank deposits have been so carefully written
down that those realised on last year yielded £3BO more
than their book value.

The North Queensland Insurance Co. shows a
loss of £2071 on its underwriting business for the year
ended 30th September, 1898, but the receipts from com-
mission, rent, and interest (£3160) cover this and leave a
small profit of £lOB9. It escaped the Melbourne fire of
last year, so the usual excuse doesn’t apply in its case ; 4
very bad year in the always-precarious marine business is
the cause of the trouble. The North Queensland has one
of the largest fire and marine businesses in Australia, the
gross premium income for the year being £107,712. Some
recent results are as follows :

The present dividend, which absorbs .£lO4B
more than the year’s profit, is certainly not fairly earned ;
still in seven years the North Queensland Insurance Co.
has earned £lO,OOO more than it has paid away in divi-
dends, and has increased its reserve from £5OOO to
£15,000, so it shows better results than the majority of
companies which deal largely with marine business. A
co. doing a business of its size wants a much larger
reserve than £15,000; still this institution is only 13
years old, and for its age it has done reasonably well. Its
total funds are £69,371 (capital, £53,438; reserve,
£15,000 ; profit balance, £933). The only objectionable
feature in the accounts is the habit of lumping together
commission, interest and rents, instead of showing the
two latter items separately so as to throw some light on
the profitable or unprofitable character of the invest-
ments.

The National Bank (Melbourne) announces that
it will repay in April, 1899, one-half of the reconstructed
4J per cent, deposits still remaining. At the original re-
construction the National locked up £6,844,884, but by
30th September last it had repaid all except about
£1,600,000, and the operation next April will reduce the
balance to about £BOO,OOO.
• * • *
A cablegram announces that the British deben-
ture-holders of M’Cracken’s City Brewery (Melbourne)
don’t see their way to reduce interest on their £500,000
worth of securities below the present 4J per cent., but
that they are willing to extend the time for repayment
till 1918, and take their huge interest regularly till then,
provided the co. calls up another £500,000. The calling-
up of another £500,000 would so strengthen the co. as to
make the debenture-people’s 4J4 per cent, quite safe.
Which is a beautiful arrangement for the 4£ per cent,
debenture-holders. The great overburdened concern
might just get through under these conditions. Its latest
woes were something like this :
1891 .. Profit .. £45,711 1895 .. Loss ..£22,923
1892 .. „ .. 30,345 1896 .. „ .. 35,874
1893 .. Loss .. 4,636 1897 .. Profit .. 1,002
1894 .. „ .. 9,983
It has been decided to call up the £500,000, and if the
money, or a good part of it, can be got in (the
company has hopes of collecting £200,000), and the
£47,000 overdraft, and £99,996 mortgages, and some of
the other liabilities are paid off with the proceeds, and the
price of beer keeps up, and the local thirst maintains its
intensity, the concern ought to right itself and become a
dividend-paying business again.

To test practically how the process will pan-out,
the Australian (German) Metal Co., which has taken over
the Wetherill patent for the magnetic concentration of
the Broken Hill zinc “ tailings,” will at once put up, near
the mines, a plant with a capacity of 100 tons daily.

British 8.H., anxious to wipe off the deficit
which, according to cables, last half-year’s work shows,
taps its limited carbonate reserves again, and, pending
sales now being negotiated, is stacking the ore at surface.
* * * *
The B.H. Junction Consolidated, an affair
born of too much excitement over the Junction, has
been abandoned, after two years spent in attempts to
put life into it. Mr. H. L. Todd was appointed to do the
burying.
* * * *
George Sutherland, Mt. Adelaide Register
leader-writer, pegs away at the Broken Hill sulphides.
After eight years’ laboratory work, he believes he has got
at something—apparently a process for re-treating the
“seconds,” or metal-bearing particles which have not
been picked up in the first treatment. And as in these
particles about 50 per cent, of the metals is contained (in
some cases, reckoning in the zinc, much more), there is a
big margin to work on. Formerly the outsider who came
along with ore-reducing ideas to the big B.H. mines fell
back suddenly with the fixed idea that he had struck the
Klondyke or the South Pole, or something equally chilly ;
but now when they have to chase hard after a sixpenny
div., the inventor whose eye isn’t quite wild is offered a
dusted chair. Sutherland is smiled upon by the B.H.
Prop, officials, who, so far, put their faith in Liihrig
vanners for re-treating purposes.
* * * *
Some Cobar notes : Cobar Gold-mines (old Fort
Bourke) landing machinery each day. Much of their
splendid cyanide plant on the spot. Hoped it will be
well-managed when in operation. Mine admirably opened
up by manager Fox, who, despite his capabilities, cannot
make much profit by amalgamation. Question whether
it would not be better to go for smelting right away. At
Cobar, Nature provides everything the smelter requires.
-Oobar Chesney still shows good ore, and is prospecting
for ironstone, which it must find soon. Young Australian
mill hung up, and should remain so. Everyone looking
for the mammoth open-cuts mentioned by S.M. Herald,
but not discovered yet. Cobar Great Western about to
part with its mine-manager. Were it possible to choke
off the board of directors, too, this small but valuable
property might have a chance to show its capabilities.
Occidental re-started cyaniding its own tailings after a
short run on Great Western material. The Occidental
will do well to stick to its own ore ; there is plenty to
deal with. Broad bodies of ore at lower levels assay well,
and are being opened up.

Grenfell, N.S.W. The progress committee

failed unanimously at less than 500 ft.

N.S.W. ’9B gold production will be in the region
of 300,0000 z. Nothing to brag about, and safe to say it
has cost to produce as much as, if not more than, it’s
worth.
The Kanowna Gold Mines Co. (Kanowna
W.A.) has decided to become a defunct cat. The present
co. has had a tempestuous existence of six months. It
started*with many brilliant reports from “ experts” who
agreed that its riches were tremendous, also _ a mine
manager stated that the supply of loz. stone in sight
was practically unlimited. Other reports talked enthusi-
astically of 2Joz. to the ton. A sample of stone went
80oz. to the ton, but as the Kanowna G.M. Co. promptly
degenerated into a2dwt. show, theco. now concludes that
the sample in question didn’t come from its mine any-
how. Apparently the concern was a salted cat of a very
bad order —one indignant shareholder described it as a
“ putrid horse.” Most of the W.A. cats are dead now,
and the funeral of this lingering feline has attracted com-
paratively little attention.
* « * »
The Western Australian Smelting Co. (Fre-
mantle) sees light ahead again, and resumes the recently
suspended construction work. Smelting will be begun
early in the year.
Some Kanowna and Bulong alluvial-lead finds
were contemporaneous with the Melb. Cup season. And
so there has been a run on Grafter, Wait-a-Bit, Cocos,
and the rest of ’em in the claim-christenings. Time, too ;
for a change in mine nomenclature was wanted badly.
Strange, by the way, how some once-popular old mine-
names have dropped out altogether—Try-me-Well, for
instance.
* • * >
Kalgoorlie mines : —Of late the mines have had
little to do with the market, the latter having been
subject to all sorts of outside influences. First bears,
then war scares, consequently a general weeding-out of
weak holders, which in the end has a good effect. Stocks
in all big mines have been firming for the last six weeks,
and the medium-priced stocks have had some recovery
from the low ebb to which they descended a while
back. There is some concern at the delay in declaring
divs. by the Ivanhoe and Lake View Consols, many
thinking that possibly the assets have been absorbed
in some way or other by the London and Globe. Cable
assurances that this is not true have been received, and
substantial divs. this month are anticipated. During the
slump of some weeks back London absorbed a great deal
of scrip in the good mines. The Adelaide market got a
great shaking, but with much better prices it has now a
healthier feeling. London seems to he able to do as it
likes with the Australian market in these mines. In the
face of magnificent yields, enormous profits, and estab-
lished permanency, Australia finds itself quite unable to
stand up, and is knocked out by the bursting of
Bottomley, a momentary war-scare, or some other trifling
circumstance.
• * * *
“Onlooker”: If the Nullagine (W.A.) “dia-
mond find ” turns out to be worth anything, there should
be a bit of a lift for the Land of Waste Acres. Writer
knew an old digger—34 years in Maoriland, California,
the Cape, and Western Australia —who possessed a fine
8-carat yellow diamond, which he had found about 90
miles east of Marble Bar. When the old boy was asked
why he didn’t look for more he replied, “ Sonny, I’ve got
enough now to buy ’em ; let others look for ’em.” He
was a firm believer in the existence of diamond fields in the
West.

the pick of the Kalgoorlie basket.

Lake View Consols, W. A., for November: 6649
tons, 80380 z., average loz. 4dwts. Bgrs. The largest
tonnage yet milled in a month, and a still greater to be
treated this month. But why is the average coming
down in the face of the amazing bonanza, of which so
much was said some weeks ago ? Must be a lot of very
low grade ore in the mine, if many tons of this rich
“ tack ” were mixed with the month’s output.
* * * *
Klondyke, W.A. Dear Bulletin.— What do
you think of the name ? I have not had time to look at
many of the shows here yet, and as they all talk thou-
sands (one claim proprietor talks of £50,000), it will take
some time before I can get around with any chance of
business. So far, they are fair surface shows, nothing
more, and not equal to many I have seen on the Murchi-
son. An example of how some of the old Fremantle firms
still do business. From Condon, the port of this field, a
wire went for three doz. hobble-buckles, and in due course
three doz. galvanised iron buckets were received. Next
sent for three sets of cart harness complete, and the sizes
of the collars were given at 17in., 18in., and 19in. Every-
thing arrived except the collars. The celebrated Fre-
mantle firm sent instead 3doz. men’s collars, sizes 17,18,
and 19.—F.W.C.
• * * *
Tindall’s, Coolgardie, threatens to have a crush-
ing of 500 tons this month. Yield uncertain ; not so calls,
which are made with astonishing regularity. Twenty-
four made to date, and a sudden stop would give holders
too great a shock. If owned in N.S.W., it would have
dropped out long since, but the Vic. mine-owner is an en-
during animal.
* * * *
Each consecutive month for the last four, Kal-
goorlie has broken the record of its gold-yield, and no
doubt, as is usual on most mining-camps, the last clean-up
of the year will be the greatest of all. It is appalling to
imagine what the condition of Westralia would be to-day
but for this field—destined to be perhaps the greatest the
world has seen. Booms come without gold, and slumps
remain in spite of tons of it.
• • • •
Burbanks West—not far from Coolgardie—gone
bung in London, where shareholders are advised to apply
for a compulsory winding-up under official inspection to
see how Bottomley stands in this shocking deal. Shares
were distributed amongst Bottomley’s faithful henchmen
and his W.A. financial companies ; and through his official
organ the public were urged to buy them at a premium
before the prospectus appeared. Eight hundred a year
went in directors’ fees ; £5OO to the Joint Stock'lnstitute
for office accommodation ; and as the directors only had
£7500 to play with, next to nothing was spent on the
mine, the great richness or barrenness of which remains
unknown, just as it was before the co. started.

div. (ss). To date it has paid £1,524,000, equal to £127
per share. The first div. was declared on Jan. 31, ’7B, and
the entire paid-up capital is £29,600.

Castlemaine’s little revival was further helped
on the other day by the “ christening ” of new crushing
and other plant at Spring Gully—a mostly Bendigo con-
cern. Formed in ’94, the co. struck payable gold in
Nov., ’9B, and has since got out over 25000 z. of gold.
The stuff runs nearly an oz. per ton, and looks well ahead.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.— What has become of the pro-
mised report on the Mount Deddick alleged mines ?
Messrs. Ferguson, Howitt, and Foster, Government
geologists, were about Deddick from the beginning of
February till the latter end of May. When Mines-Mmis-
ter Foster, and other notabilities, visited Deddick on the
2nd May, it was announced that a full and exhaustive re-
port would shortly be published. Will Ministers Foster
(member for Deddick region) and Peacock (legal manager
for Deddick mines) get a hustle on this exhaustive report?
A pushing crowd like the Deddickers would have moved
Heaven and earth to get the report published long ago if
it was at all favorable, but perhaps they are sending it to
the Bureau of Information in London to give English
capitalists the first chance.—Duncan.
Vic Govt, proposes to spend during the next
three years £20,000 in boring for deep gold leads and coal
seams'. At Ararat, as at Ballarat, a lead is more or less
supposed to get awav under the basalt. Other tests will
be made N.E. of Beaufort, at Pitfield, Mt. Mercer, in the
Murray valley about Wodonga, on the Ovens, in the
Maryborough district, and in the hope of picking up the
Huritly (Bendigo), Avoca, Loddon and other leads. The
coal bores will be put down at Apollo Bay and in S.
Gippsland. This is thoroughly legitimate drill work. In
many of the localities shaft sinking, besides being in
general overwhelmingly expensive, is often practically
impossible on account of the drifts.
Long Tunnel Extended (Vic.): Word from
London that when the mail left the offer of £45 a share
for the mine could not be completed, owing to the dis-
turbed state of finances caused by rumors of war. But
the scare is all over, and shares are selling at £3O ; so it
looks as if the sale was off.

Queensland gold output for November, 1898,
was greater than that for Nov., ’97. More quartz has
been put through, but the results were not so rich as
previously. Nov., ’97, gave 74,5570 z. of gold for 71,569
tons of quartz, while last month only turned 0ut77,0120z.
for 110,556 tons. Mount Morgan, Gympie, Char-
ters Towers each dealt with a smaller quantity
of quartz for a smaller yield. Tailings and con-
centrates, however, came to the fore, 59,175 tons having
been treated on Ch. Towers for 27,8570 z. Total Novem-
ber yield for the province was 80,0760 z. Gympie calls
absorbed £11,995, and dividends only gave £4179. On
the Towers, dividends returned £20,900, and calls were
only £9946. Mt. Morgan dividends totted up to £29,166,
while little Eidsvold called up £537 from investors and
gave £2400 in dividends.
Towers’ return for November was 18,2920 z.
from 16,440 tons. Tailings and concentrates 27,8570 z.
Gympie gave 59670 z. from 6808 tons: tailings 3290 z.
Mt. Morgan treated 17,550 tons for 16,4790 z., while
Clermont’s alluvial produced 24790 z., as against 39110 z.
in Nov., ’97. The Etheridge shows a big drop— giving
only 9130 z. as against 14390 z. for same month last year.
Ravenswood treated 1620 tons for 1717ozs.—to say nothing
of concentrates that totalled 8460z5.
Gympie figures for the last eleven months run
thusiy :—89,185 tons of stone crushed for 93,4660 z. Divi-
dends for that period, £132,396 15s. lOd.; calls,
£114,686 18s. Bd. The chief yields for November came
from the No. 1, South Great Eastern, 563 tons for
13430 z. ; Columbia Smithfield, 1160 tons for 871 oz.; No. 2,
South Great Eastern, 861 tons for 8360 z.
The sale of the cyanide plant a few weeks ago
closed the operations of the Cumberland G.M. Co., on the
Etheridge. The mine in the early 80’s had an output of
about 1000 tons a month, worth over 20z., and was at that
time one of the best-equipped and managed mines in N.Q.
Purchased by the co. at a big figure, about 1888, it shortly
afterwards ceased to be payable—as per usual. Then the
co. spent thousands in vigorous and intelligent prospect-
ing down to 900 ft., without result. Next, cyanide works
were put up to treat the big heap of tailings left over
from the good times, and, when nearly complete, a record
flood washed away the dam and about half the tailings.
The remainder were treated successfully. Now the ground
is abandoned ; everything sold.
• • • •
Mt. Garnet, Queensland, privately floated in
Melbourne ; capital, £200,000. Vendors get £96,000 in
shares and £BOOO cash; 48,000 shares issued and 56,000 re-
served. Regarded by the best of the crowd of mining
experts, who invaded the province in the interests of
copper-mining or booming this year, as a more per-
manent-looking thing than the Chillagoe deposits.
It takes a boom-bred Melbourne man to find a
really up-to-date excuse for going insolvent. The latest
Yarra-side “ broker ” when filing his schedule gave as one
of his reasons, “ having to keep eight children.” The
Court will, no doubt, by-and-bye be asked to grant a cer-
tificate on the ground of insolvent’s having to keep him-
self.
“Shaker”: It begins to look as if snv-dting,
with railway-carriage to port, is going to do great tilings
for the Etheridge. Team-carried parcels lately sent to
Aldershot and Illawarra have given results up to £lO a
ton net profit. All the same, a few mines on payable '-old
below the 300 ft. level would do more.

stituents have been brought into their present position hy
mechanical agencies.” Fancy proffering this to the ordi-

The new battery of 40 stamps lately started at
the Galleymont gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feedert
without shovelling, and the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissler,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, &c., of Melbourne,
Adelaide, and Perth, the sole agent in Australia for J. and
E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride refrigerating and
ice-making machinery, is at present showing a large stock
of these machines, which have been got ready for the
summer season. The cost of refrigerators to produce
6cwt. of ice per day has been reduced to £lOO each.
Special prices are quoted for machines to make up to 50
tons of ice. The patent carbonic anhydride system has
been adopted by the Victorian and other Australian
Governments ; also by all the leading steamship com-
panies, meat companies, dairies, and butchers of the
world.*

This paper will not weep if the N.S.W.
Railway Commissioners do carry out their present
intention of excluding ’buses from Redfern
station-yard when once the George-street electric
trams begins to run. The condition of hundreds
of Sydney ’bus-horses is such a disgrace to civilisa-
tion that the sooner such spectacles are swept
off the face of the earth the better. See the
poor broken-kneed wretches, whenever they have
a chance to stand, trying vainly to rest themselves,
first on one crippled leg and then on another. It
is a sight which ought to hurt the heart of a stone
image.
Present writer was, the other dav, on a Sydney
’bus which stopped to change skeletons. Groom
began to harness-in a “moke,” when the collar
started back, revealing a patch of raw flesh.
Writer pointed it out to driver, who questioned
the groom. “Got to go in,” said that worthy,
“ Bill says so—so in she goes.” “ And I’ve got to
drive her,” said the Jehu, “or out I go. Oh,
well”—and he gathered up the reins—“off she
goes.” And the red-raw neck bent to the collar.

expected in the home of the humble toiler in the
vineyard. A considerable amount of jewellery
went away with the midnight marauder, part of
it being collected by Mrs. Bevan, who was com-

ment of the night, and produce a diamond ring as
a peace offering. When quiet was restored, and
the distressed individual (who said that his five
children had eaten nothing for three days) had gone,
the police were telephoned for. In theory the
reverend gentleman ought to have forgiven his
enemy and gone back to bed, but the toiler in the
vineyard, when his jewellery has been taken from
him, is apt to be very like any ordinary sinner.

BEVAN’S BURGLAR.
Good Dr. Bevan’s burglar came,
And woke him from his beauty sleep,
And took (it really was a shame)
His pretty rings and cash to keep.
Not robbery,—oh, no, for see
The visitor explains with qualms,
’Tis charity compulsory—
Benevolence by force of alms !
* * * «
. One very hot day, a few weeks back, a
sickly 60-foot whale looked up the inhabitants of
Newcastle (N.S.W.), and, with very little cere-
mony, dumped itself down on Stockton beach.
Some well-wishing fishermen, with their eyes to
business, helped it further inland, then went away
to borrow a boiler, and ascertain the state of the
“ile” market. Whilst they were looking for a
suitable boiler, the whale, unfortunately, went
mouldy in its “in’ards.” Attracted by the odor,
hundreds of Newcastleites hurried over to see
what was smelling, and crowded 23-deep around
the decomposing mass. Just here, some thought-
less lady-spectator (she wasn’t the only one—by a
hundred) poked the carcase in its mouldiest part
with her parasol; the result was that the very
much dead amphibian went off bang, showering a
large mass of undefinable matter over the whole of
the spectators. When the fishermen came back
with a boiler, the lady with the parasol was miss-
ing, as well as 30 per cent, of their sperm whale,
nearly the whole of the latter being carried away
on the spectators’ clothes or scattered about the
beach. At latest, the whale (what’s left of it) is
dispensing fragrance with a great deal more than
usual vigor, and everything within a mile of it is
beginning to wear a worried look.
* * * *
The law, as laid down in N.S.W. Divorce
Court by Judge G. B. Simpson, is fast reducing
legal practitioners to a state bordering on im-
becility. No one (save Simpson J.) now knows
anything of the divorce law. Once it was possible
for a lawyer to advise a suitor as to his legal
position—now it cannot possibly be done. For
example, what advice can be given to a man re the
adultery of his wife when this judge rules, that if
a man allows his wife to remain at home after she
commits adultery he condones the offence and can
get no relief ; while if he turns her out, he is
“ throwing her into the arms of her paramour,”
and so, being the cause of her further wrongdoing
he cannot get relief then either. This is a fair
specimen of the new Simpson divorce law.
Eucla, about the most lone-
some little city in Australia, has
started a paper. It is reckoned
to be the smallest place own-
ing a paper on this earth.
Eucla is the border town be-
tween S. A. and Westralia.
Going westward, 420 miles of
red dust ends at Norseman—the
first town in that direction, if
lone, lorn, ghostly Norseman
can be called a town. East-
ward 550 miles of brownish
dust ends in the cheerful so-
ciety of the South Australian
salt lakes. Going due north
you can dodge right across
Australia and not strike a
habitation, and on the south
there is the hilarious society of
the great Australian Bight and
the affable South Pole. There
are 29 men in Eucla, four women,
seven children, and a dog or two.
The town exists because two pro-
vincial telegraph systems meet
there, and because, in the ab-
sence of Federation, it isn’t legal
to put 10 tons of pig-iron in a
dray and haul it over the 900
miles of futile dust that separate
the two nearest settlements with-
out paying duty at Eucla in
passing. The only amusement
Eucla had till the new paper ap-
peared consisted of fishing its
blown-avvay hat out of the Great
Australian Bight. So the young
Australiansof the local telegraph
department sent to the nearest
metropolis for the needful type
and a hand-press, and took to
publishing the Eucla Recorder.
The staff isn’t paid ; it turns out
its little paper gratis in the
many spare hours when it isn’t
fishing its hat out of the Bight.
There is very little advertising;
if a man does chance to lose his
dog at Eucla he knows that the
animal has run after a bear out
into the wilderness and lost it-
self. And, as Eucla only gets
its mail from Albany by schoo-
ner once a month, it isn’t worth
while for Mark Foy to put a
big standing advt. in the new organ. So the
Recorder travels on its merits, and exists mainly
to show what difficulties the Archimedean lever
can contend with and survive.
* * * *
Vic. Chief Justice Madden knows a lot.
And he lets ’em know that he knows. Startles
’em out of their sleep of ignorance, and makes
’em sit up and scratch their ears in wonderment.
He did it the other day, when, in the course of a
public deliverance, he remarked that, 2400 years
ago, a number of Spaniards settled in Cornwall.
If he had said 2000 years ago, there might have
been implied an idea of generalisation. But he
said 2400, and that at once indicated that he knew
all about it to a day. Which puzzled a Cornish-
man in the audience, and led him to remark that
men and manners must have changed since those
times. “ Because, yaw know,” he observed,
“ when tha straanger coomes dawn ta Carnwall,
Carnishmen want ta knock un deown, an’ fettle
un’s head wi a brick, or gie un a kick in tha ribs,
yaw knaw.” But it was pointed out that, at the
period of which the Chief Justice had spoken, the
Cornish were barbarians. At which Cousin Jack
brightened up. “Ah see,” said he ; “ta be sure.
An’ we be Christians neow tu. Ta be sure. It
maks a heap o’ difference.”

Hamilton, member for Bendigo in Vic.
Assembly, wants the Minister of Education to
have inserted in the School Paper—a publication
intended solely for the young—colored nightmares
of eancered and tuberculous flesh, so that the
children will know diseased meat when they see
it. Why not go one better and send the real
article around as an object-lesson ? Then when it
is a blazing hot day in the galvanised school-
house, and the senior class in diseased meat can’t
stay awake, and has to be kept in along with the

second class in unwholesome steak has to be caned

And supposing Mrs. Mulligan assaults the teacher
for whacking little Patrick because he didn’t
know his microbes, will Victoria ever be able to
take its State-school system seriously again ?
Extract from letter by Wesleyan parson
visiting Melb. Benevolent Asylum :
I beg to inform the writer that the late Mrs. Smith was
a member of the Wesleyan Church, BUT being of an
extreme old age, and a colonist of 88 years, and of a
pleasant and grateful disposition, she endeared herself to
everybody. It was the delight of ministers and people of
all persuasions to visit her.
“ BUT ! ” Truth is often more humorous than
fiction.

“ C.M.G,” : Saw an interesting mill in S.
Melbourne t’other day. It began with two dogs
accompanying two very dirty small boys. The
dogs started fighting over a bone, and the boys
began fighting over the dogs. A fair man darted
out of a house near, and cuffed one boy ; a dark
man darted from a house opposite, and cuffed the
other boy. Then the fair man struck the dark
man, and the pair “ went at it.” The dark man’s
wife presently joined in with a pot-stick, but ere
she could offer her lord material assistance, the
other man’s wife was in her back hair, and for two
or three minutes the four sets fought like devils
in the dust. The battle ended in a great triumph
for dark man, family and dog, as the fair man
soon broke away and fled, and his wife and son
and dog followed at the earliest opportunity.

“Lemuria ” : Sydney Public Library is a
perfect godsend to the young woman who has go
too well-known or hard-up to strdll round the
drapery places and make the attendants haul
down everything, and then buy a reel of cotton.
Watched one such float in yesterday with a huge
note-book, and two ocher tomes in a carrying-
strap. She sank languidly down, rang the bell,
and requested the attendant to bring her three
huge volumes that made the man stagger. She
looked at the title pages, and then, when he had
gone, drew out a “ Princess Novelette,” read that
for ten minutes, and then rang the bell again:
“ Take these away,” she said, weariedly, “and
bring me—let me see—yes, the 3rd, 7th, 11th and
12th volumes of the ‘Enclipedier Britanniker’.”
A BROKEN WEB
A spider floated a silken thread
In the grey of a misty morn
To fetter a rose to a rosebud red,
A bloom to a bloom forlorn.
The dews brought diamond gifts to leave,
The winds of the dawn crooned by,
And the spider toiled with a heart to weave
A web that would fill the sky.
The sun leaned out of the lifting mist
And laughed to the silver threads,
And wherever his passionate lips had kissed
Burned beautiful blues and reds.
But the maiden came with a sunny face
And a butterfly-net in her hand,
And shattered the web in her reckless race—
Too happy to understand.
And she danced away to the garden-door
With the wreck of her hands unseen,
But a rose will swing to a rose no more
With a silver chain between.
The rose is our garden ; each rose a heart,
And the sunlight your dear grey eyes ;
And Love’s are the fetters you burst apart
For your lovers—the butterflies !
WILL H. OGILVIE.
IN AN OLD GARDEN.
This is a shrine that held one sweet saint only ;
Now that she’s gone, with light and loneliness,
Sad is the garden, and the rosewalk lonely,
Gone the laburnum’s drooping golden tress.
Thou who was spirit of this sunny pleasaunce,
Breathe on ine with the lilac’s faint perfume ;
Come ! make the garden gladder with thy presence;
Come ! move, a sunbeam, thro’ the orchard-gloom.
She will not come ! Night’s shadow creeps and crosses
The opal splendors of September skies;
Heedless of me, and earthly loves and losses,
She walks the garden of her Paradise.
M.L.
DORA WILCOX.

The printer’s ink is scarcely dry on the
description of the Warrnambool suicide, where a
man feared to cut down the hanging citizen lest
he should be prosecuted under that strange, imagi-
nary statute, or legal bunyip, which never had
any existence ; and another case of the same sort
happens right in the city of Melb. A despondent
Dane swung himself from a beam in the bath-
room, and was found a few seconds later by a
super-sensitive person who sent for the police to
cut the man down, and did nothing vehemently
until a big copper arrived, to find (of course) that
the hangee was quite dead. This person also
didn’t drop the suicide on the floor because he was
afraid of being severely punished for interfering
with the body before the police arrived. Nobody
seems able to trace the origin of that strange de-
lusion about the sanctity of anything human that
is found bobbing at the end of a rope. Probably
it goes right back to the days when the law hung
people promiscuously on trees and elsewhere, and
it was inadvisable to interfere with the late sus-
pended till one found out that he was a suicide,
and not an execution.

Evidently Melbourne is developing an
indestructible of brain. The other day a
man visited the local hospital in a desultory, un-
obtrusive sort of way, waiting around casually for
his turn, and than asking advice re a “ bit of a
tap on the head.” The doctors examined the “ bit
of a tap,” and found a depressed compound frac-
ture of the skull with much splintered bone. Yet
the bit of a tap had happened to the recipient 60
hours earlier, and meanwhile he didn’t think it
mattered, till his wife found a depression in his
head large enough to contain a medium breakfast
egg-
The prosecution of Norton on a charge of
shooting at Meagher in Pitt-street, Sydney, failed
through the attempt to prove too' much. The
charge was shooting with intent to do grievous
bodily smash, which is a crime that carries very
heavy punishment, if proved. Also it is very hard
to prove. A man who is wildly excited and par-
tially irresponsible through unexpected wreckage
of his head, may shoot without really knowing
what he is doing or what his own intent is. The
f ac k that Norton’s bullet didn’t go anywhere near
the bulky ex-solicitor, who was then running for
his natural life up the street and presenting a
splendid target, but, only hit the pavement, was
a P olnt m Norton’s favor; the jury probably
reckoned that a man with any very definite intent
would have got nearer his enemy than that. If
Norton could have been surgically opened inline-
dlately after the episode, and the intention,
which might then be still in him, taken out and pub

When Lord Canterbury’s game was played
out, as Governor of Victoria at ,£lO,OOO a year, he
wanted to give his paymasters something to
remember him by. Thinking hard over this
matter, he remembered an old armchair at ’Ome,
in which his noble grandfather had sat for 50
years in the British Parliament. When he got to
England he shipped this reverend relic to the
Victorian Govt. The Vic. Govt, wasn’t taking
old chairs with traditions at the time. So the
furniture was put away in the cellars of Parlia-
ment House. A Queenseliff barber heard about
this, and wrote the other day to the Premier,
offering to buy the historical chair for a pound.
Turner didn’t entertain the offer. Yet they still
call him a financial genius, and a whale at raising
money !
* * * *
In every Australasian province, as well as
in every other English-speaking community, the
law has a certain kindly feeling for the brutal
assault and bash person. As a curious instance
thereof : In Wellington (M.L.), lately, five
hoodlums considerably battered a young woman—
said to be a nocturnal cruiser of the Flossie per-
suasion, though that fact doesn’t seem to have
been any too clear. They were fined 405., and the
bench added that, if the battered lady had been
“ respectable,” it would have taken quite a serious
view of the case, and sent them to gaol. But,
supposing these five ghouls had forged a cheque
or done a big embezzlement. Would the law have
taken into account the fact that the person who
lost the money bore a bad character, and miti-
gated the sentence accordingly? Not much !
Would it even have allowed the bad character of
the robbed individual to be put in as evidence for
the defence? Not in all the creation of cats ! The
law’s capacity for finding excuses for the rowdy
hustle-and-stoush brigade is simply illimitable,
and the only possible explanation is that the
average administrator of the law is a brute, though
he probably doesn’t know it, with an unconscious
sympathy for brutality.

One of the Melb. pious workhouses notifies
that “no diet is given which fires the passions.”
Referring not to alcohol, but meat. Immortal
Bumble !

Names of certain high and influential
Q.N.B. overdrafters were suppressed during the
recent trial on the ground that it was unwise to
“pander to public curiosity.” The other day a
man went into a Brisbane pub., and tore two
leaves out of Bung’s ledger. Twelve months was
the sentence awarded for this summary method of
“wiping out an overdraft.” Name in all the
papers, of course. When was any common name
like Billjones ever suppressed for fear of pander-
ing?
Though the late James Tyson was the boss
misogynist of Australia, his nephew namesake was
the direct cause of the firmer ri vetting of the mar-
riage-bonds in New South Wales. His celebrated
divorce-case of 1891 created a scare that the
majority of most marriages were invalid through
lack of “ declarations ” or “affirmations.” Parsons
and priests had been lax in requiring or in keeping
said declarations, and so the Legislature passed a
short, retrospective Act validating any weddings
where they had been omitted.
* * • *
One recent morning Gisborne (M.L.) was
struck by eight earthquakes, and four earth-
quakes struck the peaceful little town of Napier ;
also a number of usually tranquil volcanoes
started to fizzle. Maoriland is a tremulous and
tottery country at all times. Some day it will
probably be struck by a bigger earthquake than
usual, and then a town or two will be missing,
and the result will be mentioned in the Respect-
able press as a horrible evidence of the results of
socialistic legislation.

“Did she look innocent? Rather. She
looked as if butter would have congealed in her
mouth. ” —Lance Lenton.
YE DAMSEL DEBONAIR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In morning glow I see her trip
The garden path adown,
I mark the merry twinkling feet
That flash beneath her gown ;
The roses watch with envious eyne
And whisper low “ Beware !
She comes to cull that heart of thine—
This Damsel debonair ! ”
There sings a bird within my breast,
A bird upon the tree,
And both are big with charm fed song—
What may this magic be ?
Why should a murm’rous melodit
Make fragrant all the air,
Because her silv’ry laugh is heard—
My Damsel debonair !
There hangs a dewdrop on the spray,
A glitt’ring silver ball,
And, weary of his dainty dance, ’
He fain would droop and fall;
She comes—and lo ! the cunning wight
Hath found a pillow rare,
For he hath kissed her gentle cheek
—The Damsel debonair !
And many a morn when skies are dim
And mists are ling’ring low,
When down the dell I wend my way
With moody step and slow,
I hear a sound of tripping feet,
And lo ! the Day is fair,
And every warbler pipes to greet
My Damsel debonair!
