A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 13, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
To see society eating potatoes out of soup plates,
and digging its own fork into pickle bottles with
the naturalness of a cow-boy, gives away the Vere
de Vere legends that surround our nicest people.
The lunch-room at Rosehill races last Saturday
revealed a few sad, plain truths anent the manners
of Potts’s Point. For instance, I sat opposite a
gilded hopeful in marvellously correct stripes,
and, fascinated, followed his knife along a circui-
tous track from his plate to the butter, then away
to the bread, then an interval in his own mouth,
then off on a skirmish with a round of beef, into
the butter again, into the salt, a plunge into a
potato, another interval behind the waxed mous-
tache, finally a gracious hewing of the beef for
a society maiden. Really, it’s quite cruel of Rose-
hill to put Potts’s Point in the way of such inevit-
able revelations. Half-a-dozen extra waiters
might save a score of reputations. As it is, the
lunch is the sort of thing a farmer flings before his
ploughboys in a bam.
Sartorially, everybody was put into the shade by
the large heroine of a cause celbbre, gowned in a
braided coat and skirt of light dirt color, and
capped with a toque of appropriate azure. This
large person sported a miniature dogcart, thereby
enhancing her romantic value in a mysterious way
peculiar to this particular vehicle. Miss Char-
lotte Osborne’s smart white dress, well ventilated
with val. stripes, was one of the prettiest frocks on
the not-too-festive lawn. With her was Miss
Irene Marks, in fawn cloth, with red roses against
her sunny hair. Another noticeable garbing was
the ivy-green muslin of a dentist’s wife, strewn
with life-sized roses, after the manner of the recent
race frock of a well-known Melbourne beauty.
Also, a youthful dandy mustn’t be omitted from
my dress par, although I couldn’t see anything of
him when he put his stick in his mouth.
This week things are very quiet, nothing giddier
than school break-ups and head masters’ and
missuses’.speeches being provided. I went to the
Boys’ Brigade entertainment on Saturday night,
but found the evening suffering from the big
exodus to Bowral and thereabouts. By the way,
the Victoria Hall, situated upstairs in the New
Markets, isn’t the pleasantest place to reach after
dark on Saturday. The swarms of Saturday-
nigh ters that blacken the streets in that vicinity,
and throng in and out of the Markets, appear to
have a prejudice against bare-headed ladies in
white opera cloaks, and cutting sarcasms are
hurled at you as you wait in the middle of the
crowd for the lift that leads to the hall.
The chief part in Saturday’s entertainment was
played so persistently by a wicked echo that I
could not hear a word from any speaker on the
Elatform except Laurence Campbell. There must
e something very wrong with the acoustics of a
hall which won’t even allow you to hear that the
next item is going to.be omitted. The most satis-
factory items were those which had nothing to say
or sing, such as George Taylor’s clever lightning
sketches, and three telling tableaux, “The New
Mamma,” “Britannia,” and that one about Faith,
Hope and Charity, arranged by Mrs. Playfair.
The engagement of that pretty young heiress,
Miss Olive Flood, to Mr. Harley Hixon, brother of
Mesdames Geoff and Jim Fairfax, is the latest
summer fixture. Rumor is also busy reporting
and denying alternately the coming marriage of
our cleverest soubrette to a fashionably world-
worn Sydney man. And a very aesthetic affair
indeed is the engagement of a society soprano to
the very newest thing in baritones.
Popular John Bennett’s benefit came off a week
or so back, and ought to have been a booming
success financially. At one time Mr. Bennett was
as well known in this city’s theatrical circles as the
only Williamson, and was particularly noted for a
huge benefit which he worked up for the poor
Jews. I didn’t notice a Hebrew element at his
return benefit, however. A full Kerristian dress-
circle heartily enjoyed the Tivoli’s forbidden fruit,
but what a cruel disappointment is the Dazzler,
Miss Florrie West. She turned out to be a heavy
lady in a baby’s bonnet, with a raspy voice and not
even the average amount of loaded skittishness. I
tried hard to be dazzled, but there wasn’t one single
spark from her singing or dancing that a red-
haired person like myself need have evaded.
A young amateur, Miss Mercy Murray, opened
the programme with the “Hunchback,” and
played the coquettish cousin with a brainy ease
and understanding that would have been startling
even in a pro. Some weeks ago Miss Murray,
who is a graduate of Sydney University, gave a
dramatic recital which I wasn’t able to attend, but
in view of the one promising glimpse which I had
of her capabilities I would like you to hear what
the carping, critical A.N.R. of the Wilson Barrett
controversy has to say about the recital.
I went to the entertainment with the misgiving which
one usually feels on these occasions ; I have heard the
usual young lady recite before. My forebodings were
quickly removed. I know nothing of Miss Murray or of
any of her friends and therefore I write without prejudice.
In spite of the faint praise of the morning papers I expect
great things. I remember the debut of Miss Essie Jenyns,
and have seen no one here who gives greater promise
than Miss Murray. Moreover she has good looks, grace
ot figure, and of movement, and a voice which, if wanting
in power at present, is sympathetic and has a good range.
There can be no question about her dramatic force. From
the one example given, her comedy promises equal
excellence with her tragedy ; and some of her work was
fairly astonishing, when one considers the unfavorable
conditions for purely dramatic work of a simple platform
and a first appearance. Of course she has some of the
faults which always accompany inexperience. She leans
backward at an apparently dangerous angle, and she
sometimes hurries dialogue into monologue. But I am
confident that she will “ arrive ” very shortly, and that
when she has arrived she will go very far.
“ Harbor Lights ” is still booming away at Her
Majesty’s. Lilian Wheeler, whose advance has
been particularly rapid, plays Dave’s sweetheart in
a gold wig, and is unique in looking prettier
through the glasses than to the naked eye.
Nothing very smart adorns either Miss Wheeler
or Miss Ferrar, but the piece doesn’t depend on
dressing, which is almost an inevitable adjunct to
the dialogue play. Ada Ferrar, in terra-cotta
cloth, tight-fitting and plain, dresses her fair hair
low on her neck with much success. By the way,
nobody wears her hair in the same way two days
running nowadays. A wicked uncertainty as to
the correct spot where the knob should nestle
keeps women in a perpetual state of change.
Latest London news says that the little pig-tails
you see all over Sydney are absolutely impossible,
and that the greater part of the dressy women are
wearing their knobs on the top of the head, just
a little lower than last season. Only beauties
patronise the coil on the neck, a hint which people
with profiles like a piece of string in a fit would
do well to consider.
I nearly forgot to tell you that the wonderful
white silk wedding-garments were won by a blush-
ing young priest. Fearful agitation at'Manly as
to what he will do with them. One unthinking
person said inspiredly, “ Oh, you can give them to
a nun, ” which made him look more and more hope-
less. As it isn’t an unusual thing for priests to
win raffles, I really think someone ought to advise
the brotherhood not to take tickets for such incon-
venient things as ladies’ trousseaux.
This gloomy office hardly knew itself one night
last week when Mr. L. Hopkins gave a little
private rehearsal of a picture show, interpolated
with light art talk. Later on “Hop’’intends to
take the lecture and the pictures touring, and the
party was chiefly intended to find out-—in the
words of the shopman—what length you require.
Everything went off admirably, the ancient
Egyptian explaining to his mother-in-law that he
had been out to see the Nile rise, and the B.C.
mother-in-law- caustically observing that there
wasn’t any Nile mud on his sandals, being es-
pecially well illustrated. Acetylene lit up the
clever cartoons, and the evening jovially con-
densed itself into coffee and ices.
The dear old motherland seems to give Austra-
lians and other aliens a much better show of earn-
ing their humble crust at their own trade—what-
ever it may be—than does the Sunny South. Lily
Dampier and husband Rolfe are now getting good
engagements regularly in London, and, although
anxious to return to her native land, Lily is
strongly dissuaded from doing so by Papa Dampier
when he writes her, as there is much more money
in the biz at ’Ome than here.
Latest invention for a ladylike self ad: —
Mrs. wishes to thank those who contributed so
largely to the brilliant success achieved at her (whatever-
it was).
I merely re-publish this because I think it’s neat
enough to be imitated.
Perhaps one of the best instances of the “awful
double-entente ” was that which a Sydney lady of
exalted position uttered in perfect good faith to
the late Henry Parkes. This lady headed a depu-
tation to the then Premier, asking for some con-
cession to the Women’s Industry movement, and
to the mingled horror and amusement of her
female companions, prefaced her remarks with
these words: “ You know, Sir Henry, that though
I come to you on behalf of the Women’s Industry,
I only do so because I was asked—not because I
have any sympathy with the movement. I do not
hold with the modern idea of women’s work and
independence. In my opinion, every man who
can afford to marry ought to keep at least two
women ! ”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“I never saw so many pretty girls as on the
last day of the wheel-races. At Flemington dress-
parades, one’s attention is centred on recognised
society leaders —dashing matrons in the thirties
and upwards. M.C.C. skirts, for the most part,
clothe belles from 14 to 22.
“On Saturday white muslins were frequent.
Lady Brassey grows gay and Australian in her
dress tastes. Her Saturday’s gown was vivid
pink, with black lisse sash and hive-shaped
sunshade, while her little girl wore white
muslin over forget - me - not blue. But
the place to see babies in their glory was that
charitable fete, in the Robert Harper grounds, at
Toorak (Sat., 10th Dec.). The ‘naicest’ infants,
with their nurses, always do congregate at garden-
fairs, and ride the donkey and pony regardless of
expense. Janet Lady Clarke, who ‘opened’Satur-
day’s function, was in black with white fixings,
as usual, and one of those big dangling gold medal-
lions, inscribed with facsimile of donor’s hand-
writing. Her youngest daughter and two kilted
step - grandsons hardly got off the merry-go-
round all afternoon, and a two-year-old scion
of the clan was the dressiest juvenile on view.
“ Judge Madden’s recently-married daughter
Sylvia goes home with husband Yalange to his
country place at Brackley, Northamptonshire.
She will have as a near neighbour a nephew of old
man Brassey. Lionel Brassey, a son of one of Uncle
Tom’s younger brothers (now dead) lives at
Charlton Lodge, only a few miles from Valange’s
place. It is a nice part of England, within easy
reach of London, but far enough away to be quite
rural in that ‘ dewy morning breath ’ fashion that
makes the English midlands so delightful.
“ W. J. Lormer, the gassy little man who poses
as the apostle of temperance and virtue in Mel-
bourne, has been telling an audience that he knows
why Hartley Williams was passed over and John
Madden appointed Chief-Justice when Higin-
botham died. The reason is, says Lormer, that
Lady Williams had stated that when she got to
Government House as Acting-Governess, she
would exclude all members of Parliament whose
characters could not bear inspection. He says
that he had this ‘on good authority. ’ Well, his
‘ good authority ’ was no good at all. If Mrs.
Williams said any such thing, she was a very
foolish person to count her disreputable chickens
before they were hatched —before even she knew
that she would have a chance of ‘ sitting on ’ them.
Her husband says she was not such a snob as to
say anything of the sort. The truth is that Shiels,
who made the appointment in question, did so
because (as he has several times explained) he
thought Madden was not only the most brilliant
member of the bar, but the most accomplished
man in the legal profession, on the bench or off it,
and he thought that Madden would be ‘ an orna-
ment ’ to the position.
“‘Old families,’ ‘ancestral homes,’ and what
not are distressingly common in Victoi’ia nowa-
days. After Rupertswood, the most would-be
venerable estate is the one where the son and heir
was sprinkled not so long ago midst bonfire-blaz-
ing and beer-drinking by the tenantry. The
infant hero was saddled on that occasion with
some high-sounding names, ‘ hereditary bequests
from his mother’s ancestors ’ —heavy swells in the
Stuart period, doncherknow !
“ So surgeon O’Hara’s daughter, Eily, is engaged
to the eldest of the Andrew Rowans, a cousin of
her bosom friends, the Madden girls. The bride-
elect is a large, rollicking colleen, with dark hair
and one of those bright, irregular Irish faces. Her
captive is dark, too, and built on an equally large
scale.
“Another rumored engagement is that of clever
Doctor Syme to a Miss Berry, whose people live
out Camberwell way in a house abutting on the
famous ‘Cole estate.’ The deceased Cole, by the
way, was a nursery gardener in a large way, with
a fancy for land investment, and when he died his
heirs came in for quantities of bush-land, still
known to local residents as ‘ Cole’s paddocks. ’
Another souvenir of the departed was Cole’s ‘bull,’
which for long enough defended the landed pro-
perty of his late owner from juvenile trespassers.
Some of these forest enclosures are still m their
virgin state, owing to a testamentary provision
postponing sale, etc., till coming of age of de-
ceased’s grandchild.
“Re sisters-in-law, that wealthy Mount Mor-
ganaire took his deceased wife’s sister for better
or worse early this month, thus ending the weary
waiting and wondering of local matrimonial pro-
phets. Reports of the smart wedding feast re-
ferred to the juvenile bridesmaids every time as
‘ bride’s nieces. ’ Why not ‘ the bridegroom’s daugh-
ters ’ ? Anyway, the re-edited Benedict gave his
chicks ‘daisy-pendants’ of pearl and turquoise.
Another sister-in-law’s small boy acted as ‘ page ’
and got ‘gowan’ links to match the pendants.
The bride’s name is Daisy, hence the design of the
wedding trinkets. All her sisters, too, came in
for handsome souvenirs of the auspicious event
from the jubilant bridegroom. So they should,
for their square-headedness in holding on to a
good thing when once they’d got it.
“ H. H. Champion, having disposed of his not
too successful venture in sassiety journalism, put
his head into the noose of matrimony on Thurs-
day last. The bride, Miss Elsie Goldstein, took
the vows in her father’s drawing-room in St. Kilda
in a plain short cotton frock, without any of the
consolations of orange-blossom or wired gardenia.
Dr. Strong did the needful. Heretic or not he
manages to tie the knot quite satisfactorily for
many of the culchawed outside his own fold. The
household pot will still be kept boiling with the
aid of the Age, whose columns have been greatly
brightened by H.H.C.’s writing.
“A Melbourne reporter saw an eminent politician
from a Western province in Collins-street lately
and informed his office, suggesting that the
eminent one might possibly be interviewed on the
political and federal situation. ‘Was he alone?’
asked the experienced editor. ‘ No, he had a lady
with him.’ ‘Let him alone then,’ replied the
editor, who knows the politician well; ‘it wouldn’t
be fair to mention the fact that he is here.’
“ Mrs. Brough still looks just as nice off the
stage as on. In fact, her natural color manages to
assert itself when away from the glare of the foot-
lights. On the boards she is pallid, and appar-
ently prefers to dispense almost entirely with
make-up. These hot Saturday mornings down
street she looks cool as cucumbers in snowy linen
skirt, white blouse with scarlet pin-spots, and red
hat. Miss Temple, who helps her to shop some-
times, dresses quietly as a Sunday-school teacher,
and affects unworldly costume touches, such as
skirt drooping a mite below belt, etc.
“ Even when they were playing ‘ Lord and Lady
Algy, ’ most of the women didn’t strike one as des-
perately tidy. Mrs. Brough’s own things looked
self-respecting and accustomed to hang up o’
nights. Her fancy costume of ruby velvet, with
three-cornered hat and silver-headed whip, made
her sister masqueraders seem third-rate and down-
tailed, though Italia Conti, as the philandering
wife, was pretty enough.
“Considering a comedy isn’t a burlesque, that
fancy-ball scene in ‘ Lord and Lady Algy ’ was
too farcical. Hardly a character present was sane
enough for a nervous partner. Lord Algy was
drunk; his jockey pal, as ‘ Young Pretender,’ was
dumb to all outward seeming, and besides, had
forgotten his garters. An archaic marquis skipped
like a young kid of Lebanon ; and that jealous
maniac in the mask would, in real life, have
started a wild panic, first glance. The fat mother-
in-law in shepherdess dress, lugging round a toy
lamb, and pleading vainly to be suppered and
danced with, was pathetic. Other frights, frumps
and caricatures rambled aimlessly through the
bone-boiler’s halls.
“ One ungallant Paddy, at all events, lives in
the person of cross-patch parson Rentoul. In his
recent oratorical onslaught on lone spinsters, this
gentle shepherd endorsed that Scotch definition of
a ‘ Miss’ —‘ a woman with nothin’ to do, and who
hasna’ got a man.’
“ Next, the speaker ran amuck of ‘ women to be
found on the block.’ He ‘can stand the young
ones,’ it seems, but not ‘the middle-aged ones,
who try to look young, with hair, teeth and com-
plexion not their own. ’
“Misguided mannikin! Would he like them
any better if they went about bald and toothless ?
These sympathetic references to lorn females, fol-
lowed by a sharp dig at nuns and priests, led up
to praise of the Wesleyan sisterhood. And it is
little wonder that his unchivalrous reverence com-
mends those winsome Methodist sisters. With
their demure bonnets and blue gossamer haloes
they might do blessed work, indeed, as they trip
in and out of city police-courts, only for their
proneness to matrimony. Men who know no
better ask why all women don’t wear those duckv
deaconess bonnets. That’s easy. They wouldn’t
suit everyone. But they look nice on the average
wearer, ’cause the blessed call only comes to her
whose face goes well with the bonnet.
“ Simple Simons, prattling of the alleged ‘dress-
economy’ practised by natty nurses and sich,
blame society butterflies for spoiling their matri-
monial chances by palpable extravagance. Which
is sheer ignorance. The pretty Puritan doesn’t
look dainty on the cheap any more than the smart
sinner. Some of the bonniest girls in Melb. are
to be found on the Homeopathic Hospital nursing-
staff, and their fixings of white-and-lavender make
ideal settings for beauty. But let the man who
yearns to enshrine a pinnied saint, ‘ ’cause she’ll
make such an economical wifie,’ take careful note
of the saint’s laundry-bill before he does it. Then
there are the belt-buckles, sleeve-links, etc.,
donated by ‘grateful patients.’ Would-be hubby,
as permanent substitute for ‘grateful patient,’
must count the cost of such items.
“Take her altogether, the worldly woman is as
cheap a line as most. Even the gruesomeness of
a real nun comes expensive, as many a pa knows to
his cost. Not long ago, a devout R.C. family in a
Melb. suburb spent some hundreds of pounds on
festivities in honor of their daughter’s ‘ Heavenly
bridal.’ The vestal’s costly nuptial robes went to
the Church ; so did her long hair. All non-con-
ventual guests at the marriage-feast took a honey-
moon trip to a mountain resort at the expense of
the ‘ bride’s ’ parents.
“Mrs. Palmer’s concert panned out well, the
beneficiary profiting to the extent of £250 clear.
Maggie Stirling’s will be the next benefit. She
goes 'Ome next year for extra voice-culture. With
an invalid husband and young children depending

“It is wonderful how the plain, squat, stodgy
truth will sometimes peep out under the cloak of
loyalty. Sculptor Bertram Mackennal is doing a
statue of Her Gracious for Ballarat, and the
Argus reports that the sculptor, ‘while preserving
the likeness, has struck a nappy medium in im-
parting a due dignity to the figure.’ ‘Paint me
warts and all,’ was the injunction of honest
Oliver Cromwell to the painter. But the flatterer
of Queen Victoria has to ‘ strike a happy
medium ’ in order to give dignity to her figure.
It must require a lot of happy medium too.

somewhere in Bourke-street, _ Melbourne. The
soulful are indiscriminately invited by weekly
advertisement.
Y ours affectionately, Gouli-Gouli.
“Onlooker”: A few momentous fights
out of the long list of Sunday battles : Tours, 732
A.D., saved Europe from the Moslems. Started
on a Sunday, and lasted right on till following
Sunday. Hastings, 1066, known to most people.
The Armada fight also lasted from Sunday to
Sunday. And last, Waterloo.
Artesian boring in the far north of South
Australia has been set-back by the effects of the
water from Kopperamanna bore. The supply is
good, but instead of enriching the Lutheran
mission station, which it serves, the water, which
comes up 4000 ft., is so heavily charged with
mineral that it kills vegetation and petrifies most
things with which it remains in contact.

(N.S.W.), left home on a visit, and was not after-
wards heard of. The skeleton lately found under

supposed to be his.

Matthew Davies has been elected to the
committee of Vie. Law Institute. TheLawlnsti-

A. H. Landseer, father of S.A. Assembly,
who has sat continuously for Mount Barker since
1875, is so ill that it is unlikely he will ever
appear in Parliament again. He has had two
strokes of paralysis within the last few months,
and he is 70 years old. Landseer is the beloved
of the Hansard reporters, because he can always
be written-up as he speaks ; and, in the whole
of his long political career, he has never once
complained of being misreported.
“ Cornstalk ” : A well-known Mel-
bourne litterateur once told me his
version of Purves’s success as an advo-
cate in jury cases. “ The fact is, l’urves
is a most consummate actor,” said my
friend. “If you watch him closely you
will observe that he always ‘makes an
effect ’ upon entering the court, or does
something very soon which attracts the
attention of every soul there. He em-
ploys facial as well as ejaculatory com-
ments upon the proceedings as they go
on. He sneers, laughs, frowns, pretends
impatience. He interrupts and inter-
jects. When he examines or crossex-
amines—well, you know what he is then.
The result is, he becomes the protagonist
of the trial. All eyes watch him.
Whenever anything happens, or the
evidence or trial take an unexpected, or
even an expected, turn, everyone looks
to see the effect upon Purves. Whatever
the trial may be, whoever the Judge may
be, whoever the prisoner may be, the
person who, sooner or later, becomes the
central and commanding figure in court
is Purves. He overshadows the bench,
the bar, and the dock. He is, as I have
said, a consummate actor, and, in more than one
sense, a powerful hypnotist.”
Another idyll withered. A brother-in-
law of the deceased Mrs. Keightley states that
the lady did not ride that weary sixty miles into
Bathurst for her husband’s ransom as alleged in
“ Boldrewood’s ” romance. Also, that she did not
ride back with the historical £5OO. Point of fact,
she was very prosaically driven to her father’s
house at Blackdown by Doctor Peachey, where
she remained, the doctor and another man carry-
ing the ransom back. What a slump in romance !
Mr. G. P. Murray, appointed the other
day senior P.M. of Brisbane, was lieutenant of
native police in the bad old Q. “ dispersal ” days.
He was suspected at one time of being one of the
parties who carried on the anonymous newspaper
war which contributed to the final extinction of
the then malodorous black-trooper system. Mur-
ray, with his black regiment, established a fortified
camp on the edge of a scrub in Capricornian
country, whence he used to sally north and west
when duty called. The site of that camp and its
surroundings is now Rockhampton.
Stated that when Hori Grey went to M.L.
a Maori woman said she recognised in him her
long-lost son. Grey’s power with the natives is
alleged to be a consequence of the belief that
he was a reincarnated Maori.
TYSON.
So narrow was his groove that he,
Contained within this tiny tomb,
Will wish no more society,
Nor feel a lack of elbow room—
For all his life, to make and save,
He spent pent in as small a grave.

The late Constable Hoey, or-
derley to the now-departed Mel-
bourne coroner, Youl, had a deep-
rooted contempt for “side.” One
day he had a beautiful row with a
potentate who tried to sit on Hoey
as a mere policeman. “ Look here,
me bhoi,” said Hooey, “ don’t get
on the high horse because you’ve
got a few halfpence. Yer not dead
yet. I’ve seen many a one that
thought almost as much of himself
as you do, and had a bank-account
as fat as a bull-pup; but I’ve squirted
the last tbruppenny-bit out of his
weskit-pocket in the morgue with
the hose! ”
“ Dick ” Thatcher was once
got on a lovely string in a M.L.
town. He had lampooned nearly
everyone in the place, and it was
decided to announce a dinner to
Dick and the presentation to him of
a gold watch, as a “ public benefac-
tor.” Tickets were £1 Is. each, and
everybody rolled up. Mayor in
chair, and Dick in full dress. The
chairman made an appropriate
speech, and handed Thatcher a mo-
rocco case. Dick made suitable
acknowledgment, and sat down, un-
suspecting. Then someone called
out “Put it on, Dick,” and Dick,
opening the case, found a penny toy
watch. He looked round for a mo-
ment, exclaimed “ Sold, by — — ! ” and joined in
a roaring laugh at his own expense. Then began
the slaughter of the “marines!”

Late Melb. Coroner Youl
used to tell how, when his
father was parson at Launceston
(Tas.), in the early part of the
century the congregation was
summoned to service by the
striking with amalletof an iron
barrel s wung to a post. When
the parson made his periodical
visits from George Town he
used to announce his advent
by walking through Launces-
ton in full canonicals. One
day a youngster ran in and
told his mother that a man was
in the street in his night-shirt.
Rev. Youl used to officiate in
the courthouse, and sometimes
there would be men or women
outside in the stocks.
£. £. Smith, Melb.’s
advertising medico, relates how
he once (probably desiring to
investigate the ways of other
people in the healing business)
disguised himself in a flowing
beard and specs and visited a
medical clairvoyant. To the
latter he described “symptoms
akin to the above.” The rival
at once assured him that the
case was a very bad one—so
bad that he was the only man
who could possibly cure it;
and then he grewjwarm and
said that such a case in the
hands of a rascally impostor
like £. £. Smith always ended
fatally. Also he went into a
trance and got spirits to cor-
roborate his statements. Just
here Smith quietly deposited
his hair and spectacles on the
table, and smiled on his collapsed rival. “ I
won’t pay you this time, old man,” he said affably,
“but I’ll tell you what I’ll do. When you want
some advice, come to me and I’ll treat you for
nothing.”
W. R. Merry, Victoria’s boss litigant,
turned up in court the other day to show cause
why he shouldn’t be made insolvent over an unpaid
bill of costs. Merry was the contractor for a
Victorian railway some time just subsequent to
the lamented dampness mentioned in Genesis,
and certain claims of his connected therewith have
raged unsuccessfully in the Vic. courts off and on
till no man now living can quite remember the
beginning of them. The old man was a born
fighter - one of those who would spend half-a-
million in pursuing all over Australia if he
thought he had a right to the and he dropped
a big fortune one way and another into the hinder
pocket of the law.
The lees of the Norton-Meagher fracas
seem to have settled—at some considerable cost
to N.S.W. of men in wigs and blue uniforms hired
to arrest the combatants, or to try them, or to
prevent them from calling each other unpleasant
names in a court-room. Meagher, for assailing
little Norton with a whip, was fined £5. Norton,
on a charge of firing his pistol in the direction of
Meagher’s running legs, was acquitted—a sympa-
thetic upshot to his downshot. Norton seems to
have been acquitted so many times in the last few
months, on so many charges, that the business of
chronicling details grows wearisome. The Bul-
letin respectfully suggests to the Crown law
authorities that it would be cheaper to charge him
with all the crimes in the calendar in a lump,
and acquit him once and permanently. Then
Attorney-General Want could give free play to
his enthusiasm for Federation.
Walter William Begg Bentley, having won
a libel-action in Brisbane, in which he conducted
his own case, proceeded on an electioneering-tour.
He is “ stumping ” the Carnarvon electorate, with
a view to displacing Home-Secretary Fox ton at
the coming general election.
“Old Jack”: Story told of late N.S.W.
Attorney Rodd. A man in the Lands Office was
bothered by a money lender named Aaron. Went
at last to Rodd for advice and help. The lawyer
heard him through ; then he said : “Pity ! He’s
been here and instructed me to prosecute you.
I’m Aaron’s Rodd.”
THUMPER, 292 not out (W.S.W . v. Tasmania).
At Campbelltown, N.S.W., on Nov. 21,
Michael Goulding died, aged 83 years. He had
lived at Campbelltown for nearly 50 years, having
left Ireland “in a hurry” in 1848 after the arrest
of Smith O’Brien and others of the Young Ireland
Party. In Campbelltown cemetery are buried
several of the 1798 men, who settled in the district
on arrival.
Johnson, the architect, who died the other
week en route from Groperland, and was buried at
sea, met the fate of which he had a morbid dread.
Said to a friend some time back that he would
have left Perth long ago but for the fear of dying
on the way and being buried in the ocean. Many
men, many minds. It is not long ago that a resi-
dent of Napier (M.L.) devised his money on con-
dition that his heirs should take his body out to
sea and sink it in many fathoms of blue water.
Lormer, the turbulent Melb. J.P., comes
out with a wild yarn to the effect that Justice
Williams lost the Victorian Chief Justiceship be-
cause it was known that when his wife came to be
Acting-Governoress it was her intention to knock
many and various Parliamentarians off the deputy
vice-regal visiting list. Said Parliamentarians
heard of it, and influenced all they knew to upset
Williams’ chances. Justice Williams, being inter-
viewed on the subject, declares in the name of his
wife that the yarn is a devilish fable. Lormer is
likely to be heard from again ; he is that sort.
Only one thing is certain, and that is that a story
attaches to the manner of Madden’s pipping Wil-
liams on the post, which story has yet to be told.

Re par. (26/11<’98) as to death of mine-manager Bibby
in Straits Settlements. No such news has yet reached

refers must he •‘Bill ” Bibby, eldest son of Raub manager;

Aus. G.M. Co.’s shares, about S3BTO-100 (s3B)—shilling

should merit a name other than “ unfortunate ” or

The sad case of editor Winspear is clamant
for sympathy. The unfortunate fellow, as is well-
known, found journalism on behalf of the
humanity that had lost its birthright only a slow
albeit certain mode of suicide. In desperation he
turned his attention to some sort of derelict trade
in s -cond-hand goods. Among the contents of
his shop the Sydney police found certain things
which represented portion of the “loot”.of a
burgled house, or something of the sort, and
Winspear was sentenced to a term of imprison-
ment. His poor wife, maddened by the disgrace,
thereupon hanged herself,.leaving seven children
to the tender mercies of a world that had proved
too hard for their parents. The Bulletin knows
nothing of the facts of the police-case which re-
sulted in Winspear’s imprisonment; it only knows
of Winspear as a clever, foolishly-generous enthu-
siast, zealous to the verge of madness on behalf of
the needy and the oppressed. His children
should not be allowed to starve.
The tail-end of a left-off rumor about
the exploded Rougemont still occasionally comes
this way. Lately the London Chronicle inter-
viewed him about his alleged identity with Grin,
and the weary, wrinkled Crusoe hung on by his
last tooth as follows :
“ I have a double. The double is Grin. It was he who
had the diving apparatus, the experiment with which
failed. I only took it on afterwards.”
“He is like you '! ”
“ Exactly alike. His own wife would hardly know one
from the other. It is the more extraordinary because he
is really a younger man than I am. I have frequently
used bis name.”
“ Can you mention an occasion when you were both
present at the same place at the same time ? ”
“Certainly I can. On the boat, when the diving
apparatus was tried. By the way, that diving apparatus
did not cause a man’s death. He had a diseased spine ;
he would have died if he had jumped off a chair. Yes, I
and Grin were both there. I was using his name at the
time, and we were exactly alike.”
Which suggests a very awkward situation. If
Rougemont and Grin were in the same boat, and
concerned with the same diving apparatus, and
they looked exactly alike, and Rougemont was
calling himself Grin, and Grin was calling himself
Grin, anybody might become confused.

Aboriginalities.
When the railway was first “ laid on ” to
Condobolin (N.S.W.), the preliminary trips were
rendered exciting by a grizzled old cocky and his
four sons, who, armed—the old man with an
ancient muzzle-loader and the boys with stones—
used to lie in wait for the train and bombard it
as it passed. One theory was that they took it
for a new species of wild animal, and were deter-
mined to defend their hearth and home till death.
But probably it was only their way of expressing
exultation, tempered with beer, at the progress of

An eccentric gentleman was Spondulix,
accountant of the Mucki branch of the Mullock
and Mud Bank. Spon. was no end of a pistol-
shot, and every night ere turning in he used to try
Miss HELENE BURDETTE (Lyceum Theatre, Sydney).
Photo., Talma, Melbourne.
a few shots out of the bank revolver at a cigarette
advt. stuck up on the wall. Growler lived next
door, and Spon.’s bullets used-to go through the
weatherboard wall of his (Spon.’s) domicile. Just
as Growler would be dozing, a bullet would—
plunk !—flatten against his brick wall. Somehow,
all Spon.’s most trifling movements seemed to be
known to the district inspector, and Spon. gave
Growler credit for carrying tales, so decided to
try him. Spon. prepared a fishing-line by cutting
all the strands bar one, and invited Growler
to a night’s fishing in the river. They went,
and at the first cast Spon.’s line carried away
and he lost his sinker and hooks. A new
hook was snoozed on, but a second sinker could
not be found, so Spon. pulled a bunch of keys out
of his pocket and fastened them on for a sinker,
“ Don’t be a damned fool,” said Growler. “ Sup-
pose yon lose them, a nice fix you’ll be in.” “I’ll
chance it,” said Spon., and out they flew halfway
across the river. The line did carry away—at
another place where Spon. had nearly cut it
through—and then he bound Growler down by
sundry oaths not to tell a soul how he had lost the
bank keyS- Next day, up came the district
inspector, post-haste, with the duplicate keys in
his pocket, and asked Spon. to open the safe and
allow him to count the cash, and his jaw dropped
when Spon. strolled over and opened the safe. Of
course, it was a useless old bunch of keys that was
lying at the bottom of the river, and Growler was
badly bowled out.

“Yang Anyut ” : Re
immunity from snake-
poisoning (B. 12/11/’98):
Have never known im-
mune person, but have
heard of many claims to
that effect. Three times
have seen snake charmer
allow snakes (cobra, uler
blian, and cobra de ca-
pello) to bite him, but
found that in two cases
the poison-bags had been
removed, and that in third
they were empty. In last
case I permitted snake to
bite me, without ill effect,
but when three days later
I allowed same snake to
bite a fowl, it died inside
three minutes ! Since have
been bitten more than
once by so-called deadly
snakes and have resorted
to whisky, and plenty of
it. Again, I am not af-
fected even bycontactwith
freshly-cut bark of the
runyas (fabled upas-tree—
Antiaris toxicaria) though
most men are affected with
slight fever, accompanied
by blister-like rash upon
coming close to a newly-
felled runr/as. Have known
carpenters exhibit severe
symptoms of poisoning on
cutting up rungas wood,
even after tree had lain
over two years seasoning.
From this tree is obtained
the deadly sumpitan ar-
row - poison, to which I
and perhaps 20 natives
whom I have known were
quite immune, though very many are so in less
degree.

“Asmodeus”: At South Creek, near Mul-
grave, a “green” fisher landed a big flathead.
He had never seen one of the species before, and
looked at it askance as it writhed at his feet,
Asked old hand, adjacent, to diagnose forbidding
thing. Old hand classified it as a “ water-
gohanna very dangerous to handle. Undertook
to remove and destroy it. Did so accordingly—in
his own frying-pan.

‘‘ B. 1. : Recently watched goanna tackle
crows’ nest. Old crows made desperate resist-
ance, and eventually pulled the reptile out by his
tail, when he fell to the ground, and I settled
him. Inside him I found three young nearly-
fledged crows.

“ C.C. ” : The katipo spider is not the only
venomous creature in Maoriland. The hideous-
looking weta which bores holes in the Makomako
shrub and lays its eggs there is quite as poisonous.
The Maoris are more scared of it than of the
katipo. Once saw a young pakeha chase a Maori
with one he had lassooed. The Maori fled, yelling
“ Taipo, taipo” ; met another Maori, who likewise
fled. Joker kept going till he had the whole hapu
in flight. But he had to make a hurried exit that
night, with his tent burning behind him.
• * * *
Some years before the discovery of tin on
the East Coast, Tas., there came to the hut of
one Daniels, a selector, a tramp named Edwards
looking for work. Daniels put him on, and he
became so essential to his boss—and to Mrs.
Daniels—that he was still there when the news of
the brothers Krushka’s find burst over the dis-
trict. Shortly afterwards Daniels’ section was
found to contain tin, and he was at length pre-
vailed upon to part for a good price to a tin mag-
nate who paid him the money in hard cash. This
was the last ever seen of Daniels. Edwards, who
reported the departure of the couple to the main-
land, followed soon after, alone. Then trouble
arose as to the boundaries of Daniels’ old lot.
The surveyor was dead, and the only man who
could throw a light on the matter was Daniels.
Enquiries were set on foot, and at length Ed-
wards and Mrs. Daniels were discovered. The
latter declared that she and her husband had
quarrelled, and that she had not heard of him for
12 months. The search broke down at that.
Edwards was one of those who wrote their names
on their billies in a bad mining accident on the
mainland, and whose bodies were found by the
rescue-party. About this time, writer, who was
on the spot, was shown a skeleton unearthed close
to the site of Daniels’ old hut. The bones were
those of a small human : and Daniels was a small
man. Now, who could tell the best yarn—Ed-
wards’ coffined remains, the skeleton, or Mrs.
Daniels ?
• • • •
S.A. still has its ostrich farm. J. H. Angas
is one of the biggest and most interested share-
holders in the proprietary co. The number of
birds is now 560 ; the annual expenditure runs to
just over £lOOO, and in the last half-year the profit
was below <£loo—but it was a bad season. The
little industry has been running nearly a score of
years.
Bulloo Downs and Careary, the two big
Q’land cattle stations whose stock are best known
down South, have listened to the complaint of the
tanners, and brand now the one on the thigh
and the other on the forearm. The question is
being seriously considered whether in these times
it is worth branding at all. Many American
ranches don’t.
' A black-tracker on one of the Northern
.N.S.W. rivers died recently, and the body, after
being coffined by the police, was handed over to
his tribesmen for burial. About a week after, a
po 1 iceman met one of these and asked if they had
buried Tommy all right. “Oh, yes, boss.
We bin bury ’im all right. Wrapum ’im up
a’onga blanket an’ put ’im in ground. My
word, that fella box make um good boat
duck-shootin’.”
“ Melb. Townsman”: I think
we need an Act of Parliament to
prohibit publication of weather
prophecies by aboriginal
blacks and other bush autho-
rities. Sixorsevenmonths
ago I bought a new
macintosh and a pair
of very weighty
boots on the nn-
derstanding
that the ants
in the Victo-
rian wilds had
started crawl-
ing up the trees
to escape
floods. It was
to be the flood-
iest winter on
record, yet it
went near to
endinginpray-
erforrain. And
later, ere the
gold was off
the wattle, &c.,
we had voices from the interior proclaiming
that the snakes had crawled forth in their
thousands to give notice of a phenomenally
early and hot summer. Whereupon I invested in
a job lot of white linen rairnent, which is thus far
a ghastly mockery of the average temperature. If
people up country have any belief in the wisdom
of ants and snakes, let ’em keep it to themselves,
I say. Seems to me that the insects and reptiles
are imposing on their ignorance.
• * * *
Cootamundra (N.S.W.) still talks of Gov.
Carington’s never-to-be-forgotten visit. One of
the district magnates—we’ll say Kerfupps—living
out of town arrived in Coota. rather late, and had
just time to rush into an hotel for a bath and
hurry down to meet H.E. Whilst in the bath
some wag stole his socks, and Kerfupps, rather
than risk missing the show, pulled on his boots
without his “Matildas.” The Gov. was duly met,
and in his usual off-hand style was “ shouting ”
freely at Solomon’s Hotel, when Kerfupps went
to the door, and seeing several of the townies out-
side cried, “Come inside, boys, he’s not at all
particular who he shakes hands with.” The
“boys” went inside and after a few drinks a
couple of them started an argument. “I tell you
he don’t,” said one. “ Oh, rot! ” said the other.
“ Well, I’ll lay you drinks all round,” said No. 1,
“hedon’t.” “ Wager,” replied No. 2 ; and turn-
ing to Kerfupps he said, “ Look here, Kerfupps,
the fact is, this man says you never wear socks,
and I’ve bet him drinks you do. So, just flash
’em and let’s have the liquor. ” And before the
victim could reply he had dashed at Kerfupps and
pulled up the leg of his trousers, showing the bare
pelt. Ah 1 old Coota. was a live town in those
days.
* * • *
“ 0.P.” : About time somebody spoke up
against the revolting ram-operation known as
“crushing.” Like speying cows, I think the law
prohibits “ crushing ” ; but, for all that, it is still
largely practised in N.S.W. When rams get too
old for stud-purposes, instead of being killed and
thrown to the—ugh !—pigs, they are “ crushed ”
and turned out to recover and fatten. Then they
are killed and sent to the men’s huts, where that
awful creature —the modern station-hand —eats
them without a whimper.
* * * *
“G. 8.” : Re ’roo’s tail touching ground :
followed track of one last Sunday, on a road with
nearly 3in. dust on it, for a mile-and-half.
Measured his jump (10ft. Sin. on average), and his
tail only touched the ground in two places in all
that distance.
* * * •
“ J. 5.8.” : Re reptilian life-tenacity. Once
stiffened black snake and threw him into hollow
stump. Nearly five months later looked in stump,
expecting only to see skeleton, but, to my astonish-
ment, the snake looked as good as new, and, when
poked with a stick, began to wriggle about. Also,
once shot away hinder portion—including both
hind legs—of a goanna, and, six weeks afterwards,
he gave evidence, when disturbed, of being still
on “this side” by dragging what remained of
him along by fore legs, although the ants had
cleaned the flesh off much of that.
* * * *
“ R.S.” : Re Australian blacks’ powers of
mimicry. Once saw a young gin imitating a
buffalo drinking. Going down on all fours, she
imitated the burying of the huge head almost to
the eyes, the occasional stamp of a forefoot, the
throwing the water back on the belly first with
one foot then the other, the turning to lick the
shoulders, the scratching of the ribs with the hind
foot, and, lastly and most wonderful of all,
she gradually swelled herself out till she was like
a huge pumpkin, and had to give in, laughing.
* * * *
The bush dandy is an interesting study.
Writer knew one who always carried a small flat-
iron among his belongings, wherewith he carefully
did up his shirt-front on festive occasions.

A squatter, noted for closefistedness and
prayer, went out recently to pass a Chow neigh-
bor's work, and found numbers of trees untouched.
“ O, dat fella no matter,” said the Chinky, “me
worky welly fast, no see him.” “ All right, John,
when I pay you, I count very fast—one, two,
three, four, five, six, seven penny one shilling,
eh ! —you savee?” “No, no, no. Me savee you
dam logue.”
THE BUSH TWIST.
’Tis strange, but surely shackled I must be,
For though I strive to fix me, yet in vain ;
Full twenty times I’ve passed one standing tree,
And never once have known that tree again !
Gray grassy flats that sweep from granite hill
And drowsy brooks I never viewed before,
All seem familar to my mind until
Unwittingly I come to them once more.
I hear my fainting heart go pit-a-pat,
I’m dizzy with the never-ceasing strain
To separate the dim-lit places that
Go swimming in a whirlpool thro’ my brain.
Tho’ I survey the landscape, view the sun,
And carefully by these my bearings take,
’Tis strange my course is ever over-run
With duplicates of objects in my wake.
At last I shout with all my failing force,
And soon an answering shout comes ringing back :
Anon a dusky damsel on a horse
Puts me upon an old familiar traok.
I follow it the way she tells me to,
Convinced the other is the right way still,
And marvel much to see when safely thro’,
The creek reversed, and running up the hill I
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
“LACHLAN-JACK.”
Axemen ? Gippslanders and Tassies ?
Sonny mine, you make me smile !
To those chopping- 11 guns ” you mention
“ Lachlan-Jack” can give a mile !
What was that you whispered, sonny?
Where the devil were you dried !
Never heard of “Jack the Axeman,”
Over on the Lachlan-side ?
Jack was bred on Weddin’-Mountains,
And from Grenfell out to Bourke,
To him nought can hold the candle
When it comes to timber-work 1
Gidya, myall, box and jarrah,
Ironbark or mountain-gum !
He goes through ’em like a saw-mill,
And he downs ’em as they come 1
Saw him chopping for a wager —
lie has not been beat as yet—
It was raining cats and dogs, and
We were getting soaking wet.
But Tie swung his seven-pounder,
And he caused the chips to fly
In such lightning-like succession
That they kept him snug and dry 1
N.Q.

WATSON’S WHISKY.


Most of those start-
ling feats of fence-climb-
ing which rabbits are
credited with are per-
formed by does about to
become mothers ; and it
is pretty clear that they
do these things in order
to get out of the way of
the other sex. A down-
south pastoralist lately
told a Parliamentary
Commission that he had
actually seen does drop
part of their litter while
attempting to struggle
over a 4ft. netting.

“ S.S.”: Once, When
harvesting at Cardigan,
near Ballarat, had for
hut - mate a long, thin
German, who extempo-
rised a mattress from a
couple of wheat - sacks
sewn together at the
mouths and stuffed with
hay. Herman stuffed
them himself in the
morning. During the
night I was frequently
awakened by my mate
cursing pathetically in
guttural German. “Dot
vos a tarn pad ped,” he
said in the morning. “ I
haff to alter heim, he vos
like he am stuffed mit
rope.” But Herman did
not alter his bed, and on
the following night I
rested as badly as before,
and Herman rolled about
and breathed bad lan-
guage. “I alter me dot
ped dis time, sure
enough,” said Herman
when he turned out in
the morning; but he
had to be reminded of
it later, and then he
ripped up the sacks, and tumbled the hay on the
floor, and two dispirited four-foot black snakes
flopped out, and meandered for the door. I slept
peacefully through the following night. Herman
was in hospital, suffering from shock.

“6xB” discourses on derivations :
I agree that ringer derives from the old phrase, “ rings
round him”— i.e., easily beating an opponent at sport or
battle: E.g., “ Samuels could run rings round Malone.”
A ringer is by no means the fastest shearer on the board.
Very often there ar! several who can beat the ringer for
a given time. He is the most constant and hardest
worker, and puts out the most sheep during the season,
and, of course, gets the biggest cheque. The cheque
decides the ringer.
Drummer is not necessarily the slowest shearer in the
shed. He’s more often the laziest, and gets the least
money on pay-day. I don’t think the position of drums
in brass-bands (“ Mungundi’s ” suggestion) has anything
to do with this term. In olden days (old shed habits and
practices are fast dying out) the man with the smallest
amount of sheep to his credit on cut-out day used to be
drummed out of the shed by the rouseabouts.
Cobbler need not be the last or any other sheep in a
pen. By cobbler shearers mean a dirty, sticky or matted
and wrinkly sheep—one that is hard to shear. Two men
catching out; of one pen naturally avoid bad sheep—i.e.,
cobblei-s—as long as possible ; and pens not being refilled
until the last sheep has been caught the last sheep is
more often a cobbler than any other. Some pen-mates—
especially shearers of even speed-don’t select, but take
as they come. In their pens cobblers are generally the

or straggler-shearing. The pack-saddle is called the

“Bogan ” : The accepted belief that black
duck always builds on ground is wrong. Shooting
recently out Nyngan way, saw black duck flutter
out of hole in big box-tree. Climbed tree 8 or
9 feet, and found nest with 10 eggs. .Not wood-
duck or widgeon—real black duck.

Will Ogilvie’s charming volume of verse,
“ Fair Girls and Gray Horses,” is now ready. In
case of any local difficulty or delay in supply, the
best plan is to order direct from The Bulletin,
enclosing 4s. 6d. in stamps or postal notes ; and
get the book by return post.
Dear Bulletin. —Some folks in "Victoria
are very wrath with little Ben Tillett because he
has said in England that the poor old folks in our
Benevolent Asylums are very much worse treated
than the poor in English workhouses. Well, the
present writer, who has been over a good many
English workhouses and is able to make com-
parisons, says that Tillett is perfectly light.
Remember the English workhouse of to-day, under
strict Government inspection, is very different
from the workhouse of the Dickens period. The
arrogant, patronising, purse-proud insolence of
the benevolent persons who talk about their
generous treatment of the poor in Victoria, makes
one feel ill. Tillett knew what he was talking
about, and he spake the simple truth. A
“ pauper ” in an English workhouse is infinitely
better off than an “ inmate ” in, say, the Mel-
bourne Benevolent Asylum.— Bumble.
Fruit in season. In summer she provides
her meat; in winter, finds her store complete.
This is the position the thrifty housewife finds
herself when she takes advantage of the bountiful
supply of fruit we are going to have this season.
M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, of Sydney and Mel-
bourne, have anticipated the season and are now
landing their stock of specially-manufactured
fruit-jars. Lightning, Mason, Chicago, Perfec-
tion, &c-., &c., all guaranteed and air-tight. Jam-
jars all sizes and kinds.*
Tyson items from Toowoomba Chronicle :
A Toowoomba gentleman suddenly asked the question,
“ Look here, Mr. Tyson, do you believe you were destined
to be a millionaire—that God intended you to be one?”
“ I believe that God plans everything,” was the calm
reply, and, after a few moments’ silence, he continued, in
an indescribable tone, “ I firmly believe that I, James
Tyson, have a mission from Providence ; that I was sent
upon this earth for a special purpose. I believe I have a
special mission from Providence to improve properties on
the earth of this land of ours, and to make them produc-
tive. AH my life has been devoted to that object."
“ But, when you are dead, what then of your properties
and you: wealth?” queried his friend. “I have no fear
on that score,” replied Mr. Tyson. “ When lam gone,
God will fix matters up.”
Predestined to “ improve properties ” ! And—
in the Toowoomba Chronicle of April 2, 1887,
appeared the following advertisement on behalf of
Tyson:—
PRACTICAL DOMESTICATION.
A person who does not wish his name or his residence
to be known, is so impressed with the idea that the
traininsr of young women of the present day is not such
as to fit them for the position of good housewives and
mothers, is willing to give two prizes—first prize, £5O ;
second, £2s—to be competed for by the young women of
the Darling Downs district. The qualification to be aa
follows
1. Age, from 15 to 21 years.
2. Figure strong, well-grown, healthy, with good
carriage.
3. To be able to read English well aloud, to write a
decent letter according to grammatical rules, and per-
form on some musical instrument or sing moderately
well.
4. To be able to cut out and make their own dresses
and underclothing ; dress ,to be neat, no artificial means
(such as pads, steel hoops, high-heeled boots, &0., &c.)
will be allowed.
MUST DIET HIM.
PRINCESS BANG-BANG (to her royal brother, who
is feeding the fatted calf): "Ugh! Don't
give him pine-apple. You know how the pater
hates pine-apple. If he tastes it in the
preacher on the great feast-day your head
won’t be worth a cocoanut.”
5. To be able to cook a good meal and also prepare food
for a sick room.
6. A thorough knowledge of all household duties (and
likely to establish her own home in after life).
This prize-girl competition was the fun of the dis-
trict. The fun chiefly came in over article 2
“ figure strong, well-grown, healthy, with food
carriage—beg pardon, good carriage.” A dozen
girls were ranged up like show-pigs for in-
spection ; and how, asked the wicked wits,
was the decision reached ? Obviously, you
couldn’t guarantee a girl’s figure with her
clothes on; and So much ridicule was
poured on Tyson’s brilliant idea that the competi-
tion, which was intended to be an annual affair,
was promptly ended. And Tyson retired in a
huff from the field of “ practical domestication.”
Amongst the many amusing reminiscences
of Leon Caron, whose genial countenance adorns
this issue, is one to the effect that once, when con-
ducting an Emelie Melville operatic season at
Brisbane, he made a bet with since-deceased tenor
Harding that he, Caron, would appear at the
theatre that night without his beard. “In the
afternoon, accordingly, I had the beard removed,
and then secluded myself in my room till theatre-
time arrived. When I presented myself as usual
at the stage-door, however, I was refused admis-
sion—‘ No strangers allowed behind, sir’; and it
wasn’t till I assured the. janitor that I had a very
important message for Miss Melville that he let
me pass. By this time the musicians were seated
in the orchestra, and when I took my place at the
conductor’s-stand, they were obviously wondering
who the devil this was. Nor did they guess until
the middle of the overture, which, through the
librarian forgetting to put my conduct score on
my stand, 1 had to conduct by memory. Then
they knew it must be me, because I knew the
opera by heart ; and they began to laugh so much
that they nearly collapse.', dropping their instru-
ments one after the other ; while on the stage
Miss Melville, who had just made her first en-
trance, was suddenly convulsed with laughter at
the sight of me. Then everybody joined in, and
the performance had to come to a stand-still until
company and audience had their laugh out.”
Probably the most beautiful collection of
diaries for 1899 is that issued by Messrs. Collins
Brothers and co., of 69 York-street, Sydney.
They are of about two dozen varieties, ranging
from the plain, cheap, useful, and enormous
down to dainty little things in beautiful binding
for the individual whose diary is a luxury. They
are in all colors, as well as in all sizes, bound in
every possible style, gilt-edged and otherwise,
plain and fancy and miscellaneous. Whoever
wishes to keep a diary for his sins, so that he can
look back in astonishment at the end of the year,
and discover what kind of girl he was engaged
to at the beginning, should try one of Collins’
manufacture.*
Be female chemists, talked of in late issue.
A London Queen, lately to hand, urges dispensing
as an occupation to which women should look for
more than average success. Lightness of touch,
love of order, and a few other things are men-
tioned as all in woman’s favor in this calling. It
is added that, though so far as can be learned
only three females hold big dispensing billets in
the kingdom, many uncertificated country doctors’
wives make up hubbies’ prescriptions.
“ The Sunlight Year Book for 1899 ” is to
hand, with rather less information than an ency-
clopaedia, and ever so many hints, tips, and
notions about all manner of interesting subjects.
The volume would be still more useful if it were
deliberately constructed for Australian readers,
instead of for English.

figurements. Sulpholine developes a lovely skin.

POLITICAL
POINTS
It is reckoned that every qualified old per-
son in Maoriland who has taken the trouble to
send in his claim, will enter on his pension on Ist
January, 1899.
Whispers are afloat that William M‘Millan
is likely to be the next N.S.W. Agent-General.
At any rate he is wanted out of the way by the
Reid Government.
W. H. Groom, the patriarch of Q. As-
sembly, has promises equal to a majority of votes
assuring him of the Speaker’s Chair in next Par-
liament, vice Cowley, the detested.
Premier Reid, of N.S.W., says “ he would
like to bring in an Old Age Pensions Bill next
session if he has sufficient revenue.” But he
never will have sufficient revenue unless he pro-
vides in the Bill for raising it. The revenue won’t
rush at him.
S.A. Democrats endeavor to put it into
the heart of Premier Kingston to secure elective
Ministries. It would, they urge, renew King-
ston’s own long lease of power, and Holder’s, and
probably Butler’s ; but Jenkins and O’Loughlin
would probably go, and Tom Playford would step
in.
The Kingston (S.A.) Govt, goes on acquir-
ing good blocks of country for close settlement.
Mt. Browne, near Wilmington, one of the most
fruitful districts of the northern agricultural area,
has just been bought for £2 ss. per acre ; and now
the Government is negotiating for Koonoona, near
Farrell’s Flat, 70 miles out from Adelaide, on the
North line.
In drowsy December they divide S.A. As-
sembly members into the Asleeps and the Awakes.
As, for instance: Asleep—Holder, Jenkins, Butler,
Copley, Randell, Coneybeer, and Roberts.
Awake —Kingston, O’Loghlin, Glynn, Batchelor,
Downer, Brooker, and Solomon. But, for all
that, some members don’t, sleep half enough for
the progress of reform.
It becomes more glaring every day that
the cry of the Dickson faction for next elections
will be “a comprehensive railway policy ” and a
loan for a few millions. This would just suit Oily
Jimmy, who helped to engineer the great finan-
cial drunk of ten millions through Q. Assembly in
’B4. A big loan is the only thing in politics Oily
James knows much about.
After years of persistent agitation, a com-
bination of the Croydon News, the local member,
and “ the white party ” in the Mongrelian Parlia-
ment has at last succeeded in replacing the Chinese
curator of Croydon courthouse by a white woman.
If it requires such herculean effort to shift one
miserable Chow, Scalyland is doomed to one day
become part of the reconstructed Chinese Empire.
The Vic. police, whose proposed rise of
fid. a day nearly upset the Turner Govt., have a
minimum wage of 425. per week and quarters, or
48s. without quarters. They are the only public
officers whose regular (or irregular) increases
haven’t been stopped in the recent hard times, and
the only ones who get pensions, the State supply-
ing seven-eighths of the pension fund. It is a joy-
less billet to be a drunk-catcher on these terms,
yet the Yic. policeman hasn’t suffered quite so
much as other branches of the public service at the
hands of the hard-driving Turner.
Political Labor organisation at Broken
Hill (N.S.W.) was, a few years back, the envy of
most industrial centres ; but, unfortunately, from
a variety of causes it has latterly gone to pieces.
After getting the four Assembly seats in the
hollow of its hand and seriously threatening
Speaker Abbott in Wentworth, it began to get
hold of the municipal council as the seats fell
vacant. But there it started to call different parts
of itself an ass and ask it to come outside ; and
now the municipal Labor-party.consists of just two
members, and one of them has been dodging a
libel-writ or something which a former member
has wanted to serve.
N.S.W. Leg. Council demands as its little
amendment that the Federal capital shall be in
Sydney, which is going a step further than the
Assembly—the Assembly only demanded that it
should be in N.S.W. Still, both Houses are quite
solid that the capital must be in N.S.W. ; they
are both grabbing with the same mean, hungry,
selfish grab to get all the profits of union for
“our” province. Even the Labor-party, which
believes so much in the rule of the majority, sees
nothing remarkable in the idea of 1,300,000 people
of N.S.W. fixing the capital to suit themselves,
and about 2,300,000 people in the other five pro-
vinces having no say in the matter at all.
Indications that Premier Kingston is be-
coming pious and orthodox accumulate every day.
At two recent elections the Govt, nominees were
anti-tote men and rabid teetotallers, and advo-
cated policies of the Sunday-school variety. Has-
lam, defeated for the Leg. Council, is a local
preacher who would close up every public-house
and compel weary citizens to drink nothing but
sad, brown tea, and generally is such a gloomsome
person that the electors would sooner have elected
a hearse. In the recent mayoral contest in
Adelaide the contest was between beer and piety,
the pious candidate having the Govt, support.
Many of Kingston’s still faithful supporters, who
loved him for his failings in the good old days, are
tearfully asking if he contemplates joining the
Church.
Democratic Maoriland has
in places strange blue streaks of
Toryism. It still imprisons
people for debt: when the credi-
tor is a Shining Light, with a
church reputation to preserve,
he doesn’t get his debtor im-
prisoned directly, but sells his
claim to a collecting-agency, and
if it can’t get its money it jugs
defendant as a warning to the
next one. Premier Seddon
lately proposed that no order
for the imprisonment of a debtor
should be made by any amateur
J.P., or be made where plain-
tiff had bought the claim from
the original owner thereof. This
would at least have the advan-
tage of making the Shining
Light persecute his debtor in
person and take the obloquy
himself—and the Lower House,
by a majority of two, refused
even to allow the Bill to be in-
troduced !! It was the loudest
and most sudden bump Seddon
has received up to date.
Maoriland started the com-
pulsory purchase by the State
of big properties for closer settle-
ment purposes in 92, when
=£50,000 a year was set apart for
the purpose. By ’94 it was so
gratified with the result that it
increased the vote to .£250,000
a vear. In ’97 it felt so much
more gratified that it raised the
vote agai n—to .£500,000 a year.

ter Best’s land - resumption

volutionary horror just the

John Downer solemnly told the S.A.
Assembly the other day that he does not intend
to stand again for Parliament. The general im-
pression is, however, that he is angling for a big
requisition which shall induce him to change his
mind. He played the same game over the Federal
Convention election two years ago, and the scheme
worked very well. Nesbit, Q.C., will in any case
run for Downer’s district at Barossa.
G. D. Carter, a most awful failure as vie.
Treasurer, and one of those supercilious people
who, by dint of impudence and a turn for satire,
get the reputation of possessing exceptional intel-
lect, opposes the Immigration Restriction Act.
Says that while the Empire is trying to force an
“open door” in China, it will make us ridiculous
to close our door against Chinks and other aliens.
And he also says that to do so will be acting in
violation of the laws of nations and the laws of
God. Australia doesn’t accept whisky-merchant
Carter as an exponent of the laws of God.
The S.A. Immigration Prohibition Bill has
a clause to prevent the entrance of ladies of the
Flossie persuasion. “ I suppose,” sneered Solo-
mon, “that the Treasurer or the Premier will
examine all women passengers so as to decide who
are virtuous and who are not.” “ Oh, no, ’ cheer-
fully responded Kingston; “we have power to
call in expert evidence.” Mr. Glynn thought it
would be difficult to differentiate. “ Surely, _he
said, “ a few of those natural little indiscretions
which are apt to occur on shipboard, will not be
counted against the lady passengers.” “ Natural
little indiscretions” isn’t a bad way of putting it.

As against the so-called “Natal Act,”
which forms the basis of projected anti alien
legislation, it was objected in the S.A. Assembly
that if some of the local J’s.P. venture beyond the
province they would never get back again. Under
the proposed law, the stranger banging at the
gate may be called on to write correctly at dicta-
tion 50 words of English from a standard author
or from the S.A. statutes. Quite a lot of J’s.P.
couldn’t do that to save their lives.
The stupid Federal amendment of Premier
Reid, providing that the rights of each province as
regards “inland rivers ” should be more clearly
defined, met with a shriek of well-merited derision
in the Council. As all rivers are inland the
Council wanted to know what the Reid Govern-
ment meant, seeing that its amendment seemed
only to refer to some specially inland variety of
river—some kind that is more inland than the
average. And Attorney-General Want could
only say that he didn’t really know what his own
Ministry was driving at.

Dear Bulletin.—They say the Labor-
party has got Premier Reid at its mercy. God
help him ! One of the big guns of that crowd once
invited some admiring friends and constituents to
stay at his suburban residence during their trip to
the “big smoke.” They accepted joyfully, but
left next day, utterly disgusted at the awful
manner in which the great man yelled at his
trembling wife to clean his boots.—Lehuria.
The defeat of Haslam, Govt, nominee, by
squatter Tennant for S.A. Northern Leg. Council
election recently, is believed to sound the death
knell of the Household Suffrage and one or two
other important democratic measures now before
the Upper House. Haslam’s candidature was a
huge mistake from the first. He was a strong
opponent of the totalisator, which is immensely
popular in the north. The annual agricultural
show went bung years ago in S.A. for want of
something agricultural to show in these dry times,
and to abolish the tote would be to deprive the
crow-devourer of his sole remaining joy.
When the N.S.W. atmospheric gas swindle
was exposed, Neild, M.L.A., declined to refund
.£3OO which he had earned by helping to float the
bogus company, and refused to be held liable,
morally or legally, for the losses of others, on the
ground that he had acted as an ordinary broker
in an ordinary business matter. The Illawarra Har-
bor scheme promoters somewhat short-sightedly
secured Mr. Neild to father their little Bill in the
Leg. Assembly, and when the Labor-members
learned that the “Colonel” was interested to a
considerable extent in the company, they thought
they smelt an escape of gas and promptly sat on
the measure.
Dismemberment is not inimical
to vitality. Nature compensates the
legless with extra strength of arms,
the armless with stronger legs.
Therefore, as N.S. Wales Opposition
has had its tail cut off, its head
should be much improved. Not
swelled —Reid has appropriated all
that kind of thing !

Vic. Assembly fully realises how difficult
Turner would be to replace. No other Premier in
Australasia could turn a bad defeat into a majority
of nearly 2 to 1, despite the desertion of some of
his own customary followers, merely by the
announcement that the Government must have a
majority or it would go out. Even the Gillies
party, when it found it had to support Turner or
give its own Duncan a chance to form another
Ministry—well, a good deal of it supported
Turner.
The avowed reason why N.S.W. Labor-
leader M'Gowen “allowed” Premier Reid Id.
duty on tea was that it would improve the quality.
He instanced a case where a cargo of tea was
shipped to Melb., condemned there, and dumped
over to Sydney. But the consigners knew that a
duty was charged on tea in Victoria before they
*sent their loathsome consignment there. And as
they sent it all the same it wasn’t the duty but
the careful inspection which kept the stuff from
getting into the local stomach.
VVith the death of popular Premier Byrnes
the great opportunity of the Q. Labor-party has
arrived. But is the party equal to the occasion .
Most of its members are decent fellows, and fairly
straightforward politically, but not one of them
is capable of stirring up any enthusiasm in the
breasts of the people. Herein there is a mournful
similarity between Govt, and Opposition in
Queensland. Property-owners, however, dont
work on enthusiasm, and don’t particularly need
it, but Democrats can’t afford to dispense with it.
There was a man in Queensland who roused
genuine enthusiasm in the ranks of Labor, but
he, unfortunately, went to Paraguay with a bee
in his bonnet.
Bananaland has a Public Service Board
(cost .£3500 per annum) specially designed to pre-
vent political intervention in civil service appoint-
ments. T’other day Ministers Dickson and
Foxton, trying desperately to escape the odium of
poor old Papa Pinnock’s promotion, laid the blame
on the shoulders of the late Premier Byrnes.
According to Foxton, Mr. Byrnes personally
directed the position to be offered to Mr. Pinnock.
Cry rose at once, “ Where was the Public Service
Board ? ” Ministers wriggled and writhed under
the imputation conveyed in Foxton’s blundering
speech, but the admission had been made that the
Board was only a tool of the Government, and the
public said, with a grin, “ We always thought so.”
Says a Melb. daily of P.M.G. Duffy :
There has been nothing but promises and dilatoriness
for the four years of Mr. Duffy's reign. The public
patience is now about exhausted, and everyone has had
enough of the spirit of exquisite fooling in which the
Postal department has been administered.
Everyone has had more than enough, but The
Bulletin can’t say that that will make any differ-
ence to Duffy. Victoria in general is disgusted at
the mismanagement of the Postal dept., the same
as it was disgusted at the bank scandals —and
there the matter ends. When public patience is
exhausted—well, the public becomes impatient.
The public impatiently sits still and does nothing
suddenly in a great hurry for ever after.
The fatuity of the theorist ! A Mr. Bil-
linghurst read a paper in London recently on
“ Colonial Indebtedness,” and advocated, as a re-
medy, increased immigration. Which would cer-
tainly reduce the national debt as per head, but
at the same time it might just as easily swamp the
country with unemployed, and very likely make
the actual state of affairs worse than it is now.
If Mr. B. sent 10,000,000 immigrants intoN.S.W.,
that jolly and fat province’s indebtedness per head
of population would appear very insignificant in-
deed. Also, it would have to borrow at once about
.£200,000,000 to keep said immigrants in sand-
shifting jobs and similar remunerative employ-
ment. Billinghurst ought to come out as speedily
as possible and run the finances of this benighted
country.
Lieut. Skene is a Vic. soldier who desired
a vacant appointment for which he was technically
qualified. One of the regulations bearing on the
appointment said no applicant was to be more
than 25 years old, and Skene was about 23. Lieut.
Lilley, whom the Little Tin Gods favored, was
27 years old ; and the authorities altered, the
regulation to suit him, and appointed him. Lieut.
Skene had a chance of filling a similar vacancy
until the authorities, having appointed 27-year-
old Lilley, suddenly lowered the age-standard to
22—which killed Skene’s chance altogether.
Skene protested ; and the facts and figures came
out to show what a truly beautiful thing is the
Vic. military service. Now, by way of promoting
peace, it is proposed to put the age standard far
enough up again to let Skene have the appoint-
ment he has otherwise qualified for. It is better
to do that and let him in than to have him making
an unpleasant row outside.
“ Anarchist " to The Bulletin :
“ How many anarchists are there ? ” The answer de-
pends somewhat on definitions. The broad principles of
anarchism are anti-authoritarianism, anti-officialism,
anti-monopolv, anti-commercialism, individual liberty,,
and inter-individual solidarity. But those only who give
these ideas a particular application in a particular social-
political platform are usiTblly called anarchists by them-
selves or others. These have special journals with a
normal aggregate circulation of 100,000, but the papers-
of a country or language have had their circulation quad-
rupled in times of enthusiasm ; and, making allowance
for overlapping and copies bought by the curious, it seems
that the anarchists, so designated, number about a quarter
of a million, or, reckoning their families in, a million.
Other adherents to the principles, without the name and
platform, are several times as numerous. Terrorism-
the creed of “dynamite and dagger”—has no place in
the general philosophy or policy, and is indeed contrary
to strict anarchist notions; nevertheless, t here are occa-
sional terrorists. The theoretical terrorists may number
1000. In Australia, journalistic experiments show that,
with adequate branch publishing arrangements in all the
capitals, a purely anarchist paper would obtain a settled
circulation exceeding 500, hut not exceeding 1000. In 13
years there were three known terrorists, other than police
pimps and amateur fizgigs, and one of these was away
before the third appeared.

dunedin, N.Z., EXPLAINS.
The many old friends of G. H. Wood, Esq.,
ex-J.P., in Queensland and N.S.W., as well as the
newer ones of New Zealand, will read of his good
fortune with interest and pleasure. In a letter
dated Dunedin, 'sth July, ’9B, he says “ I have
much pleasure in bearing testimony to the efficacy
of Warner’s Safe Cure and Safe Pills. Over five
years I had suffered from derangements of the
liver and kidneys, so much so that at times I felt
unfit for business, my whole system being affected,
but more particularly my head. Sometimes I
thought I had congestion of the brain, and then I
was told I had Bright’s disease. Eventually I
found my liver and kidneys were out of order, and
that was the cause of my troubles. I was recom-
mended by a friend to take Warner’s Safe Cure
and Safe Pills. After the first four bottles I felt
as well as ever I did in my life. lam quite confi-
dent of the good effects it has had. I take this
first opportunity of sending an testi-
monial, of which the proprietors of Warners Safe
Cure can make whatever use they choose.”

“N.”: In a Christian line, the U.S. Con-
gress ought to have prayed for Spain during the
war. Praying publicly for a “stricken nation”
after having stricken it is a low-down hypocrisy.
Old James Service intends to retire from
the Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords and
from politics generally. He is 75 years of age,
and has been in Vic. Parliament off-and-on for 41
years. He had a portfolio in Nicholson’s Ministry
in ’59 ; but after that he was nobody in particular
for 24 years till he became Treasurer in Kerferd’s
Cabinet of 1874. Then he was Premier for five
months in 1880, and again for three years—
-1883-86. After that, the intermittent old
gentleman dropped out of the front rank and
held no more portfolios. He used to be called
the best Treasurer his province ever possessed,
but he held the cash-box in pleasant times, when
money was plentiful and it was hardly possible to
be a very bad Treasurer. The fact that he was a
cordial supporter of the Gillies boom policy sug-
gests that he wasn’t much of a financier after all.
btill it should be remembered to his credit that
he twice refused a title, that he once tried to re-
form the Upper House, that he passed the useful
Torrens Act, and that he was largely responsible
for the creation of the Federal Council—which
fell into a state of incapable flop through causes
for which Service wasn’t responsible.

LIGMOLLIENE never fails to CURE

SURPRISING CURES AFTER YEARS
OE SUFFERING.
Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir. —For years past (about ten), I have been
trouble with “Acne” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene ”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salts ” and was in-
clined not to use it; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligmolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY:—
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record pm
gress with regard to my old enemy “ Eczema Capitas,”
from which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treat-
ment for some years under the prescriptions of Pro
fessor M’Call Anderson and other eminent derma
tologists, with only partial and temporary relief
When I met you I commenced somewhat sceptically
vour ointment Ligmolliene, and after about two months’
treatment I may honestly say my enemy is defeated,
and lam perfectly well. I will only add that since I
commenced the treatment of myself I have prescribed it
to others, and am still doing so with brilliant results.
Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours sin-
cerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.
Discovered by the Proprietor,
William Henry Malyon ,
