COUGHS, COLDS on the CHEST,
BRONCHITIS & ASTHMA
Rapidly Cured by
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
inose wno nave taken this medicine are amazed at its
wonderful influence. Sufferers from any form of bron-
chitis, distressing cough, difficulty in breathing, hoarse-
ness, pain or soreness in the chest, experience delightful
and immediate relief, and to those who are subject to
colds on the chest it is invaluable, as it effects a complete
cure. It is most comforting in allaying tickling and irri-
tation m the throat, and giving strength to the voice, and
it never allows a cough or asthma to become chronic. No
house should be without it, as, taken at the beginning, a
dose J s generally sufficient, and a complete cure is certain.
Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a had attack
oi Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough
day and night and great quantity of phlegm. Night
sweats came on and pains in my chest, and I got so weak
that I could not leave my bed. At this stage I was pro-
to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Melb. Bicycle Club —proverbial for the ex-
cellent management of its cycle race meetings—made a
hideous bungle on December 3 over the Austral heats,
and, in consequence, Seharp, who would have possibly
won the big event, was debarred from starting. Accord-
ing to the rules of the Vic. League of Wheelmen, the
handicappers allot the starts and arrange the heats.
Drawing the heats by lot is an arrangement that would
be utterly unworkable in cycling events where pacing is
almost everything, and leaving sports promoters to fix
them up means (in the country, at all events) that
local men are given soft and early heats, while the half-
dozen hottest strangers have to fight out the last heat,
and the winner has to come out for the final without a
rest. As somebody has to be trusted, the League elects
to trust its handicappers.
This time the heats were arranged, and left in
the hand of the M.B.C. secretary by the handicappers, it
being understood that only the four men knew the
arrangement. Then, says the M.8.C., rumors arose
that the bookmakers had got hold of the heat allotments,
and that, as Seharp and Wren Matthews had been placed
against second-raters, the books were laying accordingly.
Satisfied that all was not square, the M. 8.0. thereupon
placed Seharp and Matthews in different heats, on which
the handicappers reported the matter to the league, and
that body demanded an explanation. The club gave
none, but guaranteed it would act legally. The result
was, Seharp came out first for the heat the handicappers
had placed him in, and was ordered off the track. He
obeyed, and later on came out for the heat the M. 8.0.
had placed him in. To the astonishment of all, he was
again chucked.
To explain how the best interests of the sport are
conserved by taking a man’s entrance fees, letting him
train three months, and then calmly handing him back
his money at the last moment, requires a lot of
M.B.C. logic. In the first place, the assertion about
the leakage is a trifle overdrawn, seeing that despite all
the vaunted secrecy of the allotments they appeared in
the Australasian on Thursday morning ; and, secondly,
the M.B.C. behaved in a most lamentable spirit in back-
ing down before the League and making the wholly inno-
cent Seharp the sufferer. It has, so it is pleaded, the
right to return any entrance fee, and legally it would
appear so from the rules, but the moral right to block a
man who has spent pounds in training fees simply to
save a row with the handicappers will meet with little re-
cognition. If the M.B.C. was sure of the position it took
up against the handicappers, it should have started
Seharp as he was placed on the programme and fought
out the question, instead of making a buffer of the unfor-
tunate rider. Anyway, the little business cost the M.B.C.
£3OO damages to Seharp.
Though Seharp would have undoubtedly carried
off the heat (won by Brooker) in which the handicappers
placed him, he would have had a hard fight with Tom
Relph had the programme been carried out. Kelph won
his heat from 90yds., catching the field two full laps from
home, and turning off the two miles in 4.31 2-5. There
were only three faster heats than this, the cracker of
all being 4.30 1-s—not so very much faster—and those
races were won after much harder riding than Relph’s.
Matthews bolted with his heat. Starting from 260, he
left the field further behind him at every pedal’s turn,
and won by a street in 4.37 2-5.
Walne seemed out of form on Deo. 3, his fall in
training having apparently left him with a good deal to
pick up. In the mile-scratch final, he just got home in
front of Barker, while W. M‘Donald, a dark outsider in
black and gold diamonds, reminiscent of Bravo, romped
in by three lengths.
“ Bravo ” M'Donald, who was a Dunolly miner,
like the Middletons, has been in the West for two years,
and evidently picked up speed there, for he made hacks
of acknowledged champions, and brought out applause
which has never been heard on the ground since Megson
won his race in 1894. In his heat Elliott ran him wide,
and he was left fully 30yds. behind when the bell went.
McDonald made it up, though, before half-a-lap was
scored, and finished 10yds. in front of everybody.
The Austral final (Dec. 10) brought out all the
natural weakness of bicycle racing as a sport. Tom Relph
was a 4 to 1 favorite at the start, little Gargurevitch was a
newspaper tip, and “ Newhaven ” Jackson, at 8 to 1, must
have carried £5OO, or more, of public money. For all the
chance that the public got to win their bets by Relph,
Gargurevitch, and Jackson, the three men might have
been jointly and severally as stiff as the proverbial
crutch. They were “ loafing,” in bike-race parlance,
when they ought to have been doing their hardest to
overtake the vanguard. Lap after lap was registered on
the board, and still this fancied trio, together with A. E.
Tame, pedalled along in the hopeless rear. Relph turned
his little cocoa-nut head to the right or left occasionally
as though he were anxious for somebody to take up the
pacing job, but he never gave any other evidence of
desire to reach the front. At last, when the race was
practically lost, Tame went away on his own, and then
the multitude could see how easily Tame’s companions
might have followed suit. But they didn’t, and their
backers were, to all intents and purposes, thrown over.
In excuse for the absurdly feeble performance of
Relph and Jackson (Gargurevitch improved his position
in the last lap) it is said that they rode without judgment,
or they rode with judgment re the hopelessness of
attempting to struggle along unpaced. The public are
left to assume that a couple of experienced riders didn’t
know enough to arrange beforehand for a fair go among
themselves. Also, the public must assume that neither of
them felt capable of an effort to reach the front unpaced,
and that they couldn't have got there had they tried.
The Bulletin will not go into this complicated question.
It will merely say that the term “Newhaven” is a
ghastly satire on the Jackson who rode in the Austral.
The winning Finnegan’s time was only 3-ssec.
faster than the time in which Relph won his heat a week
earlier, on a slower track. It was one second slower than
the race which Kellow won, from the 15-yards mark, two
years ago. There is, then, no getting away from the
argument that Relph, on his latest “form,” ought to
have raced faster. Granting that he owed his defeat to
honest circumstances, Relph’s backers may very well ask
themselves whether the cash results to them would have
been any different if the first favorite, and the 8-to-l-
chance Jackson, had conspired together to go “ cronk.”
Nearly six feet high and weighing 12J stone-
Finnegan has the build as well as the name of a police
man. He is comparatively aged for a racing cyclist,
being 26 years old and a married man, and his pre-
vious record merely consists of two small wins at
Sale, Gippsland, and a second in last year’s Mel-
bourne Druids’ Wheel Race. Finnegan is a native
of Gisbourne, where there is a regular colony from
beyant, and has done a little boxing—of course, for Gis-
bourne racecourse is scene of many a gory encounter
between the spielers of the city and the long-lipped
spud-chasers of the soil.
Finnegan is the same man who had his ear torn
clean off during a fall at the Melb. Exhibition track last
year. The scrap was picked up, sewn on, and is now as
good as new. The man who has his ear ripped off and is
plucky enough to stick to the game deserves a big win.
Who is to swear to “stiffness ” in the riding of
the back-mark men as long as they glance at one another
in apparent expectation of the pacing that doesn’t come
to pass ? Back-mark or front-mark men are said to sacri-
fice their chances to selfishness, if they don’t take it in
turns to help one another along. Draw a distinction
between the outward signs of selfishness and artfulness in
this matter. If you can’t draw a satisfactory distinction,
don't put your coin on any bikist who races inconsistently.
Bill Martin of old managed to be pretty consistent in his
plugging, and Walne doesn’t give his backers much cause
for grumble at any time. As for the others—well, even
an Austral prize is only £240, whereas the betting this
year was so big that any fancied rider might have sold
himself for £lOO.
Sweet betting it was. 6to 1 (4’s at the start)
against Relph, 6 to 1 Finnegan, 7 to 1 Morgan or
Matthews, 8 to 1 Jackson, “ 10 or 15 to 1 the others.”
This for a field of 16 runners, with all the chances of the
pacing game and probabilities of accidents thrown in.
Oddly enough there was noj accident in the Austral, but
the second and third men, Middleton and M’Gibbon,
collided after they had passed the post.
Everyone was glad to see A. B. Crisp, the Tas-
manian (an ex-Austral winner), take the Victoria Mile on
Saturday. Crisp is a plucky, straightforward straggler,
and a gentlemanly visitor who blows his nose into his
handkerchief. Some of the visitors and many of the local
men blew their noses into space, so to speak.
* • • »
Tasmania’s cricket contest with N.S. Wales was
as hollow as an empty hole, but the island team’s, weak-
ness helped to give the public a display of brilliant,
record-breaking batting by N.S.W. men. The Tasmanians
seemed hopelessly overmatched, and could only make 130
in Ist innings, while N.S.W. broke the interprovincial
record with 839—every man but Howell double figures,
and Trumper and Ired'ale 292 and 196 respectively. The
Tasmanian 2nd innings was worth 222, so N.S.W. won by
an innings and 487.
* ♦ * *
The N.S.W. Association might well have copied
the Victorian practice of opposing the Tasmanian visitors
by a “ second team.” Firstly, to even things up more ;
and secondly, to give rising talent a show. The paralys-
ing combination of absolutely the best available team
which met the “Tassies” destroyed all interest in the
match, as a match.
• • » *
Victor Trumper’s 292 (not out) for N.S.W.
against Tasmania was a great performance, but it was
hard lines on the youngster his skipper deciding to alter
the usual routine of his going in first. Had the usual
course been followed, he had a good show of smashing
previous bests. However, he" might have lost his
wicket had he gone in earlier, so perhaps it was all
for the best. Remarks are made as to Trumper’s fresh-
ness at the end of such a heavy innings, but athletes
allege that youngsters of Trumper’s age do not tire like a
set man, if untrained. George Seale and “ Griffo ” never
seemed to tire when boxing in their boyish days, although
untrained, but, when they got on in years, they found it
necessary to train like other men.
* * * *
Coningham had two strokes of bad luck last
week. He lost his place in the representative team by
the inclusion of R. A. Duff, and in the same week a fire
destroyed all his household effects.
• * * *
S.A. puts the same team into the field against
N.S.W. this Friday (16th) as played Victoria, with the
exception of Peters, who gives way for Darling. The
wheatfielders should make a better show ; but, all the
same, the match looks a good thing for N.S.W. Darling
made 70 on a bad wicket against E. Adelaide the other
day, and his batting should make S.A. much stronger.
George Giffen, by the way, has not handled a bat since
the Vic.-S.A. match.
* * * *
Darling has already been chosen as one of the
Australian Eleven selectors. The other two will probably
be Trumble and Gregory.
# # * ♦
The prospect of a good bit of sport filled the
Lyceum Theatre, Sydney, on Saturday night last, when
Harry Dunn and Lucifer met in a wrestling match for an
alleged championship. The attendance was large, and the
show spread towards the small hours of the morning. Lu-
cifer is a contortionist, and when the pair met in the first
event, Grseco-Roman style, he immediately assumed the
defensive by making his original “ split” bridge. Dunn
commenced to worry him like a terrier with a rat, but
the unusual position non-plussed Dunn, and for a solid
hour and a half did that aldermanic personage sweat, and
the audience sorrow, before he got the hang of Lucifer’s
tactics and brought him over on two shoulders.
Most of the time he was busy lifting Lucifer
and dropping him again in sore disgust, and between
times he would stand off and scratch his head
and inspect his enemy. The next spell was catch-as-
catch-can, and as catching hold of the legs is allowed in
this style, it gave Dunn a better opportunity, and he was
not so long in getting the second fall. The next style
was Cumberland, Dunn’s particular love, and the pair had
scarcely got a “ holt ” before Dunn had gained the fall
and the match. The show was disappointing, as Lucifer
knows little of the game and Dunn was out of his element
in the ground-worrying, Graeco-Roman business. The
possibilities in Australia for good men at the game were
shown by the filling of the Lyceum from floor to ceiling ;
and it may be the means of enticing across the ocean
the terrible Turks and gruesome Greeks who are
worrying and strangling each other in the States and dear
old Hingland.
* • * *
In addition to the big crowd at the wrestling
match on Saturday night, the National and Golden Gate
clubs (Syd.) were both well attended. At the former,
Jack M‘Kell and Gaffney fought 15 good rounds. At the
outset, Gaffney brought M'Kell down, but he came again ;
and, as no knock-out blow got home, the lads were both
on their feet at the end of the 15 rounds, Gaffney getting
the decision in his favor. lI.M. navy was well-repre-
sented at the Golden Gate in the amateur tournament.
Mulross banged Rayner, of the Mildura, on the point,
and knocked him out as effectively as a cannon-ball
would have done. Another Mildura man, Taylor, showed
that turkey and champagne, and the other rich food
served out to the private tar, are bad things to train on,
as his condition gave out in the sixth round, and Doran
was declared his victor.
• * * •
Boxers Jim Barron and Herb. M‘Kell, for the
third time in little more than a few months, met at the
National Club, Sydney, on 3rd December, to decide the
lightweight championship of N.S.W., but a more unsatis-
factory display has rarely been witnessed. There was
plenty rough-and-tumble, any amount of harmless clinch-
ing, and a deal of close missing, but no hurt came to
either. The writer made a note on his cuff of the good
blows struck, and a reckoning up at the termination of
proceedings disclosed the big total of four. Barron cried
enough directly the 25th round ended, saying his hand
had gone—he prepared everybody for this by announcing
from the ring, before starting, that that member was
liable to go back on him. It is to be hoped now that
Barron will adhere to his stated intention to retire from
the ring for good, and that M‘Kell will, as announced, go
to South Africa at once.
* * * *
When all Sydney was flocking madly up King-
street to see Sampson chain-snapping, and one of
the main city thoroughfares was being turned into a riot
and a pandemonium, the bell of St. James’ was calling the
righteous to prayers. Apparently the crowds were
scurrying to the bell-clang, and a stranger noting this
remarked he was glad “to find Sydney so godly a city.
It was pleasant to see so many citizens of all sorts hurry-
ing to church in the lunch-hour.”
* * * *
“ Turns ” Cavill has lately been taking most of
Sydney's prominent swimmers down in a novel manner.
Recently, he made a match with “ Sid ” Davis (ex-cham-
pion of" Balmain and a speedy performer), conditions
being that “Tunis” should swim with his legs hobbled
while the other man should have his limbs free. A fiver
aside hinged upon the result, and, after leading all the
way over a 33yds. course, Cavill won by a yard.
* * * *
Boat-sailing is different from every other class
of sport, in the fact that its exponents do not go off with
increasing years. A quarter-of-a-century ago the crack
boat-sailers of Port Jackson were George Montgomery,
George Ellis, George Fletcher, and Tom Colebrook.
Montgomery, the best of them all, is dead ; but the other
two Georges and Colebrook are still the cracks of the har-
bor. George Ellis sailed the Keriki to victory on Satur-
day last as well as he ever handled a boat in his life.
* * * *
It looked like old times on Saturday last when
the Keriki, Vigilant, Wonga, Plover, and Etfie, the crack
22-footers of the harbor, started in a spanking southerly
breeze for their trip round the Manly course, to decide
the championship of the Sydney Flying Squadron. The
Keriki was rigged for the day, and bore out the character
given her when on the stocks by the writer. Keriki and
Vigilant sailed pretty level to Manly, but, when sheets
were flattened down, the former travelled like a witch,
leaving her field hand over fist, and eventually winning
from Vigilant by 4min. 35sec. A big blow and rough sea
will always be found Keriki’s strong point.
* * * *
Though first acceptances have been declared for
the big events to be decided at Randwick (N.S.W.)
Summer meeting, but little straight-out betting has
taken place, backers doubtless remembering the way
they have before been “taken down.’’ The Villiers
Stakes looks well for Alemene, Akarini, Wahine, Satan or
Carbierra, whilst for the Summer Cup Clarion, Kallatina,
Charge, Plutus, Phillip and Woodlark appear to have
good chances. In rich December Stakes Beaulieu, Con-
diment or Dewey should be returned the winner. The
Carrington Stakes may fall to Moorefield, Burleigh or
Beaulieu, and Tatt’s Cup looks a good thing for Phillip
or his stable companion, Clarion.
* * * *
Westralia’s most important racing event —the
Perth Cup—seems likely to fall to one of the N.S.W. cast-
offs, of which a fair muster will appear at the post—
Brayleigh being apparently the pick of the bunch.
Tramp may be written a most unlucky horse, as
besides running into places in various other events, he
has filled second position in two Newcastle Tatt.’s Cups
and one Newcastle Cup -the winners being Old Clo,
Raven’s Plume, and Oban respectively. On December 3,
nothing in the paddock at Newcastle races looked better
than he, but Oban, a 100 to 3 chance, got away directly
the machine rose, and led from start to finish.
• * * *
A B.H. tote the other day (during the V.R.C.
meeting) paid £l3 2s. Bd. for 2s. 6d. The bookmakers’
limit for the same race was 10 to 1.
* * *
A bit of the sweep was not forthcoming in many
cases at Rosehill (Sj'd.) on Saturday, and what pro-
mised to be a record field of 70 or so in the Rosehill
Stakes dwindled down to 31. Betting was pretty brisk,
but those who wanted to.put a trifle on an outsider
wished for the totalisator. Betting with the “ books ’’ is
not what it used to be, arid it now takes a very small sum
to bring a horse’s price down. The writer was standing
near one of the biggest “books" waiting to put a trifle on
Lee Morna. He was forestalled by a man who put two
pounds on at 20 to 1. That couple of “ quid ’’ brought
the price down to 14 tol—and this from one of the biggest
books in Australia. Although Strathroy looked really a
good thing, 6 to 1 was a Short price to take in such a field,
but he pulled his backers through. Yet it looked none
too good till the post was passed, as Djin Djin came with
a phenomenal rush, and had she got the chance to make
her run a bit earlier would have about won, instead of
finishing half a length to the bad. Thoughts of Caul-
field arose when five of the field came down in a bunch
just before the horses entered the straight, but no great
damage was done. The Nursery Handicap was won by
the unbacked Oceanic, a filly by Erridsford out of New-
haven’s dam Oceana, trained by Martin Payten, a brother
of Tom of that ilk.
* • * *
Broken Hill t’other day saw the unusual sight
of the court-house turned into a Tattersall’s club. A
bookmaker doing business over the Melbourne Cup
wasn’t on deck when settling-day came round. He
was in Adelaide, and, as his clients didn’t hear from
him, a warrant was issued, and he was hauled back to the
Hill to account for some of the money invested with
him. Judge Gibson, as soon as the case was entered
into, wanted to know if any of the bettors who had
started the prosecution had demanded their money from
him. None of them had, for the very simple reason that
he was in Adelaide when the time came for “demanding,”
and when he returned he was in custody. However, the
point was deemed fatal; and on the book stating (through
his solicitor) that he had only gone to Crowland to bor-
row money to pay his debts, he was released on condition
that he paid-up. (It was also stated, rather irrelevantly,
that the bookie’s father was ill in bed.) His was the last
case on the list, and, as soon as the Judge left the bench,
all clients who demanded their “oof” were paid in the
court-room. But only the money they had invested. One
poor beggar who had backed H.vinettus and the Grafter,
£lOO to 2s. 6d. (and had spree’d a portion of his pros-
pective winnings on the night of the Cup) looked canni-
balistic on receiving back his half-dollar. Memo. : The
tote never has to go to Adelaide to borrow the money to
settle with, and its father is never ill in bed. That auto-
matic betting-machine is fatherless.
*' * * »
Tod Sloan, the ’Murkan scientific jockey, seems
likely to have imitators in conservative England. One
trainer already professes to have proved, by experiments,
that the Sloan system of riding “on the neck” takes
fully a stone “ off the back ” of the horse in a inile-and-a-
half race. Sloan, apparently, has improved upon the
monkeyish tactics of several previous experts—George
Fordham and Fred Archer in particular. Their ugly,
cramped style of riding was often said to lighten the
horse’s b uden, but, on the other hand, easy, straight-
backed jockeys, like Tom Cannon and Watts, rode with
almost equal success.

Of course, he came down in every subject, but Ins tmca-

nerve, for he is now a crack pigeon-shot.

“ Albert Macdonald of Orroroo,” an attractive
pamphlet issued by the Austral Cycle Agency, records

* * * *
Another Austral Wheel Race is over, but even
with the greatly -improved riders of to-day, all mounted
on the latest 1898-9 model bicycles, they have beeni unable
to equal the great ride of C. B. Kellow, who, in 1896, won
that classic race in the world’s record time of 4mm.
29sec. Kellow started from the 15yd. mark, and was
mounted on a “Bed Bird” of 1896 model. The manu-
facturers of this famous machine always contended tnat
their “ Red Birds” are two years ahead of all others, and
we think, from the above comparison, together with the
world’s competition mile record which is still held by VV.
Martin, this wheel seems to uphold their claims.*
Prevention of Seasickness. —Extraordi-
nary interest has been aroused by a large number
of letters appearing in the Evening News and
Stab testifying to the efficacy of the new remedy,
‘‘Seajoi.” The Mayor of Annandale, Mr. Allen
Taylor, writes that hitherto he was “ always sea-
sick,” but took “ Seajoi ” on a recent journey to
Port Macquarie, and “ was enabled to enjoy the
trip without suffering any of the previous discom-
fort.” Another interesting letter was from Mr.
Alex. M‘lntosh, of the North Coast S. Ship Co.,
Grafton. He wrote : “ I and Mr. Me—— each
took your preparation, and I have now the pleasure
of informing you that neither of us had the
slightest sympton of sickness on the voyage to the
Clarence.’'’ “Seajoi” is one of several valuable
remedies prepared and sold by Mr. Albert Sharp,
of 257 George-st., Sydney. (In bottles, 2s. bd.
and 4s. 6d. ; posted to any colony, 4d. and Bd.)

One of the first Democratic measures to be
thrown out by S. A. Legislative Council was that
for compulsory early closing of shops. Strangely
enough it was rejected by the vote of E. 1.
Smith, the wealthy brewer, now. on the Tory side,
who was first president of the Early Closing
Association years ago, and who made his first
political start when he got into S.A. Assembly
on the early-closing ticket.
During the month of last Sept, the New
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co. Ltd.
supplied the German warship Falke with 0000
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use on the
voyage to Samoa and Ayiia. During the vessel s
stay in our harbor 600 gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute to the
excellence of the local lager comes from the Ger-
man warship Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart
bottles of Svdney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk Lager daily.
Both ships’ crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney Lager.*

“C.F.” : Re pre-natal influence. In Sara-
wak, a young married woman who had been badly
scared by an orang-outang kept tame about .the
“ kampong,” bore a child covered with coarse red
hair to such an extent as to cause the real old man
to kill both the wild man of the woods and the
unfortunate woman. Tradition fails to account
for the child’s end in a credible manner, but there
is ample proof of its savage nature and bestial in-
clinations. The Dyaks laugh to scorn the pre-
natal explanation in which the whites believe.
Similar cases have occurred in China, and physio-
logists are not agreed as to cause. Again, know
of fox-terrier bitch which, having joined in wild-
pig hunt when pregnant, had a litter, still-born,
bar one with a distinctly porcine appearance,
including corkscrew tail.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Re W. J. Lyne’s jury-yarn. At an inquest
out-back, 11 were for a verdict of wilful murder,
and the other stuck out for manslaughter. They
were locked up for the night. It was bitterly cold,
and there was only room for three at the narrow
fire-place. One man tramped about in the back-
ground, and as his temperature fell his temper
rose, till he blurted out —“ And they call this
British justice—shutting a man up in a wretched
barn like this all night ! To with it! But
it’s not British justice : it’s that filthy, beastly
Jones.” (Jones, the recalcitrant juror, was
notorious as the dirtiest man between there and
Sydney.) Then the band played, and if two
able-bodied troopers had not been roused by the
row there would have been another inquest.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

As The Bulletin goes to press the latest
is that Neild—Majah Jawbone Neild—goes to
England for a year as N.S.W. Agent-General.
Neild never got anything in particular till he
began to judiciously totter in his allegiance to
Reid.
The hospitable Union S.S. Co. celebrated
by a very successful luncheon the arrival in Sydney
of its fine new steamer, Mokoia. Capt. Sinclair,
of the Tarawera and Monowai, who brought the
vessel out direct from Glasgow, speaks in the
highest terms of her comfort and capacity ; and
the visitors to the vessel unanimously commended
her as in many respects the finest yet placed in
running for the interprovincial trade.
The old Hereward, which has lain for the
best part of a year on the Maroubra beach, near
Sydney, perished last week as a solemn lesson on
the cussedness of inanimate objects. For months
and months tugs hauled at her in vain. Then one
night she slid off quite casually when there was no
tug about, and before one arrived a gale sprung
up, and ended the long suspense by bumping her
to death on the beach, and came near to drowning
everybody on board as well.
A new kind of apparatus for freezing ice-
cream is being introduced by Messrs. Brandt
Bros., of 236 Pitt-st., Sydney. It is a cylinder
made of tinned iron, japanned. Inside is a per-
forated zinc gallery which carries a small quantity
of ice ; in the bottom, salt and water is placed in
given quantities. In the centre of this is another
cyclinder, which is the ice-cream holder, the con-
tents being frozen in a few minutes, entirely doing
away with the turning of a handle. Moreover,
this should find favor, for it will also do for freez-
ing jellies, butter, aerated waters, &c. By this
means the cost is reduced to a minimum.*
Details received by mail from Wellington
regarding the burning of the Roman Catholic
Cathedral state that the large bronze statue of the
Virgin fell some 60 or 70 feet but was not injured.
Some years ago, when repairs were being made to
the building, the local paper sent one of its young
lions to write a par re the alterations and improve-
ments, &c. The paragraph duly appeared, and
after detailing various architectural features con-
cluded with the statement that “ over the Eastern
doorway there is a finely executed statue of a
woman with a child in her arms.”
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S.W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8,5/2. Beware of imitations. Only
address: Senior, chemist, 246 George-st., Sydney.*
Hancock, M.L.A., got a nasty slap in the
eye from murderer Archer, whom he sought to
save from the gallows. Agitating for a reprieve
in the Assembly, Hancock referred to the fact
that Archer had gone into the country and drunk
large quantities of strong tea—which was worse
than whisky—and, in consequence of that, if
nothing else, his mind was probably affected.
The reprieve movement collapsed with the an
nouncemerit of Archer’s confession, and on the
morning of his hanging the convict walked to his
death without breakfast, but after swallowing a
quart of strong, milkless tea.

On another page is an advertisement relat-
ing to photographic supplies by the well-known
firm Harrington and Co., Ltd., whose goods being
so well-known that it is only necessary to direct
attention to the announcement. Amongst photo-
graphers the name of H. and C. has become a
household word. The stock of cameras, apparatus
and chemicals is most extensive, and anyone can
be suited with an outfit in accordance with their
means, from a few shillings up to fifty pounds.
The firm deserve the support of all photographers,
they having done so much to popularise the art by
supplying up-to-date appliances at popular prices
and through the medium of the “ Australian
Photographic Journal,” a publication which has a
wide circulation in all the colonies.*
A Victorian grand-jury has found a true
bill against the detective who hired, with the
‘Crown’s money, some alleged abortionists to per-
form an illegal operation on a widow, also hired for
the purpose with the Crown’s money, with a view
to trapping them in their illegal work. It has
also found a true bill against the widow, and the
pair will presumably be tried for conspiracy. But
what about finding a true bill against the Crown,
which furnished the cash, and apparently initiated
the wretched idea? The Crown is, technically,
Queen Victoria, and, unless the Melbourne grand-
jury commits Queen Victoria for trial on a charge
of conspiring to procure abortion the proceedings
won’t look complete, anyhow.

By the death of Baden Powell, the vapid
.bores who profess to know all about everything in
Australia are less by one. The taste of Victoria
he got as Secretary to Gov. Bowen never left his
mouth ; to whisper “ Australia ” was to start him
on on a tour of dogmatically-expressed opinions
bn its affairs, past and present. He was nimble,
not active ; smarty, not smart. He had the names
or Leichhardt and a score of other explorers by
rote, and threw them about so with his tongue
that many regarded him as himself a distinguished
explorer. He was held in similar estimation as an
Australian historian. He was a blend of Hogan
and Henniker Heaton without the justification of
either for his cocksure assurance. He was a mode-
rately good photographer and water-color painter,
and, had he stuck to these branches, might have
done something worth notice. But to gas about
Australia was his ruling passion.

“Jep” on the vegetable-choV contro-
versy :
The Chow is encouraged in country districts to grow
and sell vegetables by the dirty pride of the cocky, which
prevents him (the cocky) from growing greens even for
*V S ov^ n use / Tinkering- about a cabbage-garden is
thought undignified and lowering to one vvho aspires to
the name of “farmer” or “agriculJurist,” and scorns
that of common “ gardener.” Therefore, the cooky grows
not the greens. If—as is often the case—he cannot afford
to buy from the vegeiable-John, he goes without, and
subsequent indigestion is relieved or increased by un-
limited consumption of pills. I don’t think you’ll find
one cocky vvho doesn’t pin his faith to some piil or other
as a cure-all. It’s a characteristic. Even the plain spud is
grown feebly and under protest, as a vegetable which
tends to lower the “ farmer’s ” dignity. Cocky selects
worst patch of paddock, and in spare moments roots it
up with plough, harrows it a bit, and ploughs-in seed.
He then hands the success or failure of the crop over to
Providence. Friend of mine was on a Vic. mountain
selection where vegetables were unknown. When a hag
of potatoes did happen to meander there the “ Missus ”
was within an ace of throwing’em into the “ crick” as
too troublesome to cook, but my friend interfered, pro-
mised to cook them himself, and they lasted till he left.

Perhaps you are one of those people, and are not
aware of it. You have something the matter
with you, but, not having any experience, treat it
lightly, while it gets worse and worse, untimately
destroying you.
The wise man will at once put himself in the
witness-box, and ask himself the following ques-
tions, as will the wise woman
Do I sleep badly ; do I wake up in the morning,
even when I appear to have slept all night, fagged
and jaded, with lack-lustre eyes and an inclination
only to go to bed again. Do I eat badly, or, when
Ido eat “well,” does my food agree with and
nourish me ? Do Igo about my work dejectedly—
do I suffer from headaches, pains in either the
limbs or body, and indulge in depressing retros-
pections ? Do I, in short, suffer from a loss of
mental and physical elasticity—that exultant
appreciation of life which all healthy people must
have ?
If to even one of these questions the answer be
“Yes,” then that man realises that he wants a
[ tonic —something to give him an appetite, to
steady his nerves, and to make him strong and
active in the daytime, and afford him calm and
refreshing sleep at night.

In One Skin!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ How do, Hudson ? Not looking quite the thing,
I fancy ! ”
Dr. Parrott pulled up, and held out his hand to
the curate—running his professional eye with ap-
proval, at the same time, over that young man’s
bodily presentments; ample trunk, capacious
chest, and the long, powerful arms which, in con-
junction with a certain inadequacy of lower limb,
mark a distinct physical reversion towards a
simian—or, at all events, an arboreal—ancestry.
The shortness of neck also, and the somewhat acute
facial angle continued the analogy; but the Rev.
Francis Hudson’s jaw was not unduly prominent,
and his alert grey eyes were separated by at least
the average distance.
“ Oh, nothing much ! ” Parrott continued in
answer to the curate’s unspoken question. “ Go-
ing my way ? Jump in then, and we’ll talk ! ”
“What is it you note amiss?” The big cleric
looked down at the terrier-faced little doctor, as
they rattled off together. “ I feel pretty fit! ”
“ In body—yes ! Machine’s all right, I don’t
doubt; but there’s something amiss with the
engineer ! ”
“Engineer? Oh ! the mind, you mean?”
“Ay ! You’re looking badly worried ! ”
“Look here, Parrott,” the curate answered,
after the long pause of a man who is making up
his mind —“ you have a reputation for candor.
Be candid with me. Should you call me a good
man ? Good, I mean, technically ; ‘ ornament of
my profession,’ and so on.” The curate’s laugh
had an edge to it.
“ Hum ! You’re not compliment-fishing, I
know. Yes, I should call you a good parson, de-
cidedly—as distinguished from a goody one.
What’s your own opinion ? ”
“My own opinion—l will be candid in turn—
is Dr. Parrott ” —Hudson interrupted him-
self with a snap - “ I am a hypocrite—a miserable,
cowardly hypocrite ! ”
“ Pooh ! it’s liver, after all, then ? ”
“A cowardly hypocrite,” Hudson repeated,
looking straight before him ; “inasmuch as I—a
minister of the gospel, a preacher of the doctrines
of Christ—am, in secret, a gross and, I fear, hope-
less offender against the most vital of His teach-
ings ! ”
The man’s tone carried conviction. “ Either
his head’s wrong,” thought the surgeon, “or he
really has a secret weakness for —which of the
three ? Hudson,” he added, aloud, “ I see there’s
more in this than hypersensitive self-torturing.
Tell me just as much or as little as you please,
when I come back. I make a call here. Take the
reins, will you.”
In a few minutes the doctor came back, accom-
panied to the gate by his patient’s husband—an
immensely large, hard, red-headed Celt, with a
good-humored blue eye that belied his scarred
cheek and fist-flattened nose.
“ Tough customer, that, I should think ! ” The
curate’s eye had an interest that surprised the
doctor. “Out of your line, all that ! ’’ the latter
said, as he scrambled in again. “ That’s Costigan,
the quarryman. As harmless as he’s big ; until he
has got outside half-a-foot of whisky—then it’s
‘ stand clear ! ’ For chaps of my size, at all
events ; a fellow of your scantling might have a
show. Now, about this trouble of yours. Can I
help you in any way ? ”
“ I don’t see how you can. Unless, indeed, a
systematic lowering of the system might affect the
position.”
“Ah!” The doctor winked his off-eye. “That
eliminates gambling ! ” he said to himself. “ And,
of the other two, it can hardly be liquor. So, I
suppose it’s the old, old story ! Yes ; that’s it !
The man is simply overflowing with masculinity ! ”
“I see,” Hudson said, presently, “that you
pretty well guess my secret. But you mustn’t
think I haven’t struggled— ”
“ I know you have, Hudson ! You’re not the
sort to give the Old Adam his uninterrupted fling.
You have the strength of your weakness; and
that is. what distinguishes him who is merely a
man, in this respect, from him who is also a
beast! In a word—l am no moralist ! —the fault,
if fault it is, appears to me to be so essentially
masculine a characteristic— ”
“ Ah | ” Hudson cooled a little in his manner.
“That is the voice of the Tempter! We must
crucify—but noprofessional commonplaces for you,
I suppose. I get down here. Thanks ! ”
“ Oh, there’s no fear for him ! ” the doctor told
himself as he drove on. “ He’ll keep himself in
hand all right till .he gets married, and then, I
suppose, like all the rest of the holy men, he’ll
take good care not to neglect the * increase and
multiply ’ injunction.”
* • • *
Timotheus Costigan, Esquire—not yet sur-
charged with his national kill-or-cure—made
Saturday-night progress adown Coorabinda’s one
considerable street, and found no man to say him
nay ; for Tim’s most dangerous condition was half-
cargo. Loaded-up, his offensive powers were
seriously hampered ; the means defeated the end.
“ Divil shoot me ! ” muttered Tim as he rounded
the corner for home, “if I see Buckley’s chance
of a shindy to-noight. Be me sowl, man ! ” —with
a sudden ascension of voice—“ d’ye think it’s
belangin’ t’ yerself the road is ? ’’—and Tim
diverted his course to shove rudely against a per-
son coming in the opposite direction. Which
person, stepping back for room, promptly pro-
jected an extremely “ dirty left ” into Mr. Costi-
gan’s eye, swung him an equally unladylike
“right” under the ear, and laid that astounded
warrior most unequivocally out. The moment he
arose, proceedings recommenced ; and in about
ten minutes’ time the stranger faded away into
the darkness, leaving the badly-bashed Costigan
in rapt contemplation of the stars from the broad
of his back.
* * V *
“ A bit of an artist, this was, Tim.” Dr. Par-
rott was engaged, next morning, upon some neces-
sary repairs to the person of the vanquished..
“ He must have upper-cut you in tip-top style.”
The doctor—like so many other harmless little
men—was something of a virtuoso in pugilism.
“ And you’ve no idea who he was,'you say ? ”
“ Bad luck to the bit! A stiff-built chap, he
was, an’ middlin’ tall; but he niver opened his
mouth, an’ ’twas too darruk to make out the fay-
tures of ’m.”
“ Devilish odd ! Looks almost as if he’d come
to Coorabinda on purpose to take you down a
peg.” But just then the doctor received a note
which gave him weightier cause of wonderment.
“ As I have already told you so much,” wrote
the curate, “ I will now confide to you the real
reason of the resignation which you will hear of
publicly in due course. It is, simply, that —
struggle as I may against the degrading pro-
Density I hinted of to you, the other day—some
accursed strain of the beast in my nature is too
strnnw for mp Last I nave way again—
d'Sd my Christian profeS*o» through the
Sol my brutish personality 1 As a medical
man vou mav possibly find more excuse for me on
the score of tlian l dare make for myself,
But a hypocrite at all events, I will no longer
r> ~, p
*“ Poor chap!” The little doctor carefully ap-
plied a match to the note. “ There’s better stuff
in him, after all, than in half the smug young
snufflers who think iPs virtu© that’s th© matter
with them when it’s only anaemia ! ”
* • * *
It was nearly a year later that, amongst the
crowd pouring into a Sydney “ athletic hall,” Dr.
Parrott caught sight of the ex-curate.
“The world, the flesh, and the devil ! ” said the
little man to himself. “He’s going in for the
whole contract, apparently ! Hi !—Hudson ! ”
—but Hudson had disappeared into the adytum of
the establishment. Knows some of the pugs, I
suppose ! ” surmised the doctor, as he seated him-
self amongst the spectators. Daresay 111 catch
on to him after the fun s over.
The event of the evening-afinishbetweentwo
well-known punchers—was to be preceded by a
10-round go, gentlemen, between your old
acquaintance, Foxy Blogg, and a clever gentle-
man-hamateur ! And when the pair came on,
the doctors jaw dropped, for tlie gentleman-
amateur was the ex-Rev I rancis Hudson !
*<rri? reat S f ot^ ; m £ tter ? d A 1 *® j» '
The young fool. A chopping-block
And, for the first couple of rounds, it certain y
looked a good deal that way ; for the professional
—though slightly inferior in „ a b K
seemed to get home pretty much as he liked. But
in the third round exchanges were pretty even;
and then, suddenly, in the fourth, Hudson came
JriTm to «»* JS32h5 S bom
fighter—still with a lot to learn in skill—but
physically and mentally of the stamp that no
training can ever manufacture-the stamp that
revels hardly less in the receipt of punishment
than in its infliction. The professional put forth
all his resources as a boxer; but as a fighter he
was hopelessly outclassed by the younger man,
and went to sleep over a terrifio right-hander on
the jaw in the sixth round.
• • • •
“My mistake was a very natural one,” the doc-
tor said, as he and Hudson strolled away from the
place together. “Nobody could have supposed
that expressions of such bitter self-contempt
referred merely to a predilection for fisticuffs.”
“ It’s more than that! ” Hudson said, gloomily,
“It’s bloodthirst!”
“Well call it what you like. What did you
mean, by the way, when you said in that letter
that you had ‘given way’ the night before, and
soon? That referred to some specific act, didn’t
Hudson smiled for the first time. “ I should
think that big Irish quarryman found it decidedly
specific. What was his name ? ”
“Whee-eep!” whistled the doctor. “ Costi-
gan eh! How did you manage to fall foul of
him ? He took a deal of mending next morning.”
“I put myself purposely in his way, so as to
sret some of my steam worked-off. Steam’s the
only thing I can compare it with. When I’ve
been a certain time without a fight of some sort,
the thing simply takes possession of me—l must
either have a scrimmage or burst. But, though
the one part of me absolutely can’t help my-
self, the other part of me still considers me to*
great a brute to wish to continue any of my old-
time acquaintances. Good night! ”
“ At first,” muttered the doctor, staring after
him, “I was inclined to think it a simple case of
atavism—the man’s physique is obviously in-
herited from a fighting breed ; but, when I take
his extraordinary self-reproachfulness into account
I begin to think there’s something, after all, in
the notion that one skin may hold two men.”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Visit to Australia talked of for Duke of
Connaught, who, for various reasons, would be
more acceptable to the less flunkeyish part of the
community than either Tummy, or Alfredinburgh,
or Son York. Connaught is a soldier who has
seen some active service, is less virtually Deutsche
than any of the others, and is credited with much
plain sense and dislike of “ frill.”
The N.S. W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
What has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.*
There was grievous uproar in a Westralian
hash-factory recently. The culinary department
was run by a good-looking girl upon whom the
youngest boarder was badly mashed. One even-
ing she told him she had “got notice” because
the grass-widow of another fellow had undercut
her wages. Said grass-widow was old, straight
up and down, with a face like a rock-oyster. The
youngest boarder was in despair, and laid a plan to
reinstate his girl. The new cook started opera-
tions Sunday morning moulding the duff for
dinner. When it appeared in a dish of sauce the
youngest boarder turned pale,. and when the
carver tried to open it out with the carving-knife
and failed the youngest boarder turned guilty red.
The 38 boarders suggested maul and wedges,
crosscuts, dynamite, &c , and the athletic boarder,
standing on the chair to get a better leverage,
tried to work the bread-knife into it. The wet
duff only slithered off the dish and up the table,
and the boarders, being afraid of their best
clothes, dragged cloth and everything else with
them in a frantic endeavor to get out of the road.
The athletic carver fell into the dish, which he
broke, and the duff from, the overturned table
rolled heavily across the floor and thumped solid
into the wall. Most of the boarders had fallen
over something or other in the lOsec. scuffle, and
more damage was done than the cheap cook could
pay for in six months. Supper was engineered
that night by the reinstated girl. And in the
dead of night she and her fiance, the youngest
boarder, emptied the bag of flour over the back
fence. It was mostly plaster-of-paris.
Arnott’s Living Pictures.

The Two Souls.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two souls went sweeping wide in space, two
spirits outward bound ;
A hissing comet raced them ’tween the planets
wheeling round :
They felt the flame of liquid suns, through voids
unmeasured fell,
Where.dead worlds turned and no light burned,
and one said this was Hell.
The one pale soul, a haunted thing, was gripped
with mortal awe,
And, terror-cold, it shuddered at the God Al-
mighty’s law.
But one soul rode in bluff content above the
shining bars,
And seemed to jeer each spinning sphere, and
beard the hoary stars.
Joe Paul had been a wayward man, a man of
simple worth,
Who called not on the name of God when walking
on the earth,
Nor made loud prayer in kirk or cot. The good
deeds done abroad
For men were done, and never one to glorify the
Lord.
And now flung out, a frenzied spook in deeps
eternal spun,
He knew his fault, and wept for that his earthly
course was run.
One only hope he had to join the ever blessed
host
By stealing in a shade of sin behind the goodly
ghost.
For Nahum Benn had mighty been in piety and
zeal.
He smote the child of wrath and ground the Devil
’neath his heel.
He laid a hand across the weak, and bent them to
the rod,
And, high and free, translated he the word and
will of God.
His voice was loud, his brow was grim, his body
tall and broad,
His eye was lit with flaming fire, his tongue was
as a sword.
He called the people from their joy, his gospel
fidtee to tell—
If one denied in holy pride he flicked him into
hell !
He knew with hot conviction, and each message
was a blow :
‘"The Lord says this ! ” “This is His wish!”
“The God will have it so / ”
They said Jehovah spoke by him, the dull-lifed
toiling horde.
“In truth,” said they, “a prophet! Yea, a gaffer
for the Lord!”
And now these twain from flesh absolved, with
earthy longing spent,
All through the starry vortex whirled, up to the
judgment went.
Between the gates St. Peter stood, the streaming
ghosts to scan.
The hand of Paul he took ’fore all—they met as
man and'man.
“Come in,” he cried, “you’re welcome, mate!”
and drew him up, and then
The pale ghost hid his face and said : “Thatxs
tlie soul of Benn.”
St. Peter paused, and backward looked with eye
and brow austere:
“ We know him not, and this I wot, we brook no
strangers here.”
St. Peter passed the otlier in, a soul serene, elate,
But left the ghost of Nahum Benn low whimper-
ing at the gate.
Then Paul knew how the love of God had saved a
soul in ten ;
The other nine won grace divine by simple love
of men !
EDWARD DYSON.
A Circus on our Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dave had been to town and came home full of
circus. He sat on the ground beside the tubs
while mother and Sal were washing, and raved
about the riding and the tumbling he had seen.
He talked enthusiastically to Joe about it every
day for three weeks. Dave rose very high in Joe’s
estimation.
* * • *
Raining. All of us inside. Sal on the sofa
playing the concertina; Dad squatting on the
edge of a flat stone at the corner of the fireplace ;
Dave on another opposite; both gazing into the
fire, which was almost out, listening intently to
the music; the dog, dripping wet, coiled at their
feet, shivering; mother sitting dreamily at the
table, her palm pressed against her cheek, also
enjoying the music.
Sal played on until the concertina broke. Then
there was a silence.
For a while Dave played with a piece of char-
coal. At last he spoke.
“ Well,” he said, looking at Dad, “ what about
this circus ? ”
Dad chuckled.
“ But what d’ y’ think? ”
“Well ” (Dad paused) “ yes ” (chuckled again)
—“ very well.”
“A circus /” Sal put in—“a ■pretty circus
yous A. have ! ”
Dave fired up.
“ You go and ride the red heifer, strad-legs,
same as y’ did yesterday,” he snarled, “ an’ let all
th’ country see y’.”
Sal blushed.
Then to Dad :
“ I’m certain, with Paddy Maloney in it, we
could do it right enough, and make it pay, too.”
“Very well, then,” said Dad, “very well.
There’s th’ tarpaulin’ there, and plenty bales and
old bags whenever you’re ready.”
Dave was delighted, and he and Dad and Joe
ran out to see where the tent could be pitched, and
ran in again wetter than the dog.
♦ * • *
One day a circus-tent went up in our yard. It
attracted a lot of notice. Two of the Johnsons
and old Anderson and others rode in on draught-
horses and inspected it. And Smith’s spring-cart
horse, that used to be driven by every day,
stopped in the middle of the lane and stared at it;
and when Smith stood up and belted him with
the double of the reins lie. bolted and upset the
cart over a stump.’ It wasn’t a very white tent.
. It was made of bags and green bushes, and Dad
and Dave and Paddy Maloney were two days put-
ting it up.
We all assisted in the preparations for the circus.
Dad built seats out of forked sticks and slabs, and
Joe gathered jam-tins which mother filled with fat
and moleskin wicks to light up with.
Everyone in the district knew about our circus,
and longed for the opening night. It came. A
large fire near the slip-rails, shining across the lane
and lighting up a corner of the wheat-paddock,
showed the way in.
Dad stood at the door to take the money. The
Andersons —eleven of them—first arrived. They
didn’t walk straight in. They hung about fora
while. Then Anderson sidled up to Dad and
talked into his ear. “Oh! that’s all right,” Dad
said, and passed them all in without taking any
money.
Next came the Maloneys, and as Paddy be-
longed to the circus they also walked in without
paying, and secured front seats.
Then Jim Brown and Sam Holmes, and Walter
Nutt, and Steve Burton, and eight others strolled
along. Dad owed all of them money for binding,
which they happened to remember. “In yous
go,” Dad said, and in the lot went. The tent
filled quickly, and the crowd awaited the opening
act.
Paddy Maloney came forward with his hair
oiled and combed, and rang the cow-bell.
Dave, bare-footed and bare-headed, in snow-
white moles and red shirt, entered standing
majestically upon old Ned’s back. He got a great
reception. But Ned was tired and refused to
canter. He jogged lazily round the ring. Dave
shouted at him and rocked about. He was very
unsteady. Paddy Maloney flogged Ned with the
leg-rope. But Ned had been flogged often before.
He got slower and slower. Suddenly, he stood
and cocked his tail, and, to prevent; himself fall-
ing, Dave jumped off. Then the audience yelled
while Dave dragged Ned into the dressing-room
and punched him on the nose.
Paddy Maloney made a speech. He said:
“ Well, the next item on the programme ’ll knock
y’ bandy. Keep quiet, you fellows, now, an’ y’ll
see somethin’.”
They saw Joe. He stepped backwards into the
ring, pulling at a string. There was something on
the string. “Come on ! ” Joe said, tugging. The
“something” wouldn’t come. “Chuck ’im in,”
Joe called out. Then the pet kangaroo was
heaved in through the doorway, and fell on its
head and raised the dust. A great many tigly
dogs rushed for it savagely. The kangaroo
jumped up and bounded round the ring. The
dogs pursued him noisily. “ Gerrout /” Joe
shouted, and the crowd stood up and became
very enthusiastic. The dogs caught the kangaroo,
and were dragging him to earth when Dad rushed
in and kicked them.in twos to the top of the tent.
Then, while Johnson expostulated with Dad for
laming his hrindle slut, the kangaroo dived
through a hole in the tent and rushed into the
house and into the bedroom, and sprang on the
bed among a lot of babies and women’s hats.
When the commotion subsided Paddy Maloney
rang the cow-bell again, and Dave and “ Podgy,”
the pet sheep, rode out on Nugget. Podgy sat
with hind-legs astride the horse and his head lean-
ing back against Dave’s chest. Dave (standing
up) bent over him with a pair of shears in his hand.
He was to shear Podgy as the horse cantered
round.
Paddy Maloney touched Nugget with the whip,
and off lie went —“ rump-ti-dee, dump-ti-dee.”
Dave rolled about a lot the first time round, but
soon got his equilibrium. He brandished the
shears and plunged the points of them into Podgy’s
belly-wool—also into Podgy’s skin. “ Bur -ur-r t ”
Podgy blurted and ’struggled violently. Dave
began to topple about. He dropped the shears.
The audience guffawed. Then Dave jumped ; but
Podgy’s horns got caught in his trousers and made
trouble. Dave hung on one side the horse and the
sheep dangled on the other. Dave sang out, so
did Podgy. And the horse stopped and snorted,
then swung furiously round and round until five
or six pair of hands seized his Load and held him.
Dave didn’t repeat the act. He ran away hold-
ing his trousers together.
It was a very successful circus. Everyone en-
joyed it and wished to see it again—everyone but
the Maloneys. • They said it was a swindle, and
ran Dad down because he didn’t divide with
Paddy the 3s. Gd. he took at the door.

STEELE RUDD.
Advance Australia ! Two Melb. small
boys, aged respectively 13 and 14, have been
arrested for forgery, and also found guilty of a series
of well-conceived and smartly-executed thefts
from city warehouses. In one instance the
youngsters, having stolen the keys of a tobacco-
factory, drew upon the stock at leisure, but broke
open a window and made nail-marks on the. sill,
and thus befooled “ the law ” into the belief that
it was a common burglary. The province will lose
two good policemen' if these youths take to
politics.
Beautiful sentiments from actual address
of N.S. W. country candidate for aldermanship :
I am in favor of a man having full local option of taking
his glass of beer at all hours and at all times, whether he
is able to pay for it or not. I am in favor of settling all
disputes, by strikes—never bandy words; strike, your
opponent and strike hard—with a stick. lam in favor of
extending the franchise to every woman and child in the
colony. I believe in rain-making by dynamite in our dry
town, and would like to be employed at the job. In fact,
I am a grand man. I feel convinced that I will top the
poll.
He didn’t!
In an up-mountain train several passengers
were pestered by a young fellow with continual
remarks prefaced with “Suppose.” Glancing out
of the window he noticed the fearful fall one
could have, and enquired generally: “If the
train fell over, what do you suppose would happen
tous?” Then an old Yankee drummer spake ;
“ Look a-here, young man, ‘ s’posin’ ’ you was out
on the prair-rie, and ‘s’posin” you met a fierce
kan-ga-roo ; and ‘s’posin” she jammed you in her
pouch. Now, ‘.s’posin’ ’ your head stuck oiit, .and
‘s’posin” that ’roo was chased by a pack of
dingoes, what do you ‘ s’pose ’ you’d do? D’ you
‘s’pose ’ you’d slink in, d’ you ‘ s’pose ’ you’d siink
out, or d’ you ‘ s’pose ’ you’d bite that ’roo’s tail to
make her go faster?” Then the youth ceased
supposing.

Death Stared Him.
HIS COMPANION-SEIZED BY A SHARK
-THE SHOCK, COUPLED WITH AN
ACCIDENT, LEFT RICHARDC. BROWN
A WHECK—DOCTORS FAIL. BUT DR.
MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS SCORE
A VICTORY.
Having heard of an experience which few are
called upon to undergo, a reporter was despatched
to learn the true facts of the case, and on being
interviewed, Mr. Richard C. Brown, a store-
keeper at No. 7 Burton-street, North Sydney, re-
lated the following particulars: “One stormy
night, whilst employed upon the Government
steamer Fitzroy, I was ordered to form one of the
crew called out to communicate with the sand-
pump Doris. Upon reaching the bar our boat
capsized, and Martin Gunner and myself
attempted to swim for shore. Gunner disap-
peared—seized by a shark. My blood ran cold.
I turned to get back to the boat, when Captain
Grant cried, ‘ Brown, for God’s sake try to come
back.’ After a desperate struggle and sinking
twice I reached it, though I was as one dead. The
captain then swam ashore, and we were eventually
rescued by the Trial Bay convict lifeboat. That
night at Seal Rocks Dr. Kelly sat at my bedside
trying to ease my terrible pain. James Anderson,
who accompanied me to Sydney Hospital, knows
what I suffered ; but after four weeks I started to
improve, when I was ordered to join the Rhoa at
Cape Hawke. From there I was transferred to
Manning River, where I met with an accident
while heaving one of the doors at the depository
ground, one of the handles flying up and hitting
me in the chest. I was carried bleeding to the
Taree Hospital, and was in a serious state for
many weeks. Doctors informed me my chances
of recovery were doubtful, and the Railway
Medical Officer certified I was unfit for duty. I
was offered one hundred pounds compensation by
the Government, which I received. I was a
physical wreck, when a relation asked me to try
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, as they had cured
him. I purchased some from a chemist close by.
At this time 1 was unable to walk a hundred
yards without the aid of two sticks. My liver felt
as if a knife-blade was sticking in it, my back was
dead, and I continually vomited blood. I had
only used three bottles of the pills when the pain
grew less and I ceased to vomit blood. I could
feel myself growing stronger every day, and the
chills disappeared. My friends saw the change,
and knew that Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
wrought it, for I used no other remedy. 1 con-
tinued their use a few weeks, and now I can
truthfully say that I am entirely.cured, and never
felt better in my life.” Here Mr. Brown opened
his shirt, saying, “ All that remains of my suffer-
ings are these old scars. There are hundreds
know my case and miraculous cure.”

Oh, the Multitude Who Say It!
Speaking of another world than this, a certain
Book, from which so many draw comfort in times
of trouble, says: “ The inhabitant never saith., 1
am sick.” But in this world the air is full of the
weary, wailing cry, “I am sick,” “I suffer so,”
“ Can nothing be done to rid me of pain ? ”
Here is another case of it another out of a
number beyond all counting or imagining. Yet
this one got well. Do you want to hear about it ?
or do you know of any pain-racked mortal who
might like to hear about it? Then read, either
for yourself or for someone else. We give the facts
exactly as they were given to us.
“ In the early part of 1889 I began to suffer from
illness. I had at first a bad taste in my mouth,
and belched up a sour, gaseous fluid. My appetite
was poor, and, after eating, I had a burning pain
at my chest, and palpitation of the heart. I had
also a gnawing, grinding sensation at the pit of the
stomach, with a feeling of sinking, as though I
were being bodily lowered into a pit. My food
never seemed to settle, but repeated, and I had to
spit it out; this going on until my stomach was
empty. . ,
“ I lost a deal of sleep at night. I got very weak
and thin, losing over a stone in weight; I was so
feeble that I could hardly drag myself along. As
time went on I grew irritable and nervous, and
took no interest or pleasure in anything.
“ Sometimes better and at other times worse, I
continued in this way until August of last year
(1893), when I had to give up my employment as
collier at the Waterloo Main Colliery, where I
had been at work for seven years. At this time I
had got so bad that I sat in my chair from morn-
ing till night, too weak and prostrated to go about.
When I did venture out I dare not go alone for
fear I should fall down in the street.
“ At times a strange feeling came over me, as if
my heart had stopped beating ; and on one of
these occasions I felt as if I were dying, and said
to my wife, ‘ Lass , it’s all up with me.’
“ During my long illness I consulted several
doctors, and they all said my heart was sound,
and that the trouble was all from my stomach and
liver. They gave me medicines and recommended
a change of air. I therefore went to Blackpool,
Scarborough, and Whitby, but. was no better either
from the change or from the doctor’s physic.
“ One day a book was left at my house telling
about Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and I
thought it might possibly help me. So I got a
bottle from Mr. Webster, chemist, Green Road,
and when I had finished that bottle I found relief,
for my food no longer repeated and the pain at
my heart was easier. I kept on with the medicine
arid soon my appetite returned, and I Gould eat
without feeling any pain or distress. Gradually I
improved! getting stronger and stronger every
day. I can now eat anything, and do my work
with pleasure. I feel quite a new man, and con-
sider that Mother Seigei’s Syrup lias saved my
life. Yours truly, (Signed) George Hopton, 25
Bayswater Terrace, Ftoundhay Road, Leeds,
February Bth, 1891. ”
No words of ours c»n add an ounce to the
weight of this plain and manly letter. From it,
as from a hilltop, we can see the sad picture of
those four years of misery. The discouraged and
suffering husband, the patient, burden-bearing,
helpful wife. Heaven be praised that it all ended
as it did —in renewed health —instead of as it
night have ended. The disease was that insatiate
fiend and monster —indigestion and dyspepsia;
cunning as a fox, destructive as a man-eating
tiger. In this case, as in so many others, Mother
Seigel’s Syrup demonstrated that power which has
made it celebrated all over the world—in palace
and in hut, wherever the inhabitant saith, I am
tick. If you are one of these, try it now. If you
are healthy and hearty, thank God for that, and
pass the news of George Hopton’s illness and re-
covery on to any who need to hear it.

TESTIMONIAL.
Adarostown, September 11th, 1896.
BONNINGTON & CO., Manufacturers of Irish Moss, &c.,
179 Harris Street, Sydney.
Dear Sirs,— Being a sutlerer for years from fibroid phthisis and bronchitis I de-
sire to add my testimony in favor of your Irish Moss. As a soother of irritating
cough, with much expectoration, I have found it to be the most effectual of anything X
I have tried. It loosens the mucus, which, therefore, comes more readily away and
the cough is thereby considerably pacified. ’
I remain, Yours respectfully,
THOMAS STOKES.
